 <s n="628"/>There were plenty of bucklers, too, and empty wagons which they could carry off.
 <s n="629"/>They used all these to boil the meat which they ate that day.
</section>


<section n="2" chapter="2.1.7">
 <s n="630"/>It was already about full-market time when messengers came from the king, heralds of the country, and one Hellene among them, Phalinos, who happened to be with Tissaphernes and highly respected;
 <s n="631"/>for he claimed to be expert in tactics and fighting in arms.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.1.8">
 <s n="632"/>These came and summoned the captains, and said, “The king having killed Cyros and won the victory, commands the Hellenes to lay down their arms, and find at the king’s door what good they can.”
</section>

<section n="6" chapter="2.1.9">
 <s n="633"/>Such was the king’s message.
 <s n="634"/>The Hellenes were very angry, but Clearchos only said, “It is not for victors to lay down their arms.”
 <s n="635"/>Then to the other captains, “You, my fellow-captains, answer these gentlemen as you think fairest and best; 
 <s n="636"/> I will be here in a moment,”
 <s n="637"/>for a servant had just called him to see the sacred parts which had been taken out from a victim 
 <s n="638"/> which was being sacrificed.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.1.10">
 <s n="639"/>Then Cleanor the Arcadian, eldest of them, said they would die sooner than lay down their arms;
 <s n="640"/>and Proxenos the Theban said, “I wonder, Phalinos, whether the king speaks as conqueror or asks our arms as a friendly gift.
 <s n="641"/>If as conqueror, why does not he come and take them?
 <s n="642"/>If he wants us to yield them willingly, let him tell us what the soldiers will have left, if they make him a present of these?”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.1.11">
 <s n="643"/>Phalinos answered, 
 <s n="644"/> “The king considers he is victor, since he has killed Cyros.
 <s n="645"/>For who is there who will challenge him for his empire?
 <s n="646"/>He considers that you also are his, because he holds you in the midst of his country, surrounded by impassable rivers, and he can bring against you such a multitude of men as you could not kill if you had the chance.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.1.12">
 <s n="647"/>Theopompos the Athenian followed and said,
 <s n="648"/> “My dear Phalinos, we have nothing good here, as you see, except arms and courage.
 <s n="649"/>While we keep our arms, we think we can use our courage; 
 <s n="650"/> but if we give them up, we shall lose our lives too.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.1.13">
 <s n="651"/>Hearing this, Phalinos laughed, and said, 
  <s n="652"/> “Why, you are quite a philosopher, young man, you speak neatly;
 <s n="653"/>but let me tell you that you are foolish if you think your courage can overcome the king’s power. The king’s strength is too great for your bravery to prevail.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.1.14">
 <s n="654"/>A few others, it is reported, showed a little weakness, and said they had been faithful to Cyros, and the king might find them valuable if he liked to be their friend;
 <s n="655"/>he might use them for a campaign into Egypt or something else, and they would go.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.1.15">
 <s n="656"/>At this moment Clearchos returned and asked whether they had given their answer.
 <s n="657"/> Phalinos broke in, and said, 
 <s n="658"/> “These people say all sorts of things, Clearchos;
 
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.1.16">
<s n="659"/>tell us what you say.”
 <s n="660"/>He answered, 
 <s n="661"/> “I was glad to see you, Phalinos, and so were the others, I think;
 <s n="662"/>you are a Hellene, and so are we, every one of us that you see before you.
 <s n="663"/>In our present case we ask your advice as to what we ought to do.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.1.17">
 <s n="664"/>Advise us in God’s name what you think fairest and best, something that will do you honour when it is reported in time to come: how Phalinos was once sent by the king to command Hellenes to lay down their arms, and they asked his advice, and this was his advice.
 <s n="665"/>You know it is inevitable, that whatever you advise will be told all over Hellas.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.1.18">
 <s n="666"/>Clearchos gave these hints hoping that the king’s ambassador might advise them himself not to lay down their arms, and then the others would be more confident.
 <s n="667"/>But Phalinos did the opposite; he turned round, and said,
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.1.19">
 <s n="668"/>“This is my advice. If you have one hope in ten thousand to make war against the king, and to save your lives, do not lay down your arms.
 <s n="669"/>But if there is no hope whatever of safety against the king’s wish, I advise you to save yourselves in the one way you can.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.1.20">
 <s n="670"/>Clearchos answered to this, “Very well, that is what you say: from us take this answer.
 <s n="671"/>We believe that if we are to be friends to the king, we should be more valuable friends with arms than without;
 <s n="672"/>and if we are to make war, it is better to make war with arms than without.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.1.21">
 <s n="673"/>Phalinos said, 
 <s n="674"/> “We will take back this answer; let me say that the king added, that there shall be a truce if you stay where you are, but if you move forwards or move backwards, war.
 <s n="675"/>Answer this too, then: will you stay, and is there a truce?
 <s n="676"/>Or shall I report that there is war?”
</section>

<section n="6" chapter="2.1.22">
 <s n="677"/>“Report then," said Clearchus, "that our view is the same as the king’s.”
 <s n="678"/>“What does that mean?” said Phalinos.
 <s n="679"/>Clearchos answered, 
 <s n="680"/> “If we stay where we are, a truce;
 <s n="681"/>“If we stay where we are, a truce;
 <s n="682"/>if we move forwards or backwards, war.”
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.1.23">
 <s n="683"/>He asked again, 
 <s n="684"/> “Truce or war, which?”
 <s n="685"/>Clearchos made the same answer again, 
 <s n="686"/>“Truce if we stay here, war if we move forwards or backwards.”
 <s n="687"/>But he did not indicate which he meant to do.
</section>

<section n="6" chapter="2.2.1">
 <s n="688"/>So Phalinos departed and those with him.
 <s n="689"/>Then Procles and Cheirisophos returned from Ariaios, 
 <s n="690"/> but Menon stayed with him.
 <s n="691"/>These reported that Ariaios said there were many Persians better than himself who would never let him be king;
 <s n="692"/>“but if you wish to go away with him, he tells you to come to-night.
 <s n="693"/>Otherwise, he says he will go early in the morning.”
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.2.2">
 <s n="694"/>Clearchos said, 
 <s n="695"/> “Very good, so be it.
 <s n="696"/>If we come, then as you say;
 <s n="697"/>if not, do whatever you think most profitable for yourselves.”
 <s n="698"/>But he did not tell these either what he meant to do.
</section>

<section n="8" chapter="2.2.3">
 <s n="699"/>After this, when the sun was already sinking, he called the captains and other officers together, and spoke to them.
 <s n="700"/>“Gentlemen,” he said, “I sacrifice for advance against the king, and the omens were not favourable.
 <s n="701"/>There is perhaps nothing strange in that;
 <s n="702"/> for between us and the king is the Tigris, a navigable river which we could not cross without boats, 
 <s n="703"/> and boats we have none.
 <s n="704"/>However, we cannot stay where we are, 
 <s n="705"/> for we cannot get provisions.
 <s n="706"/>But for going to the friends of Cyros the omens were completely favourable.”
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.2.4">
 <s n="707"/>“This is what we must do.
 <s n="708"/>Dismiss, and dine on what we can find:
 <s n="709"/>when the first horn sounds for turning in, pack up;
 <s n="710"/>at the second, put the stuff on the animals;
 <s n="711"/>at the third, follow your guide, keeping the animals next to the river, and the shields outside.”
</section>


<section n="2" chapter="2.2.5">
 <s n="712"/>The captains and officers did so;
 <s n="713"/>and for the rest, he led and they followed—not that they chose him, but they saw that he alone understood what the leader ought to do, and the others were inexperienced.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.2.6">
 <s n="714"/>The march from Ephesus in Ionia to the battlefield was 93 stages, 535 parasangs, 10,620 stadia;
 <s n="715"/>and from the battlefield to Babylon was said to be 360 stadia.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.2.7">
 <s n="716"/>After this, when darkness came, Miltocythes the Thracian deserted to the king, taking with him his horsemen, forty in number, and some three hundred of the Thracian footmen.
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.2.8">
 <s n="717"/>Clearchos led the others as he had told them, and they followed,
 <s n="718"/>and they reached Ariaios and his force at the first stage-house, about midnight.
 <s n="719"/>They grounded arms in their ranks; and there was a meeting of the captains and other officers with Ariaios.
 <s n="720"/>The Hellenes and Ariaios and the chief of those with him swore to be allies and to be faithful to one another;
 <s n="721"/>the nationals also swore to lead the way honestly.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.2.9">
 <s n="722"/>This they swore, cutting the throats of a bull and a boar and a ram over a shield: the Hellenes dipt a sword in the blood, the Asiatics a spear.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.2.10">
 <s n="723"/>When the oath had been taken, Clearchos said: “Now then, Ariaios, since the same journey is before us all, tell me what notion you have of the road.
 <s n="724"/>Are we to return the same way as we came, or have you thought of another that seems better?”
</section>


<section n="6" chapter="2.2.11">
 <s n="725"/>He said, 
 <s n="726"/> “If we go back by the way we came, we should perish of hunger outright;
 <s n="727"/>for we have no provisions now.
 <s n="728"/>Even on our way here we got nothing at all from the country on the seventeen last stages;
 <s n="729"/>where there was anything we have used it all up on the march already.
 <s n="730"/>The way we think of taking now is longer, but there will be no lack of provisions.”
</section>

<section n="6" chapter="2.2.12">
 <s n="731"/>“We must make the first stages as long as we can, that we may get away from the royal army as far as we can;
 <s n="732"/>for if once we can put two or three days’ journey between us, the king will never be able to catch us.
 <s n="733"/>With a small force he will not dare to follow;
 <s n="734"/>with a large force he will not be able to go fast,
 <s n="735"/>perhaps he will even fall short of provisions.
 <s n="736"/>That’s my opinion,” he said.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.2.13">
 <s n="737"/>This plan of campaign meant nothing more than to cut and run;
 <s n="738"/>but fortune planned better.
 <s n="739"/>When day came, they marched with the sun on their right hand, calculating that by sunset they would reach villages in the Babylonian country;
 <s n="740"/>and in this they were not deceived.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.2.14">
 <s n="741"/>But while it was still afternoon they thought they saw enemy horsemen;
 <s n="742"/>and those of the Hellenes who were not in their ranks fell in, and Ariaios (who was travelling in a wagon because he had been wounded) got down and put on his corselet, and so did those with him.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.2.15">
 <s n="743"/>While they were arming, their scouts came back reporting they were not cavalrymen but pack-animals grazing.
 <s n="744"/>And they soon understood that the king was encamped somewhere near,
 <s n="745"/>for smoke was seen in villages not far off.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.2.16">
 <s n="746"/>Clearchos did not lead against the enemy, for he knew that the men were weary and hungry, and it was late already;
 <s n="747"/>yet he kept straight on without swerving, for he would not let them think he was running away,
 <s n="748"/>and at sunset he brought up his van to the nearest villages and encamped.
 <s n="749"/>From these everything had been stripped by the royal army, down to the woodwork of the cottages.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.2.17">
 <s n="750"/>However, the first men encamped after a fashion, and those behind coming along in the dark lay in the open as it chanced, and they made a great noise calling to one another, so that even the enemy heard;
 <s n="751"/>and the nearest of the enemy even fled out of their encampment.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.2.18">
 <s n="752"/>This became clear next day;
 <s n="753"/>for not an animal was to be seen, no camp, no smoke anywhere near.
 <s n="754"/>The king himself was frightened, as it seemed, at the army’s approach,
 <s n="755"/>as he made clear by what he did the day after.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.2.19">
 <s n="756"/>However, as the night went on a panic fell on the Hellenes also and there was din and disturbance as one might expect in a panic.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.2.20">
 <s n="757"/>Clearchos sent round Tolmides the Elean, the herald who was with him as it happened, the best herald of his time; he was to call for silence, and proclaim from headquarters, one talent reward for information who let the ass loose in the camp.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.2.21">
 <s n="758"/>When this was proclaimed, the men knew that it was an empty panic and the commanders were safe and sound.
 <s n="759"/>At break of day Clearchos ordered the Hellenes to form in line in the same position which they had in the battle.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.1">
 <s n="760"/>It was clear now that the king had been terrified by their approach, as I wrote above;
 <s n="761"/>for the day before he had commanded them to lay down their arms, but now at sunrise he sent heralds to treat for a truce.
</section>


<section n="2" chapter="2.3.2">
 <s n="762"/>When they reached the outposts, these went in search of their commanders;
 <s n="763"/>and Clearchos, who happened to be then reviewing the lines, heard the report of the outposts, and sent back word that the heralds were to wait until he had leisure.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.3.3">
 <s n="764"/>When he had arranged his army so that a solid array could be easily seen everywhere, with no-one visible outside the forces, he summoned the mission,
 <s n="765"/>and went to meet them with the best-armed and finest men he had, and told the other captains to do the same.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.4">
 <s n="766"/>When he came up to the mission, he asked what they wanted.
 <s n="767"/>They answered that they came to arrange a truce, with full powers from the king to treat with the Hellenes, and to report their proposals to the king.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.5">
 <s n="768"/>Clearchos then said, “Report to him, then, that first there must be battle;
 <s n="769"/>we have no breakfast, and no man shall dare to speak of truce to Hellenes without providing breakfast.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.6">
 <s n="770"/>When the embassy heard this, they rode away, and soon came back;
 <s n="771"/>so it was plain that the king was somewhere near, or someone whose duty it was to arrange things.
 <s n="772"/>They said that the king thought it a reasonable request, and they had brought guides to take them where they would find breakfast, if they could arrange a truce.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.7">
 <s n="773"/>Clearchos asked whether the truce was offered just for the men as they went and came back, or was it a general truce for all.
 <s n="774"/>They replied, “For all, until we give our report to the king.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.8">
 <s n="775"/>When they had finished, Clearchos removed them and held council;
 <s n="776"/>and it seemed best to accept this truce and quietly to go and get the provisions.
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.3.9">
 <s n="777"/>So he said, “I agree with that, but I will not tell them at once;
 <s n="778"/>I will delay until the ambassadors are anxious for fear we may refuse to make truce.
 <s n="779"/>I think, however,” he said, “that our own men will fear the same!”
 <s n="780"/>When he thought it was the right time, he announced that he would make truce,
 <s n="781"/>and bade them lead him at once to the provisions.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.10">
 <s n="782"/>So they did; but Clearchos, although he had made the truce, marched with the army in battle order, himself commanding the rearguard.
 <s n="783"/>They came on ditches and canals full of water, which they could not cross without bridges;
 <s n="784"/>but they managed by making bridges of fallen date-palms or others which they cut down.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.11">
 <s n="785"/>Then one could learn what a commander Clearchos was.
 <s n="786"/>In his left hand he held the spear, a stick in his right; and if he thought there was any shirking, he picked out the right man and gave him one,
 <s n="787"/>and also he lent a hand himself, jumping into the mud, so that all were ashamed not to work as hard as he did.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.12">
 <s n="788"/>Those of thirty years and under were told off for this job;
 <s n="789"/>but when they saw how keen Clearchos was, the older men helped.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.3.13">
 <s n="790"/>What made him more anxious was that he suspected the ditches were not always so full of water, for it was not the season for watering the plain; he thought the king might have let in this water on purpose to show them how many dangers they would meet on the march.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.14">
 <s n="791"/>At last they reached villages from which the guides directed them to take the provisions.
 <s n="792"/>There was plenty of corn and date-wine and a sour drink made by boiling dates.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.15">
 <s n="793"/>The palm-nuts or date-nuts which we see in our country are such as were kept for servants;
 <s n="794"/>but those reserved for the masters were select, wonderfully fine and large, just like amber in colour: and some they dry and store for delicacies.
 <s n="795"/>These were delicious as a relish for wine, but they give headache.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.16">
 <s n="796"/>That was the first time the men tasted the brain or top-cabbage of the palm, and most of them were surprised at the shape and peculiar flavour.
 <s n="797"/>This also was very apt to give headache.
 <s n="798"/>When the brain was taken from the palm, the whole tree withered.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.3.17">
 <s n="799"/>There they remained three days.
 <s n="800"/>Then arrived Tissaphernes, and the queen’s brother, and three other Persians, with a long train of slaves.
 <s n="801"/>When the Hellenic captains met them, Tissaphernes first spoke through an interpreter.
 <s n="802"/>He said:
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.18">
 <s n="803"/>“I am a near neighbour to Hellas, gentlemen; and when I saw you follow into danger and difficulty, I thought it a grand opportunity if I could persuade the king as a favour to let me bring you back safe to Hellas;
 <s n="804"/>for I think that would earn me your thanks and your country’s.
</section>






<section n="2" chapter="2.3.19">
 <s n="805"/>With this intention I put my petition before the king, saying that he might fairly gratify me because I first told him that Cyros was advancing against him, and I brought him aid along with my news;
 <s n="806"/>and I alone of all those who faced the Hellenes did not take to flight, but I rode through and joined the king in your camp which he had then reached, after killing Cyros and pursuing the nationals with Cyros along with the forces which are with me now, the most loyal of all his men. He promised me that he would consider this;
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.3.20">
 <s n="807"/>but he told me to come and ask why you made war upon him.
 <s n="808"/>And I advise you to answer moderately that it may be easier for me to find how I may arrange something good for you.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.21">
 <s n="809"/>The Hellenes retired and consulted what to say;
 <s n="810"/>and they answered, Clearchos being spokesman:
 <s n="811"/>“We did not assemble to make war on the king, nor did we march against the king; but Cyros found many excuses, as you know well yourself, that he might catch you unprepared and bring us here.”
</section>



<section n="1" chapter="2.3.22">
 <s n="812"/>However, when we saw him in danger, we were really ashamed before gods and men to desert him, after offering ourselves to him long since for his service.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.23">
 <s n="813"/>But now that Cyros is dead, we do not lay claim to the king’s empire, and there is no reason why we should damage the king’s country or wish to kill the king himself.
 <s n="814"/>We wish to march home, if no-one molests us; but if we are injured we shall try to defend ourselves, with God’s help.
 <s n="815"/>If moreover, anyone turns up to help us, we shall be no less ready to help him as far as we can.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.3.24">
 <s n="816"/>Tissaphernes listened to him, and said, 
 <s n="817"/> “I will report this to the king, and bring back his answer.
 <s n="818"/>Until I return let the truce be kept, 
 <s n="819"/>and I will provide you a market.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.25">
 <s n="820"/>Next day he did not return, 
 <s n="821"/> and the Hellenes were anxious;
 <s n="822"/>but he came the day after, and said he had procured from the king the boon of safety for the Hellenes, although many had urged that it was unworthy for the king to let go those who had made war against him.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.26">
 <s n="823"/>At the end he said, 
 <s n="824"/> “Now you may receive our promise to keep the country at peace with you, and to take you back to Hellas without fraud;
 <s n="825"/>we will provide a market, and wherever you cannot buy, we will allow you to take what is necessary from the country.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.3.27">
 <s n="826"/>“You on your part must take your oath to march as through a friendly country, only taking food and drink without violence whenever we do not provide a market, but when we do you must buy provisions.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.3.28">
 <s n="827"/>This was agreed; they took the oath, and Tissaphernes and the queen’s brother gave their right hands to the captains and officers of the Hellenes, and these did the like.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.3.29">
 <s n="828"/>After this Tissaphernes said, 
 <s n="829"/>“Now, then, I return to the king;
 <s n="830"/>when I have arranged what I wish, I will come back ready to conduct you to Hellas, and myself to return to my province.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.1">
 <s n="831"/>After this the Hellenes and Ariaios waited for Tissaphernes, encamped near each other, for more than twenty days.
 <s n="832"/>Meanwhile Ariaios was visited by brothers and other relations, and some of his people had visits from Persians, who encouraged them, and brought pledges to some from the king, that the king would bear no malice against them for their campaign with Cyros or for anything else now bygone.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.2">
 <s n="833"/>While this went on Ariaios’s people clearly began to think less about the Hellenes.
 <s n="834"/>So for this reason they displeased many of the Hellenes; and these complained to Clearchos and the other captains, and said:
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.4.3">
 <s n="835"/>“What are we waiting for?
 <s n="836"/>Don’t we know that the king would give anything to destroy us, and show our whole nation what a terror it is to make war on the Great King?
 <s n="837"/>Now he deludes us to stay because his army is scattered;
 <s n="838"/>but when the whole army is assembled again there is no doubt he will attack us.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.4">
 <s n="839"/>“Perhaps they are digging somewhere or fortifying somewhere to make the road impassable.
 <s n="840"/>He will never be willing, if he can help it, that we shall go back to Hellas and report that a few men like us beat the king in front of his own door, and got away to laugh at him.”
</section>

<section n="7" chapter="2.4.5">
 <s n="841"/>Clearchos answered, 
 <s n="842"/> “I have all this in my own mind, 
 <s n="843"/> but I remember that if we go now he will think we have broken the truce and gone out for war.
 <s n="844"/>Then again, firstly no-one will make us a market or help us to get provisions;
 <s n="845"/>secondly, there will be no-one to guide us;
 <s n="846"/>thirdly, if we do anything like that Ariaios will leave us at once.
 <s n="847"/>So we shall not have one friend left, and even those who were friends will be our enemies.”
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.4.6">
 <s n="848"/>“Then the river: if there is any other difficult river for us I don’t know, 
 <s n="849"/>but the Euphrates we know cannot be crossed if the enemy oppose.
 <s n="850"/>Further, we have no cavalry to help us if we must fight, but the enemy have plenty, and very good;
 <s n="851"/>so if we win, how could we kill anyone?
 <s n="852"/>And if we are beaten, none of us could escape.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.7">
 <s n="853"/>“As for the king, with all these things in his favour, if he really wishes to destroy us, I don’t know why we need swear oaths and give pledges, and then by perjury make his own word worthless to all the world, both ours and his!”
 <s n="854"/>He would often speak like this.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.8">
 <s n="855"/>Meanwhile Tissaphernes came with his forces, as on the road home, and Orontas came with his;
 <s n="856"/>he brought also the king’s daughter, his bride.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.9">
 <s n="857"/>They left the place at once, Tissaphernes leading and providing a market;
 <s n="858"/>Ariaios marched also at the head of Cyros’s national force, along with Tissaphernes and Orontas, and camped with them.
</section>


<section n="3" chapter="2.4.10">
 <s n="859"/>But the Hellenes suspected these, and kept to themselves with their own guides.
 <s n="860"/>They always camped with a distance between them, a league or more; 
 <s n="861"/>both parties watched each other like enemies, and this at once made more suspicion.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.11">
 <s n="862"/>Sometimes when they were gathering wood from the same place, and foraging for grass and such things, they came to blows with each other, 
 <s n="863"/> and this made more hostility.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.4.12">
 <s n="864"/>After they had gone three stages they reached what is called the Wall of Media, and passed behind it.
 <s n="865"/>This was built of baked bricks set in asphalt, twenty feet thick, one hundred high;
 <s n="866"/>its length was said to be twenty leagues,
 <s n="867"/> and it is not far from Babylon.
</section>

<section n="7" chapter="2.4.13">
 <s n="868"/>From this place they marched two stages, 8 leagues; 
 <s n="869"/> and they crossed two canals, one by a bridge, one by eight boats fastened together.
 <s n="870"/>These canals came from the River Tigris; 
 <s n="871"/> trenches are dug from them over the land, the first larger, then smaller ones,
 <s n="872"/> until at last they become little runnels such as we have in Hellas over millet fields.
 <s n="873"/>Then they reached the Tigris, 
 <s n="874"/>near which was a city, large and populous, named Sittace, distant from the river about a mile and a half.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.14">
 <s n="875"/>The Hellenes encamped near it beside a park, large and beautiful and thick with all sorts of trees;
 <s n="876"/>but the nationals crossed the river first, where they were quite hidden from view.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.4.15">
 <s n="877"/>After dinner it happened that Proxenos and Xenophon were walking in front of the camp,
 <s n="878"/> and a man asked the sentries where he could see Proxenos or Clearchos;
 <s n="879"/>he did not ask for Menon, although he came from Ariaios, Menon’s friend.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.4.16">
 <s n="880"/>When Proxenos said, “I’m the man you are looking for,” the fellow said, 
 <s n="881"/>“I have been sent by Ariaios and Artaozos, who were true to Cyros and remain loyal to you. They bid you be on your guard against an attack to-night; 
 <s n="882"/> there is quite an army close by in the park.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.4.17">
 <s n="883"/>And they bid you to send a guard for the Tigris bridge, because Tissaphernes intends to break it down to-night that you may not cross but may be caught between the river and the canal.”
</section>


<section n="2" chapter="2.4.18">
 <s n="884"/>Hearing this they took him to Clearchos, and told him what he said.
 <s n="885"/>Clearchos was very much upset and alarmed.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.4.19">
 <s n="886"/>But a young man present observed that the attack and breaking down the bridge did not fit well together.
 <s n="887"/>“If they attack,” he said, “they must either win or lose.
 <s n="888"/>If they win, why break down the bridge?
 <s n="889"/>For then we could never escape and be safe however many bridges there are.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.20">
 <s n="890"/>“But if we win, with a broken bridge they will never escape themselves;
 <s n="891"/>nor will any of that multitude across the river be able to help them with a broken bridge.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.21">
 <s n="892"/>Clearchos listened, and asked the messenger how much country there was between the Tigris and the canal.
 <s n="893"/>He said a great deal, and many villages and many large cities.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.4.22">
 <s n="894"/>Then he understood at once that the Persians had sent the man, for fear the Hellenes might cut the bridge and stay in the island, defended by the Tigris on one side and the canal on the other, 
 <s n="895"/> and might get their provisions from the country between, which was large and good with plenty of hands to till it;
 <s n="896"/>then again, it might be a refuge for anyone who wanted to damage the king.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.4.23">
 <s n="897"/>After this they went to rest;
 <s n="898"/> however, they still sent a guard for the bridge.
 <s n="899"/>And no-one attacked them from any part, and none of the enemy came to the bridge, as the guards reported.
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.4.24">
 <s n="900"/>But at dawn they crossed the bridge, which was made of thirty-seven vessels fastened together;
 <s n="901"/> they took all possible precautions, for some Hellenes from Tissaphernes brought word that they meant to attack while they were crossing.
 <s n="902"/>But this was false;
 <s n="903"/> however, as they were crossing, Glus appeared with some scouts to see if they were crossing the river, 
 <s n="904"/>and when he saw, he galloped away.
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.4.25">
 <s n="905"/>From the Tigris they marched four stages, 20 leagues, to the River Physcos, breadth 100 feet;
 <s n="906"/> there was a bridge across.
 <s n="907"/>There also was a large city, named Opis.
 <s n="908"/>There the Hellenes were met by the bastard brother of Cyros and Artaxerxes, from Susa and Ecbatana, bringing a large army to support the king; 
 <s n="909"/> and halting his army, he watched the Hellenes passing by.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.4.26">
 <s n="910"/>Clearchos led them two abreast, sometimes marching and sometimes halting.
 <s n="911"/>As long as the leading section of the army halted, so long there must be a halt through the whole army;
 <s n="912"/>so as the command ran down, the whole army seemed to be enormous, even to the Hellenes themselves, and the Persian was dumbfounded at the sight.
</section>


<section n="3" chapter="2.4.27">
 <s n="913"/>Thence they marched through Media seven desert stages, 30 leagues, as far as the villages of Parysatis, the mother of Cyros and Artaxerxes.
 <s n="914"/>These Tissaphernes, in mockery of Cyros, gave over to the Hellenes to plunder, but no captives to be taken.
 <s n="915"/>There was plenty of corn there and sheep and other things.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.4.28">
 <s n="916"/>From there they marched four desert stages, 20 leagues, keeping the Tigris on the left.
 <s n="917"/>At the first stage beyond the river was an inhabited city named Cainai, large and prosperous, from which the natives used to bring over on rafts of skin, loaves, cheeses, and wine.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.5.1">
 <s n="918"/>After this they reached the River Zapatas, breadth 400 feet.
 <s n="919"/>There they stayed three days;
 <s n="920"/> at this time there were suspicions, but no manifest plot.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.2">
 <s n="921"/>So Clearchos resolved to approach Tissaphernes, and see whether he could stop these suspicions before they brought war.
 <s n="922"/>He sent word that he wished to meet him.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.5.3">
 <s n="923"/>Tissaphernes readily invited him to come.
 <s n="924"/>When they went, Clearchos said:
 <s n="925"/>"I know, Tissaphernes, that oaths have been sworn and pledges given between us that we will not harm each other;
 <s n="926"/>but I see you watching us like enemies, and we, seeing that, watch you too."
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.4">
 <s n="927"/>"But as by my inquiry I cannot perceive that you try to do us any hurt, and I know well that we never think even of such a thing, I thought it best to speak to you in hopes we may get rid of our mutual distrust."
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.5">
 <s n="928"/>"For I know it has often happened that either from slander or from suspicion men have been frightened of each other, and wishing to get in first before they suffer, they have done incurable mischief to others who were not intending to do anything of the sort, nor indeed wishing to do it."
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.6">
 <s n="929"/>"I think such misunderstandings are best ended by meeting face to face; so here I am, to assure you that you have no reason to distrust us."
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.5.7">
 <s n="930"/>"The first and chiefest thing is, that our oaths before heaven stop us from being enemies;
 <s n="931"/>I could never deem one happy who knows he has disregarded his oath.
 <s n="932"/>In war against heaven, what speed could save? What place could be a refuge?
 <s n="933"/>How could one escape? What darkness could he run to? What stronghold could receive him? All things in every place are in God’s hands, God equally rules all."
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.8">
 <s n="934"/>"That is what I think about the oaths, and about the gods before whom we made our pact of friendship and deposited it there;
 <s n="935"/>but of human things, I think at this present you are the best thing we have."
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="2.5.9">
 <s n="936"/>"For with you every road is easy, every river can be passed, there is no want of provisions.
 <s n="937"/>Without you every road goes through darkness,
 <s n="938"/> for we know nothing about it;
 <s n="939"/>every river is hard to cross, every crowd terrible, and most terrible of all the desert,
 <s n="940"/> all full of want."
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.10">
 <s n="941"/>"If indeed we were so mad as to kill you, by killing our benefactor should we not bring into the ring against us that most mighty champion in reserve, the king himself?
 <s n="942"/>Now for myself: let me tell you what high hopes and prospects I should rob myself of, if I tried to do you any harm."
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.11">
 <s n="943"/>I wished Cyros to be my friend because I thought him most able to benefit anyone he might wish;
 <s n="944"/>but you I see now in his place. You hold the power and domain of Cyros and keep your own besides; and the king’s power, which was Cyros’s enemy, is your ally."
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.12">
 <s n="945"/>"This being so, who is so mad that he does not wish to be your friend?
 <s n="946"/>Besides, I will tell you reasons why I have some hope that you will wish to be mine."
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.5.13">
 <s n="947"/>"I know the Mysians are a nuisance to you, and I think I could make them humble with the force I have here.
 <s n="948"/>I know about the Pisidians too;
 <s n="949"/>and there are many other nations like that, which I think I could make to cease from troubling your happiness.
 <s n="950"/>The Egyptians, too, I understand you Persians are very angry with them; and I don’t see any force which could help you to punish them better than this I now have."
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.14">
 <s n="951"/>"Then again, take your own neighbours; if you wished to be a friend you could be the greatest friend, and suppose anyone annoyed you, as master you might turn upon him if you had us to serve you, for we should serve you, not for the pay, but for the gratitude we should owe for saving us."
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.5.15">
 <s n="952"/>"When I think over all this in my mind it seems astonishing that you distrust us, and I would gladly hear you tell me by name who is an orator clever enough to persuade you that we are plotting against you."
 <s n="953"/>Tissaphernes then answered the speech of Clearchus:
 <s n="954"/>"Indeed I am glad, Clearchos, to hear your sensible words. For if that is your mind, I think you would be your own enemy as well as mine if you should plan mischief against me."
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.5.16">
 <s n="955"/>"Indeed I am glad, Clearchos, to hear your sensible words.
 <s n="956"/>For if that is your mind, I think you would be your own enemy as well as mine if you should plan mischief against me.
 <s n="957"/>Listen, and I will show you that you have just as little reason to distrust either the king or me."
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.17">
 <s n="958"/>"If we wished to destroy you, do you think we lack cavalry or infantry or armament, quite enough to damage you with no fear of suffering any harm in return?"
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.5.18">
 <s n="959"/>Then do you think there would be no suitable places for attack?
 <s n="960"/>Remember all the plains which you traverse with such hardship even while they are friendly.
 <s n="961"/>Look at all the mountains you must cross, which we could occupy before you and make impassable. Think of all the great rivers, where we can regulate our portions and decide how many we want to fight with. Some of them you could not possibly cross unless we ferried you over!"
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.19">
 <s n="962"/>"Even if we should fail in all these, at least let me remind you, ‘Fire is stronger than fruit’;
 <s n="963"/> we could burn up all the crops and enlist famine against you—however strong you are, you couldn’t fight that!"
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.20">
 <s n="964"/>"Then while we have so many ways to make war upon you, all without danger for ourselves, why should we choose the only way which is impious before heaven, the only way which is disgraceful before men?"
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.21">
 <s n="965"/>"Men must be wholly helpless and resourceless and constrained by necessity, and they must be wicked too, if they are willing to use perjury to God and treachery to men in doing a thing.
 <s n="966"/>No, Clearchos, we are not so unreasonable or so foolish as that."
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.22">
 <s n="967"/>"Well, then, if we could destroy you, why did we not go so far?
 <s n="968"/>I assure you the reason was my passion to be trusted by the Hellenes. Cyros came up country trusting to this mercenary force, and I wished to go down again, made strong by benefits done to his same force."
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.23">
 <s n="969"/>"How you can be useful to me you have said, at least partly, but the greatest thing of all, I know:
 <s n="970"/>the tiara on the head only the king may wear upright, but the tiara on the heart perhaps with you to help another might find it easy to wear so."
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.5.24">
 <s n="971"/>Clearchos thought he spoke honestly, and he said:
 <s n="972"/>“Then since we are such friends, those who try by calumny to make us enemies are worthy of death?”
 <s n="973"/>“Yes,” Tissaphernes said, “and if your captains and your officers will come here, I will tell you openly who accuse you of plotting against me and my army.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.25">
<s n="974"/>“Yes,” Tissaphernes said, “and if your captains and your officers will come here, I will tell you openly who accuse you of plotting against me and my army.” 
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.26">
 <s n="975"/>“Then I will bring them all,” said Clearchos, “and I will reveal to you also where I hear this about you.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.27">
 <s n="976"/>After this conversation Tissaphernes made a great fuss of him, and asked him to stay, and they dined together.
 <s n="977"/>Next day when Clearchos came back to the camp it was clear that he thought himself great in friendship with Tissaphernes. He reported what he had said. “We must all go,” he said, “as he asks, and any of us who are proved to be calumniating him, must be punished as traitors and enemies to us all.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.28">
 <s n="978"/>But he suspected the calumniator was Menon; for he knew that he and Ariaios had meetings with Tissaphernes, and that he was intriguing and scheming against himself in order to get command of the whole army and make himself a friend to Tissaphernes.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.29">
 <s n="979"/>Clearchos also wished that the whole army should follow his will, and that troublesome persons should be out of the way.
 <s n="980"/>But some of the soldiers protested that the captains and officers ought not all to go and trust Tissaphernes.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.30">
 <s n="981"/>But Clearchos insisted strongly, until he arranged that five captains should go and twenty other officers; 
 <s n="982"/>and about two hundred soldiers went with them to do their marketing.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.31">
 <s n="983"/>When they reached the door of Tissaphernes the captains were invited in, Proxenos the Boeotian, Menon the Thessalian, Agias the Arcadian, Clearchos the Laconian, Socrates the Achaean;
 <s n="984"/>the other officers stayed at the door.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.32">
 <s n="985"/>A little while afterwards, at one signal, those inside were seized, and those outside were cut down.
 <s n="986"/>Soon after a number of barbarian horsemen rode about the plain, and killed every Hellene they met, slave or free.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.33">
 <s n="987"/>The men wondered at this display of horsemanship from the camp, and did not know what to make of it until a fugitive came in, Nicarchos, an Arcadian, wounded in the belly and holding his bowels in his hands, and told what had happened.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.34">
 <s n="988"/>Then all the Hellenes ran for their arms, all aghast, expecting they would come to the camp instantly.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.5.35">
 <s n="989"/>They did not all come, but Ariaios and Artaozos and Mithradates came, who had been Cyros’s most trusted friends;
 <s n="990"/>the interpreter of the Hellenes said he saw and recognized the brother of Tissaphernes with them.
 <s n="991"/>They were attended by some three hundred Persians in corselets.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.36">
 <s n="992"/>When these came near they called for any captain or officer that they might deliver the king’s message.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.37">
 <s n="993"/>After this two captains came out, carefully guarded, Cleanor the Orchomenian, and Sophainetos the Stymphalian, and with them Xenophon the Athenian, who wished to hear news of Proxenos;
 <s n="994"/>Cheirisophos happened to be absent in a village with others getting provisions.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.5.38">
 <s n="995"/>When they stood within hearing, Ariaios said: “Listen, Hellenes.
 <s n="996"/>Clearchos was shown to have perjured himself and broken the truce; therefore he has been punished and he is dead; but Proxenos and Menon, because they reported his plot, are in high honour.
 <s n="997"/>You lay down your arms, that is the king’s demand; 
 <s n="998"/>he says they are his, because they belonged to Cyros his slave.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.5.39">
 <s n="999"/>The Hellenes made Cleanor their spokesman to answer, and he said, “You abominable wretch Ariaios, and you others who were friends of Cyros, have you no shame before gods and men?
 <s n="1000"/>You swore the oath to have the same friends and enemies as we did, and you have betrayed us, with Tissaphernes, that most impious and godless villain! You have murdered the very man to whom you gave your oath, and you have betrayed us all, and you join our enemies to come against us!”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.5.40">
 <s n="1001"/>Ariaios replied,
 <s n="1002"/> “Because Clearchos was proved to have plotted first against Tissaphernes and Orontas, and against us all who are with them.”
 <s n="1003"/>Xenophon rejoined upon this,
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="2.5.41">
 <s n="1004"/>Clearchos, then, is punished, if he broke the truce contrary to his oath,
 <s n="1005"/> for the perjured are justly put to death,
 <s n="1006"/>but what of Proxenos and Menon? They are your benefactors, and they are our captains, then send them here;
 <s n="1007"/>for it is clear that being friends to both parties they will try to advise the best both for you and for us.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.5.42">
 <s n="1008"/>The barbarians discussed this for a long time, and then went away without answering a word.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.6.1">
 <s n="1009"/>The captains, captured thus, were carried up to the king and there beheaded.
 <s n="1010"/>One of them, Clearchos, was a true soldier and war was his passion, as all agreed who formed their opinions from personal knowledge.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.6.3">
 <s n="1011"/>However, the ephors changed their minds as soon as he had set out, and tried to turn him back from the Isthmus; but he would not obey them any longer, and sailed away to the Hellespont.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.4">
 <s n="1012"/>Consequently, he was sentenced to death by the Spartan government for disobedience to orders.
 <s n="1013"/>He was already a banished man when he came to Cyros.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.5">
 <s n="1014"/>He took the money, but he did not turn to a lazy life: no, with this money he collected a force and made war on the Thracians, and conquered them in battle;
 <s n="1015"/>he ravaged them after that, and went on with the war until Cyros wanted his army—then he went off to make war again under him.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.6">
 <s n="1016"/>These seem to me the doings of a man whose passion was war.When he could have kept at peace without shame or damage, he chose war; when he could have been idle, he wished for hard work that he might have war; when he could have kept wealth without danger, he chose to make it less by making war; 
 <s n="1017"/>there was a man who spent upon war as if it were a darling lover or some other pleasure.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.7">
 <s n="1018"/>So war was his passion; and he seemed a true soldier, because he loved perils;
 <s n="1019"/>ready to lead against the enemy by day or by night, and he kept his head in danger, as those with him agreed one and all in every place.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.8">
 <s n="1020"/>He had also the repute of a true leader, as far as was possible for a man of his character.
 <s n="1021"/>He was able to think out ways to get provisions for his forces, and then to get them; and he was able to fix firmly in their minds that Clearchos must be obeyed.
</section>





<section n="2" chapter="2.6.9">
 <s n="1022"/>This he did by being severe;
 <s n="1023"/> he was grim to look at and harsh in voice, he punished heavily and sometimes in anger, so that now and again he was sorry for it.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.10">
 <s n="1024"/>And he punished on principle, for he thought an unpunished army was good for nothing; indeed, he used to say that a soldier ought to fear his commander more than the enemy,
 <s n="1025"/>if he was to keep guard, or keep his hand off friends, or attack the enemy without excuses.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.11">
 <s n="1026"/>In danger, however, the soldiers wished absolutely to obey him and chose no other;
 <s n="1027"/>at such times his grimness appeared brightness, they said, his severity seemed strength against the enemy, so that they thought it salvation rather than severity.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.12">
 <s n="1028"/>But when the danger was past, many left him if there was an opportunity to pass over to other commanders;
 <s n="1029"/>for he had no charm, and he was always severe and cruel,
 <s n="1030"/>so the soldiers felt towards him as boys towards a schoolmaster.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.13">
 <s n="1031"/>But when they began to succeed in conquering an enemy under him, his men had strong motives to make them useful; for one thing, they felt confident of victory, 
 <s n="1032"/>and for another, they feared punishment from him, which made them obedient.
 
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.14">
 <s n="1033"/>But when they began to conquer the enemy with him, the benefits were great.
 <s n="1034"/>The soldiers gained courage against the enemy and feared his discipline, which made them orderly.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.15">
 <s n="1035"/>Such was Clearchos as a commander;
 <s n="1036"/>but it was said he did not like very much to be commanded himself.
 <s n="1037"/>He was about fifty years old when he died.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.16">
 <s n="1038"/>Proxenos the Boeotian ever since the days of his youth had longed to become a man capable of doing great things;
 <s n="1039"/>and because of this longing he paid his fees to Gorgias of Lentinoi.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.17">
 <s n="1040"/>After being with Gorgias he thought himself at once quite capable of being a ruler,
 <s n="1041"/>and since he was a friend of the first men of his day he thought himself the equal of any man as a benefactor.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.6.18">
 <s n="1042"/>Yet although he longed so much for these great things, all the same it was quite clear that he did not wish to get any of them by injustice; with justice and honour he thought these things ought to be got, but not without.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.19">
 <s n="1043"/>To rule gentlemen he was well able,
 <s n="1044"/>but still he could not put fear or respect for himself into his soldiers. Indeed, he had rather respect for the soldiers than they had for him, 
 <s n="1045"/> and he obviously feared rather to be disliked by the soldiers than the soldiers feared to disobey him.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.20">
 <s n="1046"/>He thought that to praise one who did well, and not to praise the offender, was enough to make a ruler and the reputation of a ruler.
 <s n="1047"/>Therefore the gentlemen among his associates liked him and wished him well, but common men schemed against him and thought him easy game.
 <s n="1048"/>When he died, he was about thirty years old.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.21">
 <s n="1049"/>There was no doubt about Menon the Thessalian; it was obvious that he desired with all his heart to be rich, that he desired to rule in order that he might get more, that he desired to be honoured in order that he might make more profit.
 <s n="1050"/>He wished to be a friend to the most powerful that he might do wrong and not be punished.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.6.22">
 <s n="1051"/>He believed that the shortest way to accomplish what he desired was perjury and lying and deceit, but simplicity and truth were just foolishness.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="2.6.23">
 <s n="1052"/>It was clear that he had affection for no one, but he obviously plotted against any he called friend.
 <s n="1053"/>He never laughed at an enemy, but he always talked as though he was laughing at all his associates.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.24">
 <s n="1054"/>He had no designs against the possessions of his enemies,
 <s n="1055"/> for he thought it was difficult to get hold of things well-guarded;
 <s n="1056"/>but he believed he was the sole discoverer of how easy it is to get hold of the unprotected, that is, what his friends had.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.6.25">
 <s n="1057"/>If he found out that any were perjured and dishonest, he feared them as being well-armed; but pious men, and those who practised truth, he tried to use as being cowards.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.26">
 <s n="1058"/>As others pride themselves on godliness and truth and honesty, so Menon prided himself on his power to deceive, to fabricate falsehoods, to mock at his friends.
 <s n="1059"/>If a man was not unscrupulous, he always thought him one of the uneducated.
 <s n="1060"/>If he wanted to make himself first in the friendship of any, he thought it should be done by slandering those who were first.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.27">
 <s n="1061"/>To make his soldiers obedient, his principle was to share in their misdeeds;
 <s n="1062"/>he expected to be honoured and courted if he showed that he would and could do most in that line.
 <s n="1063"/>If ever anyone held aloof, he called it a real kindness that he had not destroyed him while they were friends.
</section>


<section n="2" chapter="2.6.28">
 <s n="1064"/>There are indeed certain obscure charges against him which may not be true, but here are some things which everyone knows.
 <s n="1065"/>He was in the bloom of his youth when he got command of the mercenaries from Aristippos; he became very intimate with Ariaios, who was a barbarian, because the man liked handsome lads; he himself, while yet beardless, had a bearded favourite in Tharypas.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="2.6.29">
 <s n="1066"/>When his fellow-generals were put to death for marching with Cyros against the king, he was not put to death although he had done the same; but after the death of the others, he was punished with death by the king, not beheaded like Clearchos and the rest, which is thought to be the quickest death, but it is said that he was kept alive for a year and tortured until he died the death of a malefactor.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="2.6.30">
 <s n="1067"/>Agias the Arcadian and Socrates the Achaian were also put to death.
 <s n="1068"/>No one had a bad word to say of these, either as soldiers or as friends.
 <s n="1069"/>Both were about five-and-thirty years of age.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.1">
 <s n="1070"/><note>GRC: Rouse does not translate this summary of the previous book.</note> 
</section>


<section n="1" chapter="3.1.2">
 <s n="1071"/>After the captains had been seized, and the officers and men who were their escort had been cut down, the Hellenes were in despair, full of anxiety.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.3">
 <s n="1072"/>These thoughts passed through their minds and made them spiritless.
 <s n="1073"/>Few of them tasted food that evening, few lit a fire; many of them never came into camp that night, but they rested each where he chanced to be.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.4">
 <s n="1074"/>There was in the army an Athenian, Xenophon, who came with them neither as captain nor officer nor man, but Proxenos had invited him to come, being an old family friend. 
 <s n="1075"/> Proxenos promised, if he would come, to introduce him to Cyros, who was, he said, more than home and country to himself.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.5">
 <s n="1076"/>Xenophon read his letter, and consulted Socrates the philosopher about this trip.
 <s n="1077"/>Socrates had a suspicion that there might be some state objection to his being friendly with Cyros, because Cyros had favoured the Lacedaimonians in their war against Athens; so he advised Xenophon to go to Delphi and inquire of the oracle about this journey.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.6">
 <s n="1078"/>Accordingly Xenophon went and asked Apollo what god he should sacrifice and pray to, that he might best accomplish the journey he had in mind, and come back safe and successful.
 <s n="1079"/>Apollo named the gods to whom he must sacrifice.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.7">
 <s n="1080"/>When he came back he told the oracle to Socrates.
 <s n="1081"/>But Socrates blamed him because he had not asked first whether it was better for him to go or to stay, but just decided to go, and then asked how he could best do it.
 <s n="1082"/>“But,” said he, “since you did ask that, you must do what the god bids.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.8">
 <s n="1083"/>So Xenophon sacrificed to those gods as he was directed, and set sail; and at Sardis he found Proxenos and Cyros on the point of going up country, and then he was introduced to Cyros.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.9">
 <s n="1084"/>Proxenos urged him to stay, and Cyros urged him too, and promised he would send him home as soon as the campaign was over.
 <s n="1085"/>The expedition was said to be against the Pisidians.
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="3.1.10">
 <s n="1086"/>And so he joined it, being thus deceived—but not by Proxenos, 
 else of the Hellenes except Clearchos.
 <s n="1087"/>for he did not know of the attack to be made upon the king, nor did anyone else of the Hellenes except Clearchos.
 <s n="1088"/>However, when they got as far as Cilicia, it seemed to be clear to all that they were marching against the king.
 <s n="1089"/>Most of them followed for shame of one another and Cyros, although they feared the journey and went against their will.
 <s n="1090"/>And one of these was Xenophon.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.11">
 <s n="1091"/>And now in their desperate plight, he was unhappy like the rest and could not sleep.
 <s n="1092"/>But he did snatch a nap, and then he saw a dream.
 <s n="1093"/>He thought there was lightning and a thunderbolt fell on his father’s house, and all was in a blaze.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.12">
 <s n="1094"/>He woke at once in terror; the dream he judged to be good in one way, because amid troubles and dangers he seemed to see a great light from Zeus.
 <s n="1095"/>But in another way he feared, because he considered the dream to come from Zeus the king and the blaze of fire seemed to be all round, so he feared difficulties would fence him in all round, and he might not be able to get out of the country of the king.
</section>
<section n="2" chapter="3.1.13">
<s n="1096"/> But what such a dream really means may be seen from what happened afterwards; 
<s n="1097"/>and this is what happened. 
<s n="1098"/>As soon as he was awake, the first thought that came into his head was, 
<s n="1099"/>“Why do I lie here? 
<s n="1100"/>The night is going on, 
<s n="1101"/>and with daylight it is likely the enemy will come. 
<s n="1102"/>If we fall into the power of the king, now we must see the most cruel sights, and feel the most terrible pains, and die by violence—and how can it be helped? 

</section>
<section n="4" chapter="3.1.14">
 <s n="1103"/>But how are we to defend ourselves?
 <s n="1104"/>No-one is preparing for that, no-one cares about that, but we lie here as if it were possible to live in peace and quiet!
 <s n="1105"/>What about me then?
 <s n="1106"/>What captain from what country do I expect to do that?
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.15">
 <s n="1107"/>Then he got up, and first called together the underofficers of Proxenos.
 <s n="1108"/>When they were collected he said: "Gentlemen, I cannot sleep and I don’t think you can.
 <s n="1109"/>And I can’t lie here when I see what a plight we are in."
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.16">
 <s n="1110"/>It is clear that the enemy did not show us open war until they thought they had everything well prepared; and no one among us takes the pains to make the best possible resistance.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.17">
 <s n="1111"/>Yet if we give way and fall into the king’s power, what do we expect our fate will be?
 <s n="1112"/>When his own half-brother was dead, the man cut off his head and cut off his hand and stuck them up on a pole.
 <s n="1113"/>We have no one to plead for us, and we marched here to make the king a slave or to kill him if we could, and what do you think our fate will be?
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.18">
 <s n="1114"/>Would he not go to all extremes of torture to make the whole world afraid of making war on him?
 <s n="1115"/>Why, we must do anything to keep out of his power!
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.19">
 <s n="1116"/>While the truce lasted, I never ceased pitying ourselves, I never ceased congratulating the king and his army.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.20">
 <s n="1117"/>But when I thought of these our soldiers—we had no share in all these good things unless we bought them, and few had anything left to buy with; and to procure anything without buying was debarred by our oaths.
 <s n="1118"/>While I reasoned like this, I sometimes feared the truce more than the war now.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.21">
 <s n="1119"/>However, now they have broken the truce, there is an end both to their insolence and to our suspicion.
 <s n="1120"/>There lie all these good things before us, prizes for whichever side proves the better men; the gods are the judges of the contest, and they will be with us, naturally.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.22">
 <s n="1121"/>These men are perjured before them,
 <s n="1122"/> while we saw so many good things and kept strictly away from them because of our oaths sworn to the gods.
 <s n="1123"/>therefore we may enter the contest, it seems to me, with much better hearts than they can.

</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.23">
 <s n="1124"/>Besides, our bodies are better fitted than theirs for bearing cold and heat and hardship.
 <s n="1125"/>And our souls are better, thank God!
 <s n="1126"/>They are men easier to wound and to kill than we are if the gods give us victory as before.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.24">
 <s n="1127"/>Perhaps others are now thinking the same, but in heaven’s name don’t let us wait for someone else to come and pat us on the back and say, 'Go it. Here’s a grand enterprise!'
 <s n="1128"/>Let us take the lead and show the others how to be brave! Show yourselves the best of officers, and as worthy to be captains as the captains themselves!
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.25">
 <s n="1129"/>Count on me, if you are willing to make the start; I will follow you, or if you order me to lead, I will not make my youth an excuse, but I think I am old enough to keep danger from myself!
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.26">
 <s n="1130"/>The officers on hearing this address all asked him to be their leader, except a certain Apollonides who spoke in the Boeotian dialect.
 <s n="1131"/>He said it was all nonsense to pretend that any safety could be found except with the king’s consent, if that could be had, and at the same time he began to speak of the difficulties.
 <s n="1132"/>But Xenophon cut him short and said:
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.27">
 <s n="1133"/>“Man, I’m amazed at you! You have eyes to see, but you understand not; you have ears to hear, but you remember not!
 <s n="1134"/>You were there with the others, I think, when the king was so proud after the death of Cyros, and sent to demand our arms.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.28">
 <s n="1135"/>We refused to give them up, but went and encamped beside him. What didn’t he do! Sent ambassadors, asked for a truce, gave provisions till he got it!
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.29">
 <s n="1136"/>And when the captains and officers did what you tell us to do, and went for a conference without arms, trusting to this truce—where are they now?
 <s n="1137"/>Beaten, stabbed, insulted, the poor devils can’t even die, though that is what they long for! And you know all this, and you say those who urge resistance are talking nonsense, and you tell us to go there again and try persuasion!
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.30">
 <s n="1138"/>In my opinion, men, we ought not to let this man be one of us.
 <s n="1139"/>We ought to take his rank from him, and put a pack on his back, and make him carry for us. This man is a disgrace to his own country and to all Hellas, because he is a Hellene and yet behaves like this.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.31">
 <s n="1140"/>Then Agasias the Stymphalian broke in and said: “But this fellow has nothing to do at all with Boeotia or Hellas.
 <s n="1141"/>I have noticed that both his ears are bored, like a Lydian’s.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.32">
 <s n="1142"/>And so they were; accordingly, they drove him away.
 <s n="1143"/>The others went the rounds; wherever a captain was in some post they called him along, wherever there was none, the second-in-command, or where a lieutenant was left alive, the lieutenant.
 <s n="1144"/>When all were collected, they seated themselves in front of the place of arms.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.33">
 <s n="1145"/>They were about one hundred captains and other officers.
 <s n="1146"/>It was about midnight now.
 <s n="1147"/>Then Hieronymos of Elis, the eldest of Proxenos’s officers, began to speak.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.34">
 <s n="1148"/>He said: “Gentlemen, captains and officers of our army, seeing the state we are in, we thought it best to meet and to invite you, that we might devise some good plan amongst us.
 <s n="1149"/>I call on you, Xenophon, to speak as you did to us.”
 <s n="1150"/>Then Xenophon said: “Well, we all know that the king and Tissaphernes have seized all of us they could.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.35">
 <s n="1151"/>Then Xenophon said: “Well, we all know that the king and Tissaphernes have seized all of us they could.
 <s n="1152"/>And it is clear they are planning to destroy us if they can.
 <s n="1153"/>My opinion is that we ought to do anything and everything not to fall into the power of these barbarians, but turn the tables on them.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.36">
 <s n="1154"/>“Let me point out to you, then, that you now here assembled have a grand opportunity.
 <s n="1155"/>All these soldiers have their eyes on you. If they see you are discouraged, they will all be cowards; but if you show that you are making preparations against the enemy, and if you call on them, you may be sure they will follow you and try to imitate you.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.1.37">
 <s n="1156"/>“Perhaps it is fair to expect you to be a bit better than they are.
 <s n="1157"/>You are captains, you see, or you are in command of troops and companies, and while there was peace, you had more wealth and honor.
 <s n="1158"/>Then now when war has come, we must ask you to be better than the mob.
 <s n="1159"/>And to plan and labor for their behalf, if necessary.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.38">
 <s n="1160"/>“Now, first of all, I think you will do the whole army a great service if you take care at once to appoint captains and officers in the place of those who have been lost.
 <s n="1161"/>For it is true one may say universally that without commanders nothing good or useful could ever be done:
 <s n="1162"/> good discipline always saves, but disorder has destroyed many.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.39">
 <s n="1163"/>“When you have appointed as many commanders as are wanted, assemble all the other soldiers and encourage them; that will be just what they want now.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.40">
 <s n="1164"/>“Perhaps you have noticed yourselves how crestfallen they were when they came into camp.
 <s n="1165"/>How crestfallen they went on guard; in such a state, I don’t know what you could do with them, either by night or by day.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.41">
 <s n="1166"/>“But if someone could turn their minds from wondering what will happen to them, and make them wonder what they could do, they will be much more cheerful.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.42">
 <s n="1167"/>“You know, I am sure, that not numbers or strength brings victory in war; but whichever army goes into battle stronger in soul, their enemies generally cannot withstand them.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.1.43">
 <s n="1168"/>“I have noticed also myself, gentlemen, that when men seek for nothing in warfare but only life at all costs, they are generally the ones to die, and that with disgrace and ignominy; but when they recognize that all men must die, for this is their common lot, and strive only to die with honor, these I generally see growing to old age, and while they live, much happier.”
</section>




<section n="1" chapter="3.1.44">
 <s n="1169"/>“Learn this lesson yourselves, for now is the time we need it: be men yourselves, and encourage others to do the same.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.1.45">
 <s n="1170"/>With these words, he sat down;
  <s n="1171"/> and after him, Cheirisophos rose and said:
 <s n="1172"/>“All I knew of you before, my dear Xenophon, was just that I heard you were an Athenian.
 <s n="1173"/>But now I admire you for what you say and what you do, and I wish there were many more like you—that would be a public benefit.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.1.46">
 <s n="1174"/>“And now, gentlemen,” he went on, “don’t let us waste time; dismiss and choose commanders, those who need them.
 <s n="1175"/>Then come back here to the middle of our camp, and bring the selected men.
 <s n="1176"/>After that, we will summon all the troops to a meeting there. And we must have Tolmides the herald there too.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.1.47">
 <s n="1177"/>As he said this, he got up and went to do what was necessary without delay.
 <s n="1178"/>Then officers were chosen: in place of Clearchos, Timasion the Dardanian; in place of Socrates, Xanthicles the Achaian; in place of Agias, Cleanor the Arcadian; in place of Menon, Philesios the Achaian; in place of Proxenos, Xenophon the Athenian.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.1">
 <s n="1179"/>When these had been elected, it was almost dawn, and the commanders came forward.
 <s n="1180"/>They decided to set the watch and to convene the men.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.2">
 <s n="1181"/>“Soldiers, this is a dangerous time, when we have lost such captains and officers and men; and besides, our former allies, Ariaios and his men, have betrayed us."
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.3">
 <s n="1182"/>“Let us prove good men, and not give in, but try to save ourselves by a handsome victory if we can.
 <s n="1183"/>If not, at least let us die with honour, but let us never fall into our enemies’ hands alive.
 <s n="1184"/>For I think we should suffer such things as the gods would do to their enemies.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.4">
 <s n="1185"/>After him Cleanor the Orchomenian rose and said: “You see, men, the king’s perjury and impiety! You see the treachery of Tissaphernes!"
 <s n="1186"/>“He invited Clearchos to sit at his table, and then made this very act the means of destroying those men!”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.5">
 <s n="1187"/>“Ariaios again—we would have made him king, we gave and received pledges to be true to each other, but he did not fear God."
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.6">
 <s n="1188"/>“These men may the gods punish; but we, seeing this, must not be deceived by these men again."
 <s n="1189"/>“We must fight our strongest, and leave our fate in the hands of the gods.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.7">
 <s n="1190"/>Then Xenophon rose; he was arrayed for war in his finest dress.
 <s n="1191"/>“If the gods grant victory,” he thought, “the finest adornments are most proper for such a victory; if I must die, after grand ambitions I would meet my end in grandeur.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.8">
 <s n="1192"/>“The perjury and treachery of the barbarians Cleanor has spoken of, and I think you know it yourselves.
 <s n="1193"/>Then if we wish to be friends with them again, we must feel very much disheartened when we see what our captains have suffered when they trusted themselves to them in good faith.
 <s n="1194"/>But if we have in mind to punish them in arms for what they have done, and to be henceforth in war with them out and out, then with God’s help there are many good hopes of safety.”
</section>

<section n="7" chapter="3.2.9">
 <s n="1195"/>While he was speaking someone sneezed; the soldiers hearing this, kissed their hands to heaven.
 <s n="1196"/>Xenophon said: “While we were speaking of safety came an omen of Zeus the Saviour!
 <s n="1197"/>Then I think we should vow to this god a thanksgiving for salvation as soon as we reach the first friendly country, and vow a sacrifice to the other gods according to our ability.
 <s n="1198"/>Whoever agrees with this, let him hold up his hand.”
 <s n="1199"/>All held up their hands.
 <s n="1200"/>Then they made their vow and chanted the battle-hymn.
 <s n="1201"/>After this matter of piety was settled he began again.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.10">
 <s n="1202"/>“I am just saying that we have many good hopes of safety.
 <s n="1203"/>Firstly, we abide by our solemn oaths; but the enemy have perjured themselves and broken the truce contrary to their oaths.
 <s n="1204"/>It is reasonable, then, that the gods should be against our enemies and should be our own allies; and they are able quickly to make the great small, and easily to save the small in time of danger, if it be their pleasure.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.11">
 <s n="1205"/>“Next I will remind you of the dangers which our ancestors faced, and you will see that it is proper for you to be good men, and that good men are saved with the gods’ help even from great danger.”
 <s n="1206"/>“The Persians came, you know, with their enormous fleet, to wipe out Athens; but the Athenians dared to stand up to them and conquered them.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.12">
 <s n="1207"/>“They had vowed to Artemis to sacrifice as many goats as they should kill Persians; but they could not find enough, so they resolved to sacrifice every year five hundred, and they do so still.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.13">
 <s n="1208"/>“Again later, when Xerxes collected that innumerable host against Hellas, then again our ancestors conquered the ancestors of these men by land and sea.
 <s n="1209"/>We can see proofs of this in the trophies, but the greatest proof is the freedom of those cities in which you were born and brought up.
 <s n="1210"/>For you revere no man as master, but only the gods.”
</section>


<section n="3" chapter="3.2.14">
 <s n="1211"/>“Such were your ancestors.
 <s n="1212"/>I will not say indeed that you are not worthy of them.
 <s n="1213"/>No, not many days ago you faced these their descendants, many times your number, and conquered them.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.15">
 <s n="1214"/>“Then you did your part well, in order to make Cyros king.
 <s n="1215"/>Now that your own life and safety is at stake, I take it you ought to be much better and much more resolute.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.16">
 <s n="1216"/>“Moreover, you must be more confident against your enemy.
 <s n="1217"/>At that time you had no experience of them, yet you dared to attack them with the spirit of your fathers.
 <s n="1218"/>Now you have found by experience that they will not stand up to you even if they are many times your number. Then why should you fear them?”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.2.17">
 <s n="1219"/>“And pray consider it no great loss that Cyros’s men who stood by us once have left us.
 <s n="1220"/>For they are greater cowards than those whom you beat.
 <s n="1221"/>At least they took refuge with them when they left us.
 <s n="1222"/>If people want to be in the front ranks of a flight, I prefer to see them in our enemies’ ranks rather than in ours.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.18">
 <s n="1223"/>“If any of you is downhearted because we have no cavalry but the enemy have many, remember that their ten thousand cavalry are only ten thousand men.
 <s n="1224"/>No-one ever died in battle from the kick or the bite of a horse; the men make them do whatever they do in battle.”
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="3.2.19">
 <s n="1225"/>“In fact, we have a safer carrier than cavalry; 
  <s n="1226"/>there they hang on their horses fearing a fall as well as a foe, 
   <s n="1227"/>but we stand on the ground. We can hit harder if anyone attacks, and aim better.
 <s n="1228"/>The cavalry have indeed one advantage;
 <s n="1229"/> they can cut and run more safely!

</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.20">
 <s n="1230"/>“Perhaps you feel bold enough about battle, but you are anxious because Tissaphernes will not guide us any longer and the king will not provide a market. Think a moment whether it is better to have Tissaphernes for a guide, when he is clearly planning mischief against us, or any men we can catch to make them guide us; they know that if they do us wrong, their lives and bodies are at stake.”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.21">
 <s n="1231"/>“And provisions: is it better to buy from the market which those men provided, small measure for big price, when we have not even the price now; or to take it ourselves if we beat them, with any measure we like?”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.22">
 <s n="1232"/>“Perhaps you recognize we are better off as it is, but think the rivers an impassable obstacle; you consider you have been badly deceived when you crossed them.
 <s n="1233"/>But think whether this is not really the most foolish thing which the barbarians have done. All rivers are passable when you come near the source, hardly knee-deep, although they may be impassable far down.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.23">
 <s n="1234"/>“But assume that the rivers will not let us across and no guide can be found, even so we must not lose heart.
 <s n="1235"/>Look at the Mysians; we should not say they are better than we are, but we know there are many large and prosperous cities full of them, in the king’s country, without his leave.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.24">
 <s n="1236"/>“So with us: I think we ought not to show that we have set out for home, but make preparations to live here.
