 <s n="1237"/>I know quite well that the king would give plenty of guides to the Mysians, and hostages for honest dealing, and he would make roads for them even if they wished to depart in four-horse chariots.
 <s n="1238"/>Be sure he would think himself thrice blessed to do it for us, if he saw us preparing to settle down!”
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.25">
 <s n="1239"/>“But I am afraid that if we once learn to live idle in luxury, and to dally with the fine big women and girls of the Medes and Persians, we may be like the lotus-eaters and forget the way home!”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.26">
 <s n="1240"/>“I think, then, that we must first try to reach Hellas and our own people, and show the Hellenes that they are poor only because they want to be, when they could bring their paupers over here and see them rich.
 <s n="1241"/>But don’t forget, men, that all these good things belong to the conquerors!”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.2.27">
 <s n="1242"/>“And now it is necessary to say how we can travel most safely and fight most successfully.
 <s n="1243"/>First of all, then, I propose that we burn the wagons we have, that the baggage-train may not be our captain, and we may go where is best for the army.
 <s n="1244"/>Next, burn the tents too: 
 <s n="1245"/>for these are a nuisance to carry, they don’t help us to fight or to find provisions.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.28">
 <s n="1246"/>“Further, let us get rid of superfluous baggage, keeping only what we need for battle or eating or drinking; so the greatest number of us will be under arms, and as few as possible carrying baggage.
 <s n="1247"/>You know, of course, that if we are beaten, someone else owns all our goods; 
 
 <s n="1248"/>if we win, we may call the enemy our porters.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.29">
 <s n="1249"/>“It remains to say what I think is the chief thing of all.
 <s n="1250"/>You see that the enemy dared not make war upon us until they had seized our leaders.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.30">
 <s n="1251"/>“Very well: the commanders must be much more careful than before, and the commanded must be more obedient than before.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.2.31">
 <s n="1252"/>“And if anyone disobeys, we must vote that each and all of you must help the commander to punish.
 <s n="1253"/>So the enemy will find themselves mightily mistaken: for this day they will see ten thousand Clearchoses instead of one,
 <s n="1254"/>who will never permit anyone to be a coward.”
</section>

ZZZ

<section n="5" chapter="3.2.32">
 <s n="1255"/>“But now it is high time for me to finish; 
 <s n="1256"/>perhaps the enemy will be here soon.
 <s n="1257"/>Then let those who think I am right support me at once, that my proposals may be carried out.
 <s n="1258"/>Or if anyone has anything better, let him speak up boldly and say so, even if he is a private soldier, 
 <s n="1259"/>for our common safety is our common need.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.2.33">
 <s n="1260"/>After this Cheirisophos said, 
 <s n="1261"/>“If we need anything else, beside what Xenophon says, we can do it by and by;
 <s n="1262"/>but I think it best to carry these proposals at once.
 <s n="1263"/>Whoever agrees, let him hold up a hand.”
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="3.2.34">
 <s n="1264"/>All held up their hands.
 <s n="1265"/>Then Xenophon rose again
 <s n="1266"/> and said, “Men, listen to something else I have to propose.
 <s n="1267"/>It is clear that we must march where we can find provisions,
 <s n="1268"/>and I hear there are good villages not more than ten miles away.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.2.35">
 <s n="1269"/>Then we should not be surprised if the enemy follow us when we go, as cowardly dogs chase and bite those who pass, if they can, but run away if they pursue.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.2.36">
 <s n="1270"/>Then it is safe perhaps to march in a hollow square, with the armed men outside and the baggage and crowd of camp followers safer in the middle.
 <s n="1271"/>Suppose we decide who is to lead the square, and keep order in front, and who is to be on the flanks, and who to bring up the rear, we shall not have to consider when the enemy come, but we can just use the arrangements.
</section>





<section n="4" chapter="3.2.37">
 <s n="1272"/>If anyone sees anything better, let us do as he says: 
 <s n="1273"/>otherwise let Cheirisophos lead the van, since he is a Lacedaimonian,
 <s n="1274"/>and the two eldest captains see to the flanks;
 <s n="1275"/>while the youngest, I and Timasion, will take the rear for the present.
</section>

<section n="5" chapter="3.2.38">
 <s n="1276"/>We will just try this arrangement, and for the future we can do as may seem best.
 <s n="1277"/>If anyone has a better plan, let him speak.”
 <s n="1278"/>As no-one spoke, he said, 
 <s n="1279"/>“Those in favour, hold up a hand.”
 <s n="1280"/>All agreed.
</section>

<section n="7" chapter="3.2.39">
 <s n="1281"/>“Now then,” he said, “dismiss and do it as agreed.
 <s n="1282"/>And if any of you wants to see his dear ones again, let him remember to be a true man,
 <s n="1283"/>for that’s the only way to do it.
 <s n="1284"/>Whoever wants to live, let him try to conquer, 
 <s n="1285"/>for the conquerors kill and the conquered die.
 <s n="1286"/>If anyone wants riches, let him try to win; 
 <s n="1287"/>for conquerors preserve their own and take the goods of the conquered.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.3.1">
 <s n="1288"/>After these harangues, they went and burnt the wagons and tents; superfluous things they gave to any who wanted them, and threw the rest into the fire.
 <s n="1289"/>This done, they took breakfast.
 <s n="1290"/>While they were at breakfast, up came Mithradates with about thirty horsemen, and, calling the captains within hearing, he spoke as follows:
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.3.2">
 <s n="1291"/>“I was faithful to Cyros, as you know, Hellenes, and now I am friendly to you; indeed I spend my days here in great fear.
 <s n="1292"/>So if I saw that you had some profitable plan for safety,
 <s n="1293"/>I would join you with all my servants.
 <s n="1294"/>Tell me, then, what you have in mind, and regard me as a friend and well-wisher, willing to make my journey along with you.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.3.3">
 <s n="1295"/>The captains consulted, and decided to answer this, 
 <s n="1296"/>with Cheirisophos as spokesman:
 <s n="1297"/>“We are resolved to pass through the country doing as little damage as possible, if we are left alone;
 <s n="1298"/>but if anyone tries to hinder our passage, we will fight him as stoutly as we can.”
</section>




<section n="3" chapter="3.3.4">
 <s n="1299"/>After this Mithradates tried to show them that it was impossible to save their lives except with the king’s consent.
 <s n="1300"/>Then it became clear that he was a spy; 
 <s n="1301"/>for one of the relations of Tissaphernes was there to keep him loyal.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.5">
 <s n="1302"/>After this the captains thought it best to proclaim war without parley for as long as they were in the enemy country;
 <s n="1303"/>for they used to come and try to corrupt the soldiers, and they did corrupt one officer, Nicarchos the Arcadian, who deserted at night with about twenty men.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.6">
 <s n="1304"/>After this, they breakfasted and crossed the Zapatas River;
 <s n="1305"/>then began their march in order, with baggage and mob in the middle. They had not gone very far when Mithradates appeared again with some two hundred horsemen and bowmen and slingers, four hundred or so, very light and trim.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.3.7">
 <s n="1306"/>He came towards them in friendly fashion, 
 <s n="1307"/>but when they were near suddenly there were arrows from both horse and foot, and the slingers too wounded many.
 <s n="1308"/>The Hellenic rearguard suffered severely but could do nothing in return:
 <s n="1309"/>for the range of the Cretan bowmen was less than the Persian, and besides being bare they had to be inside the defenses, and the slingers were beyond the range of their javelins.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.3.8">
 <s n="1310"/>So Xenophon ordered pursuit, 
 <s n="1311"/>and the men-at-arms and targeteers of his rearguard did pursue,
 <s n="1312"/>but they caught none of the enemy.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.9">
 <s n="1313"/>For the Hellenes had no horsemen, and the footmen could not catch the enemy footmen with a long start in a short run;
 <s n="1314"/>of course they could not go far from the army,
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.3.10">
 <s n="1315"/>and the barbarian horsemen shot arrows behind them as they rode and hit them, besides they had to come back all that way fighting.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.3.11">
 <s n="1316"/>The result was that they made no more than three miles or so in all that day,
 <s n="1317"/>but towards evening they reached the villages.
 <s n="1318"/>Then again there was great discouragement. Cheirisophos and the older captains blamed Xenophon because he left the lines to pursue, and risked himself but could do no harm to the enemy.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.12">
 <s n="1319"/>Xenophon listened, and said they were quite right in blaming him, as the result had showed.
 <s n="1320"/>“But I was compelled to pursue,” he said, “when I saw that we suffered so badly by standing still and we could do nothing in return.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.13">
 <s n="1321"/>When we did pursue, what you say is the truth,” says he;
 <s n="1322"/>“we could do them no damage, and got back with great difficulty.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.3.14">
 <s n="1323"/>Thank God they had only a small force, so they could not hurt us seriously, but they showed us what we want.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.15">
 <s n="1324"/>Their arrows and slings have such a range that the Cretans cannot reach them, nor those who throw by hand;
 <s n="1325"/>and when we pursue, it is impossible to go far from our army, and in a short space not even a fast runner could catch a runner with a bowshot start.
</section>


<section n="3" chapter="3.3.16">
 <s n="1326"/>Well, then, if we are to keep them from damaging us on the march, we must find slingers and horsemen at once.
 <s n="1327"/>I hear there are Rhodians in the army, and most of them know how to sling, as they say, and their shot has twice the range of the Persians.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.3.17">
 <s n="1328"/>The Persians use a stone as big as your fist, and so they cannot throw far, but the Rhodians know how to use leaden bullets.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.3.18">
 <s n="1329"/>If we can discover, then, which of them possess a sling and pay them extra for this, and pay for plaiting more slings, and invent some other privilege for those willing to be posted as slingers, perhaps we shall find some useful men.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.19">
 <s n="1330"/>I see also that there are horses with us; I have some myself, some were left by Clearchos, many others which we captured are carrying baggage.
 <s n="1331"/>If we take the pick of these, and put other animals to carry, we can use the horses for cavalry, and perhaps these will do damage in the pursuit.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.3.20">
 <s n="1332"/>This was agreed to; 
 <s n="1333"/> and that night about 200 slingers were found up, and the next day some 50 horses and horsemen were passed as fitting, and buff jackets and corselets were provided for them, and Lycios the Athenian, Polystratos’ son, was put in command of the horse.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.1">
 <s n="1334"/>They stayed there that day, and on the next began the march earlier than usual,
 <s n="1335"/> for they had to pass through a ravine where they feared the enemy might attack them in the pass.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.2">
 <s n="1336"/>When they had passed, Mithradates appeared again with 1,000 cavalry and some 4,000 bowmen and slingers.
 <s n="1337"/>He had demanded so many from Tissaphernes, and promised that if he got them he would deliver the Hellenes into his hand: he despised them because in the former attack with a small force he had done much damage, as he thought, and suffered nothing at all.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.3">
 <s n="1338"/>When the Hellenes had gone about a mile from the pass, Mithradates came through with his force.
 <s n="1339"/>Orders had been given which of the targeteers and men-at-arms were to pursue, and the horsemen were told to pursue boldly and a sufficient force would follow.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.4">
 <s n="1340"/>So when Mithradates caught them up, and arrows and slingstones began to come, a trumpet sounded for the Hellenes, and the appointed parties doubled out at once and the horsemen galloped; the enemy did not wait for them but ran back into the ravine.
 <s n="1341"/>In this pursuit, many of the barbarians were killed, and some of the cavalry were taken alive, about eighteen.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.5">
 <s n="1342"/>The dead bodies were mutilated by the Hellenes unbidden, to strike terror into the enemy.
 <s n="1343"/>They only imitated the Persian custom.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.6">
 <s n="1344"/>But the enemy retired after this, and the Hellenes marched unmolested the rest of the day as far as the River Tigris.
</section>

<section n="6" chapter="3.4.7">
 <s n="1345"/>A large deserted city was there, named Larisa, 
 <s n="1346"/>which had once been inhabited by Medes.
 <s n="1347"/>The wall of this place was five-and-twenty feet thick, and one hundred high, 
 <s n="1348"/>the whole being two leagues round.
 <s n="1349"/>It was built of clay bricks, 
 <s n="1350"/>on a stone base twenty feet high.
</section>


<section n="2" chapter="3.4.8">
 <s n="1351"/>When the Persians took the empire from the Medes the Persian king besieged this place and could not take it in any way; 
 <s n="1352"/>then a cloud covered the sun and hid it until the people escaped, and so it was taken.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.9">
 <s n="1353"/>Near this city was a stone pyramid, one hundred feet wide and two hundred high.
 <s n="1354"/>On this pyramid were many of the natives who had escaped from the villages near.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.4.10">
 <s n="1355"/>From this place they marched one stage, 6 leagues, to a large deserted fortress named Mespila,
 <s n="1356"/>formerly inhabited by Medes.
 <s n="1357"/>The foundation was made of polished stone full of shells, fifty feet wide and fifty high.
 <s n="1358"/>The circuit was 6 leagues.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.11">
 <s n="1359"/>The foundation was made of polished stone full of shells, fifty feet wide and fifty high.
 <s n="1360"/>The circuit was 6 leagues.
 <s n="1361"/>There the king’s wife Medeia, is said to have taken refuge when the Medes lost their empire to the Persians.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.12">
 <s n="1362"/>The Persian king besieged this place, but could not take it either by time or by force; 
 <s n="1363"/>but Zeus affrighted the inhabitants by thunder, and so it was taken.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.13">
 <s n="1364"/>From this place they marched one stage, 4 leagues.
 <s n="1365"/>On this stage Tissaphernes appeared, with his own cavalry and the forces of Orontas, who married the king’s daughter, and the nationals whom Cyros had brought up country with him, and those whom the king’s brothers had brought to support the king; it so appeared to be an immense army.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.14">
 <s n="1366"/>When he was near he stationed some ranks in the rear, and led others along the flanks, but he dared not attack and he wished to run no risk, only gave orders to shoot with bow and sling.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.15">
 <s n="1367"/>But the Rhodians posted here and there slung their bullets and the bowmen shot their arrows, and not one missed a man (he would have found it hard to miss if he had tried); so Tissaphernes quickly retreated out of range, and the other ranks retreated too.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.16">
 <s n="1368"/>For the rest of the day they went on marching,
 <s n="1369"/> and the barbarians did no harm by their sharpshooting; 
 <s n="1370"/>for the Rhodians had longer range than the Persians, even the bowmen.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.17">
 <s n="1371"/>The Persian bows are very large;
 <s n="1372"/> so the arrows they picked up were useful to the Cretans, who practised with them, shooting high and as far as possible.
 <s n="1373"/>Plenty of gut was found in the villages, and lead, which they used for the slings.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.4.18">
 <s n="1374"/>So on this day, after the Hellenes had encamped in villages which they found, the barbarians had the worst of that skirmish, and retreated.
 <s n="1375"/>The day following the Hellenes spent in the villages collecting provisions,
 <s n="1376"/> for there was plenty of corn there.
 <s n="1377"/>Next day they marched on over the plain, and Tissaphernes followed, skirmishing.
</section>


<section n="2" chapter="3.4.19">
 <s n="1378"/>Now the Hellenes discovered that the square was a bad formation with the enemy following.
 <s n="1379"/>If the wings of the square draw together when the road is narrow, or where the hills make it necessary, or a bridge, the armed men must be greatly distressed and march badly; they are squeezed up and disordered at the same time, so they are not easy to manage because they lose discipline.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.20">
 <s n="1380"/>Again, when the wings open again after their late distress, the ranks must be separated again, and there are empty spaces between, and all are discouraged with an enemy behind.
 <s n="1381"/>Or if they have to cross a bridge or some other crossing, each man tries to get over first, 
 <s n="1382"/>and then it is easy for the enemy to attack.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.21">
 <s n="1383"/>When the commanders perceived this they made six companies of a hundred men each, and appointed a lieutenant to command each, and officers for each half-company and quarter-company.
 <s n="1384"/>When the wings drew in on the march these companies fell to the rear so as not to confuse the wings, and they lined up outside the wings.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.22">
 <s n="1385"/>When the wings expanded again they filled up the gap in the line between, in line of companies in column if narrow, half-companies if wider, quarter-companies very wide, 
 <s n="1386"/>and so the gap was always filled.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.23">
 <s n="1387"/>Thus they were not disordered if they had to cross a ford or a bridge, but the companies crossed in turn; 
 <s n="1388"/>and when they needed the line these were ready.
 <s n="1389"/>In this way they marched four stages.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.24">
 <s n="1390"/>While they were in the fifth they saw a palace and many villages round it, but the road to this led amongst high foothills rolling down from the mountains under which the village lay.
 <s n="1391"/>The Hellenes were glad indeed to see these mounds, which was natural when the enemy were cavalry.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.25">
 <s n="1392"/>By and by they left the plain and went up and down the first mound; then as they were about to go up the second there were the barbarians volleying down the slope from the top javelins, slingstones, and arrows, and the masters could be seen flogging on the men!
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.26">
 <s n="1393"/>They wounded many, and defeated the Hellenic light-armed men, and shut them up in the square, so that slingers and bowmen were both quite useless among the mob all that day.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.27">
 <s n="1394"/>So the Hellenes had a hard time of it; but when they tried to pursue they could scarcely get to the top in their heavy arms, but the enemy quickly leaped away.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.28">
 <s n="1395"/>When they retired to the main army the same things happened; and on the second mound the same again, so that they decided not to move the soldiers from the third mound until they had sent a party of light infantry from the right wing up the mountains.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.29">
 <s n="1396"/>As soon as these were posted above the pursuing enemy, the enemy no longer attacked the descending troops for fear they should be cut off and find enemies on both sides.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.30">
 <s n="1397"/>So they proceeded for the rest of the day, the army on the road moving over the foot-hills, the others going parallel on the mountain-side, until they reached the villages.
 <s n="1398"/>They put eight surgeons in charge, 
 <s n="1399"/>for the wounded were many.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.31">
 <s n="1400"/>They stayed there three days, partly for the sake of the wounded, partly because there was plenty of food, wheatmeal, wine, and barley collected for horses in great quantity.
 <s n="1401"/>These had been stored up for the regent of the country.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.32">
 <s n="1402"/>On the fourth day they came down into the plain.
 <s n="1403"/>When they came upon Tissaphernes with his forces, experience taught them to encamp at the first village they saw and not to march fighting all the while;
 <s n="1404"/>for there were large numbers unable to fight, the wounded men and their bearers, and those who carried the arms of the bearers.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.33">
 <s n="1405"/>After they had encamped, when the barbarians began to attack them by their long shots, the Hellenes had much the best of it; for there was a great difference between defending a fixed position and making a running fight on the march.
 <s n="1406"/>There was a great difference between defending a fixed position and making a running fight on the march.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.34">
 <s n="1407"/>When late afternoon came it was time for the enemy to depart; 
 <s n="1408"/>for they never encamped at a less distance than seven or eight miles, for fear that they might be attacked in the night
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.4.35">
 <s n="1409"/>The Persian army is good for nothing at night.
 <s n="1410"/>Their horses are tied up, and generally hobbled as well, that they may not run away if cast loose.
 <s n="1411"/>If there is any disturbance, the Persian has to saddle and bridle his horse, and put on his corselet, and then mount—all difficult things at night when there is a disturbance too.
 <s n="1412"/>That is why they used to encamp far away from the Hellenes.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.36">
 <s n="1413"/>When our men noticed that they were about to go and the word went round, the trumpet sounded to pack up.
 <s n="1414"/>The enemy heard it, and for some time they did not set out.
 <s n="1415"/>When it was late, they went, since they thought it did not pay to travel at night and come into camp.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.37">
 <s n="1416"/>As soon as the Hellenes saw they were really off, they also broke up camp and marched, and did as much as seven miles.
 <s n="1417"/>The distance between the two armies was so great that next day the enemy did not appear at all, nor the day after. On the fourth day the barbarians had gone ahead by a night march and they seized a high position above the road by which the Hellenes had to go, a mountain spur on the right, under which was the descent into the plain.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.38">
 <s n="1418"/>When Cheirisophos noticed that the hill had been seized he called Xenophon from the rear, and ordered him to come up in front with the targeteers.
</section>

<section n="6" chapter="3.4.39">
 <s n="1419"/>But Xenophon did not bring them, 
 <s n="1420"/>for he saw Tissaphernes appear with his whole army.
 <s n="1421"/>He rode up by himself and asked, “What is it?”
 <s n="1422"/>The other said, 
 <s n="1423"/>“You can see; 
	<s n="1424"/>the hill above the descent has been seized, and we cannot pass unless we dislodge them.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.40">
 <s n="1425"/>“Why didn’t you bring the targeteers?”
 <s n="1426"/>He replied that he did not think it safe to leave the rear undefended when the enemy were in sight there.
 <s n="1427"/>“Well, at least,” said Cheirisophos, “it’s high time to consider how we can drive the fellows from that hill.”
</section>




<section n="5" chapter="3.4.41">
 <s n="1428"/>Then Xenophon looked up at the head of the hill above their army, and saw an approach from this to the peak where the enemy were, and he said, 
  <s n="1429"/>“The best thing for us, Cheirisophos, is to go for that height at once.
 <s n="1430"/>If we seize it the men over the road can’t hold their position.
 <s n="1431"/>You stay with the army, if you wish, 
  <s n="1432"/>and I shall be glad to try that; or you go, and I'll stay.”

</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.42">
 <s n="1433"/>“Take your choice,” said Cheirisophos, “you have my leave.”
 <s n="1434"/>Xenophon chose to go. “I’m the younger,” he said, but he asked for a detachment from the van, 
 <s n="1435"/>because it was a long way to bring men from the rear.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.43">
 <s n="1436"/>So Cheirisophos let him have the light infantry from the van, and took instead those from the centre.
 <s n="1437"/>He gave him also the 300 picked men that he had himself in the van.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.44">
 <s n="1438"/>As soon as the enemy on the hill perceived their march upon the height, they began to race for this same place.
 <s n="1439"/>They set off at full speed.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.4.45">
 <s n="1440"/>Then there was a rare din, the Hellenic army cheering on their men, and the army of Tissaphernes cheering theirs.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.4.46">
 <s n="1441"/>Xenophon rode by on his horse, shouting, “Men, remember this is a race to Hellas!
 <s n="1442"/>To your wives and children! One little effort now, and we shall do the rest of our journey without a blow!”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.47">
 <s n="1443"/>Soteridas the Sicyonian shouted, 
  <s n="1444"/>“It isn’t fair, Xenophon!
 <s n="1445"/>You ride on a horse, I’m tired out carrying this shield!”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.4.48">
 <s n="1446"/>Xenophon, hearing this, jumped off the horse, and pushed the man out of his place.
 <s n="1447"/>He took the shield from him and went as fast as he could.
 <s n="1448"/>He had his corselet on, too, as it happened, so it was misery for him.
 <s n="1449"/>“Look sharp!” he called to the front, “Hurry up!” to the rear, and he could hardly keep up himself.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.4.49">
 <s n="1450"/>The other men whacked Soteridas, and pelted him, and gave him plenty of hard words.
 <s n="1451"/>They made him take back his shield and march too.
 <s n="1452"/>Xenophon mounted and rode on again, and led them as long as the path allowed, but when it became too rough he left the horse and made all haste on foot.
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.5.1">
 <s n="1453"/>Then the barbarians turned and fled where they could, and the Hellenes occupied the height.
 <s n="1454"/>The army of Tissaphernes and Ariaios turned away and went by another road.
 <s n="1455"/>Cheirisophos came down and encamped in a village full of good things.
 <s n="1456"/>There were many other villages full of good things along the River Tigris.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.2">
 <s n="1457"/>In the late afternoon suddenly the enemy appeared on the plain, and they cut down some Hellenes who were scattered about the plain plundering.
 <s n="1458"/>Indeed, many herds of animals had been caught while trying to cross the river.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.3">
 <s n="1459"/>There Tissaphernes’ men began to burn the villages.
 <s n="1460"/>Many of the Hellenes were downhearted for fear they might find no places to get provisions if the burning went on.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.4">
 <s n="1461"/>Cheirisophos had been to the rescue, 
 <s n="1462"/>and he was returning when Xenophon came riding down past the lines and called out as he met them:
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.5.5">
 <s n="1463"/>“There, men of Hellas! Do you see they admit that the country is ours already?
 <s n="1464"/>They have done what they made us promise not to do in their treaty! We were not to burn the king’s country, and now they are burning it like a foreign country!
 <s n="1465"/>Well, if they have provisions for themselves, they will see us going there too.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.5.6">
 <s n="1466"/>“Come, Cheirisophos,” says he, “I think we ought to keep them from burning our country.”
 <s n="1467"/>Cheirisophos answered, 
 <s n="1468"/>“No, I don’t.
 <s n="1469"/>Let’s burn, too,” says he, “and so they will stop sooner.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.5.7">
 <s n="1470"/>When they reached the camp most were busy about the provisions, but the captains and officers met.
 <s n="1471"/>All was despondency now.
 <s n="1472"/>On one side were high mountains, on one side a river so deep that even the spears could not sound it.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.8">
 <s n="1473"/>In this unhappy state a Rhodian man came up and said, 
 <s n="1474"/>“I am ready, sirs, to take you across four thousand men at a time, if you provide me with what I want and pay me one talent.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.9">
 <s n="1475"/>“What do you want?” they asked. “Skins I want,” says he, “two thousand skins.
 <s n="1476"/>I see plenty of sheep and goats and cattle and asses. Flay them and blow up the skins and they will easily give us a crossing.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.10">
 <s n="1477"/>“I will ask you too for the straps you put round the baggage animals.
 <s n="1478"/>I fasten the skins together with these, I moor each one by tying stones to it and letting them down into the water like anchors, I carry them across by the current in a line and make fast the two ends, I throw wood on the top and earth over that.”
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.11">
 <s n="1479"/>“They will not sink, you will see that in a minute: every skin will hold up two men.
 <s n="1480"/>They won’t slip;
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.5.12">
 <s n="1481"/>the wood and earth will save them.”
 <s n="1482"/>The captains thought this a pretty notion, but impossible to carry out,
 <s n="1483"/>for there were numbers of cavalry on the other side to stop them; they would be down on the first lot and would not let them do anything at all.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.13">
 <s n="1484"/>Then the next day they retreated again to the unburnt villages, after burning those they left.
 <s n="1485"/>So the enemy did not ride after them, but watched them, and seemed to be wondering where they would turn now and what they meant to do.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="3.5.14">
 <s n="1486"/>Then most were busy about the provisions, 
 <s n="1487"/> but the captains met again, and collecting their captives questioned them about the country round about, what each district was.

</section>

<section n="1" chapter="3.5.15">
 <s n="1488"/>They said that the southern parts lay on the road towards Babylon and Media, by which indeed they had come. Towards the east it led to Susa and Ecbatana, where the king is said to spend the summer. Across the river to the west it led to Lydia, to Ionia; the road through the mountains and to the north led to the Kurds.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.5.16">
 <s n="1489"/>These they said lived in the mountains and they were warlike; they did not obey the king.
 <s n="1490"/>Once a royal army had invaded them, 112,000 strong, and not a man came back because of the difficulties of the country.
 <s n="1491"/>But when there was truce between them and the governor in the plain, “we mix with them,” says he, “and they mix with us.”
</section>

<section n="4" chapter="3.5.17">
 <s n="1492"/>After hearing this, the captains seated together those who claimed to know about each part, without revealing which way they meant to go.
 <s n="1493"/>At last they decided that they were obliged to cross the mountains and get into the country of the Kurds.
 <s n="1494"/>For the men said that when they had passed through this they would reach Armenia, a large and rich country which was the province of Orontas.
 <s n="1495"/>Thence they said it would be easy to go wherever they wished.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="3.5.18">
 <s n="1496"/>Upon this they offered sacrifice, so as to set out whenever it should seem to be the right time.
 <s n="1497"/>For they feared that the pass over the mountains might have been occupied already.
 <s n="1498"/>So they sent round the word that after dinner all should pack up and rest, and follow when the command was given.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="4.1.4">

<section n="4.1.5" sentences="1">
 <s n="1504"/>It was about the last watch, and enough of the night was left for them to pass over the plain in darkness; then the word was sent round, and they rose up and marched, and by daylight they had reached the mountains.
</section>

<section n="4.1.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="1505"/>Cheirisophos was leading the army, with all the light-armed troops round him, and Xenophon with men-at-arms of the rearguard followed without any light troops;
 <s n="1506"/>for there seemed no danger that anyone would follow on the rear of an army going up.
</section>

<section n="4.1.7" sentences="3">
 <s n="1507"/>Cheirisophos reached the summit without seeing any of the enemy;
 <s n="1508"/>then he led on,
 <s n="1509"/>and each wave of the army as it rolled up followed into the villages which lie in the dells and corners of the hills.
</section>

<section n="4.1.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="1510"/>By this time all the Kurds had left their houses, with wives and children, and fled into the mountains;
 <s n="1511"/>but plenty of provisions were to be had, and the houses were provided with huge numbers of brass pots. But the Hellenes took none of them, nor did they pursue the people. They spared them in the hope that the Kurds might let them pass through the country as friends, since they were enemies to the king;
</section>

<section n="4.1.9" sentences="3">
 <s n="1512"/>Only they took all the provisions they found, for they had to do it.
 <s n="1513"/>But the Kurds would not listen to any call,
 <s n="1514"/>or do any other friendly act.
</section>

<section n="4.1.10" sentences="2">
 <s n="1515"/>When the last of the Hellenes had come down from the summit into the villages, it was already dark; so narrow was the road that the ascent and descent had taken the whole day.
 <s n="1516"/>Then some of the Kurds gathered and attacked the last lot, and killed some and wounded others with stones and arrows. They were only a few, for the Hellenic invasion had come as a surprise.
</section>

<section n="4.1.11" sentences="3">
 <s n="1517"/>But if there had been more, there was danger that a large part of the army would have been lost.
 <s n="1518"/>That night they spent in the open among the villages;
 <s n="1519"/>but the Kurds lit numbers of fires on the mountains all round in sight of each other.
</section>

<section n="4.1.12" sentences="1">
 <s n="1520"/>At dawn the captains and officers of the Hellenes met and decided to take with them only the strongest of their baggage-animals, and no more than were necessary; to leave all the rest, and to let go all captives lately taken that were in the army.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="4.1.13">
 <s n="1521"/>Many animals and captives made the pace slow, and many of those in charge of them were out of the fight, and with all these men twice as much provision had to be found and carried.
 <s n="1522"/>Heralds were sent round to cry these orders.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="4.1.14">
 <s n="1523"/>When breakfast was over they marched on; but the captains posted men beside the road at a narrow place: if they found any of the aforesaid things not left behind they took possession. The men mostly obeyed, but some did smuggle away a good-looking boy or woman. 
 <s n="1524"/>They marched so far that day, sometimes fighting and sometimes resting.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="4.1.15">
 <s n="1525"/>But next day came a heavy storm, yet they must go on
 <s n="1526"/> for want of provisions.
 <s n="1527"/>Cheirisophos led the way, Xenophon guarded the rear.
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="4.1.16">
 <s n="1528"/>The enemy attacked them vigorously, and in those narrow places they came close and shot arrows and slingstones.
 <s n="1529"/>So the Hellenes were compelled to go slow what with chasing and coming back.
 <s n="1530"/>Often Xenophon sent word for a halt, when the enemy attacked vigorously.
</section>

<section n="4.1.17" sentences="3">
 <s n="1531"/>Sometimes Cheirisophos halted when the word came, but the last time he would not halt—
 <s n="1532"/>he went on quickly and sent word to follow.
 <s n="1533"/>So it became very much like a flight for the rearguard.
</section>

<section n="1" chapter="4.1.18">
 <s n="1534"/>There died a good man, Leonymos, a Laconian, shot with an arrow through shield and corselet into the ribs, and Basias, an Arcadian, right through the head.
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="4.1.19">
 <s n="1535"/>As soon as they reached a halting-place, Xenophon went as he was straight to Cheirisophos, and reproached him: “Why didn’t you halt? We were forced to fly and fight at the same time.
 <s n="1536"/>And now two fine fellows are dead, and we could not take them up and bury them!”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="4.1.20">
 <s n="1537"/>Cheirisophos answered, “Look at the mountains!” says he. “See! no way up anywhere:
 <s n="1538"/> this one way you see steep too, and you can see all that mob.
 <s n="1539"/>They have occupied the pass and they won’t let us get out!
</section>

<section n="2" chapter="4.1.21">
 <s n="1540"/>That’s why I made such a hurry and would not halt. I hoped to get there first and seize the pass
 <s n="1541"/>, and the guides we have say there is no other way.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="4.1.22">
 <s n="1542"/>Xenophon said,
 <s n="1543"/> “Well, I have two captives.
 <s n="1544"/> Those men were so troublesome that I laid ambush, which gave us a minute to breathe also; we killed a good few and took care to take some alive just in order to be guides, because they know the country.”
</section>

<section n="3" chapter="4.1.23">
 <s n="1545"/>Then he brought up the men, and questioned them separately, whether they knew any other road than the one they could see.
 <s n="1546"/>One said no, although many terrors were applied; 
	<s n="1547"/>and since he had nothing useful to say, he was killed in sight of the other.
</section>

<section n="4.1.24" sentences="2">
 <s n="1548"/>The second said that the first said he knew no way because a daughter of his was married to a man over there;
 <s n="1549"/>but he would take them by a road where even animals could go.
</section>

<section n="4.1.25" sentences="1">
 <s n="1550"/>Asked if there were any place ahead difficult to pass, he said there was a height which must be occupied first or nothing could get by.
</section>

<section n="4.1.26" sentences="1">
 <s n="1551"/>Then it was decided to call the officers both of heavy and light infantry, to tell them all this and to ask if anyone were man enough to volunteer.
</section>

<section n="4.1.27" sentences="2">
 <s n="1552"/>From the heavy-armed two Arcadians came forward, Aristonymos, a Methydrian, and Agasias, a Stymphalian, while a third in rivalry, Callimachos, a Parrhasian, offered to lead volunteers from the whole army;
 <s n="1553"/>“for I know,” says he, “many young men will follow if I lead.”
</section>

<section n="4.1.28" sentences="2">
 <s n="1554"/>Then they asked if any of the light-armed officers would join?
 <s n="1555"/>Aristeas, a Chian, came forward, one who was often useful to the army for enterprises of this kind.
</section>

<section n="4.2.1" sentences="3">
 <s n="1556"/>It was now late afternoon, and they ordered them to take a bite and go.
 <s n="1557"/>They tied up the guide and handed him over to these; they arranged that if they could occupy the height, they were to guard it that night and sound the trumpet at dawn.
 <s n="1558"/>Then the party on the height were to attack the enemy who held the pass, and they would themselves come out with succour as soon as they could.
</section>

<section n="4.2.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="1559"/>With this arrangement the first party marched, about two thousand, in torrents of rain.
 <s n="1560"/>Xenophon led his rearguard towards the visible pass, to draw the enemy’s attention and to hide the others if possible as they moved round.
 <s n="1561"/>Xenophon led his rearguard towards the visible pass, to draw the enemy’s attention and to hide the others if possible as they moved round.
</section>

<section n="4.2.3" sentences="2">
 <s n="1562"/>The rearguard soon reached a ravine which they must traverse before they came to the steep part; and then the barbarians rolled down round stones of all sizes, some as big as a wagon-load, which hit the rocks and splintered in all directions like slingstones—it was absolutely impossible to go near the entrance.
 <s n="1563"/>The rearguard soon reached a ravine which they must traverse before they came to the steep part; and then the barbarians rolled down round stones of all sizes, some as big as a wagon-load, which hit the rocks and splintered in all directions like slingstones—it was absolutely impossible to go near the entrance.
</section>

<section n="4.2.4" sentences="6">
 <s n="1564"/>Some officers baulked in this place tried others, 
 <s n="1565"/>and this went on till it was dark;
 <s n="1566"/>when they thought they could not be seen, they went away for supper,
 <s n="1567"/>and some of the rearguard had not even had breakfast.
 <s n="1568"/>But the enemy did not cease rolling the stones all night,
 <s n="1569"/>as they could hear by the noise.
</section>


<section n="4.2.5" sentences="2">
 <s n="1570"/>The party with the guide went round and caught the enemy outpost sitting round the fire;
 <s n="1571"/>these they killed or chased away, and stayed there themselves, thinking they now held the height.
</section>

<section n="4.2.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="1572"/>But they did not hold it, for where the guards had been sitting there was a round hill above, along which ran the narrow path.
 <s n="1573"/>However, there was an approach from this place to the enemy who were sitting on the visible road.
</section>

<section n="4.2.7" sentences="6">
 <s n="1574"/>Thus they spent the night; 
 <s n="1575"/>and when daylight came they formed up and moved in silence towards the enemy.
 <s n="1576"/>There was a mist, so they were able to come near without being seen.
 <s n="1577"/>When they caught sight of each other, the trumpet sounded, and they rushed on the fellows cheering;
 <s n="1578"/>the enemy did not stand, but left the road and fled;
 <s n="1579"/>few were killed, for they went light.
</section>

<section n="4.2.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="1580"/>Cheirisophos and his men heard the trumpet, and charged straight up by the visible road;
 <s n="1581"/>other captains pushed along by pathless ways wherever they happened to be, getting up as they could and hoisting each other by their spears.
</section>

<section n="4.2.9" sentences="4">
 <s n="1582"/>These were the first to meet the men who had occupied the height.
 <s n="1583"/>Xenophon with half the rearguard marched by the road which the party with the guide had taken,
 <s n="1584"/> for this was quite easy for baggage-animals;
 <s n="1585"/>the other half he posted behind the animals.
</section>

<section n="4.2.10" sentences="2">
 <s n="1586"/>On the way they came to a hill above the road with enemy in possession, whom it was necessary either to cut down or else to be separated themselves from the other Hellenes.
 <s n="1587"/>The men could have gone on above as the others had done, but the animals could not pass by any other way.
</section>

<section n="4.2.11" sentences="1">
 <s n="1588"/>Here, shouting encouragement to each other, they charged straight up the front in company columns; they did not surround it but left a way for the enemy to escape if they wished.
</section>

<section n="4.2.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="1589"/>For a while the savages fought them off as well as they could, with arrows and spears, but they would not face them and ran away from the place.
 <s n="1590"/>However, no sooner had they passed this hill than they saw another in front occupied, and determined to attack this again.
</section>

<section n="4.2.13" sentences="1">
 <s n="1591"/>But Xenophon saw that if he left the captured hill, the enemy might take it again and set on the baggage-train as it passed, and the train was long in that narrow path; so he left in charge captains Cephisodoros Cephisophon’s son, an Athenian, and Amphicrates Amphidemos’ son, an Athenian, and Archagoras, an exile from Argos, and himself marched with the others to the second hill and took it in the same way.
</section>

<section n="4.2.14" sentences="1">
 <s n="1592"/>After this a third hill remained, the steepest of them all, the one where that outpost had been taken in the night by the volunteers.
</section>

<section n="4.2.15" sentences="2">
 <s n="1593"/>But as soon as they came near, the savages left the hill without a blow, which astonished them all and made them suspect they had left it for fear of being encircled and besieged.
 <s n="1594"/>But the truth was they saw from the height what was happening behind, and all moved upon the rearguard.
</section>

<section n="4.2.16" sentences="1">
 <s n="1595"/>Now Xenophon with the youngest men climbed the hill, and told the others to go on, that the last companies might come up with them, until they could ground arms on the level beside the road.
</section>

<section n="4.2.17" sentences="1">
 <s n="1596"/>At this moment Archagoras arrived. He was in flight and said that they had been dislodged from the first hill, and that Cephisodoros and Amphicrates were dead, with all the others who had not jumped down from the rocks and got back to the rearguard.
</section>

<section n="4.2.18" sentences="2">
 <s n="1597"/>After this feat, the natives came to a ridge opposite to the hill,
 <s n="1598"/>and Xenophon spoke to them through an interpreter about a truce, and asked for the dead.
</section>

<section n="4.2.19" sentences="4">
 <s n="1599"/>They promised to give these back on condition that they would not burn the houses.
 <s n="1600"/>Xenophon agreed to this.
 <s n="1601"/>While they were parleying and the rest of the army was passing, all the people of the place had been collecting;
</section>

<section n="4.2.20" sentences="3">
 <s n="1603"/>and as soon as they began to descend from the hill to join the others where they were halted, the enemy charged at once in great force with loud shouts.
 <s n="1604"/>When they were on the top of the hill which Xenophon had left, they rolled down rocks.
 <s n="1605"/>One man’s leg was broken, and Xenophon was deserted by the man who carried his shield.
</section>

<section n="4.2.21" sentences="1">
 <s n="1606"/>But Eurylochos of Lusia, one of the heavy-armed men, ran up to him and covered both with one shield, so they retreated, and the others joined the main body.
</section>

<section n="4.2.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="1607"/>After this, the whole Hellenic army united, and they encamped there in luxury;
 <s n="1608"/>there were fine houses in plenty, and wine so plentiful that it was kept in cisterns of cement.
</section>

<section n="4.2.23" sentences="2">
 <s n="1609"/>Xenophon and Cheirisophos arranged to give up the guide in return for the dead bodies,
 <s n="1610"/>and they rendered to the dead all the honors customary for brave men, so far as possible.
</section>

<section n="4.2.24" sentences="2">
 <s n="1611"/>Next day they marched without a guide;
 <s n="1612"/>but the enemy hindered their passage by fighting and forestalling them at every narrow place.
</section>

<section n="4.2.25" sentences="1">
 <s n="1613"/>When they obstructed in front, Xenophon marched out from behind and made a loop over the mountains, trying to get above them and break the barrier in front;
</section>

<section n="4.2.26" sentences="1">
 <s n="1614"/>when they attacked the rear, Cheirisophos marched out and tried to get above the assailants and break the barrier behind. Thus they always looked after each other and each party supported the other.
</section>

<section n="4.2.27" sentences="3">
 <s n="1615"/>Sometimes when a party climbed a hill for this purpose, the natives made it hot for them as they came down again,
 <s n="1616"/>for they were so light that they could escape from quite close,
 <s n="1617"/>as they had nothing but bows and slings.
</section>

<section n="4.2.28" sentences="7">
 <s n="1618"/>They were very good bowmen;
 <s n="1619"/>they had bows of nearly three cubits, the arrows more than two cubits long.
 <s n="1620"/>When they shot an arrow, they pressed the left foot upon the lower part of the bow as they drew the string;
 <s n="1621"/>the arrows went through shield and corselet.
 <s n="1622"/>When the Hellenes could get hold of any, they fitted thongs to them and used them as javelins.
 <s n="1623"/>In these districts, the Cretans were most useful;
 <s n="1624"/>their commander was the Cretan Stratocles.
</section>

<section n="4.3.1" sentences="3">
 <s n="1625"/>That night again they spent in the open, among the villages lying above the plain by the River Centritis, width about two hundred feet, which was the frontier between Armenia and the country of the Kurds.
 <s n="1626"/>There the Hellenes had time to breathe, delighted to see a plain.
 <s n="1627"/>The river was distant from the mountains nearly a mile.
</section>

<section n="4.3.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="1628"/>There they had a happy night, with plenty to eat, talking and talking about the struggle now past.
 <s n="1629"/>For they had spent seven days passing through the Kurds’ country, fighting all the time, and they had suffered worse things than all the king and Tissaphernes did to them.
 <s n="1630"/>They were quit of all that, they thought, and fell happily asleep.
</section>

<section n="4.3.3" sentences="1">
 <s n="1631"/>At daybreak they saw horsemen somewhere across the river, armed and prepared to oppose a crossing, and footmen on the banks, marshalled above the horsemen, to hinder them from landing in Armenia.
</section>

<section n="4.3.4" sentences="3">
 <s n="1632"/>These were mercenaries of Orontas and Artuchas, Armenian and Mardian and Chaldean.
 <s n="1633"/>The Chaldeans were said to be free men and strong;
 <s n="1634"/>they were armed with long wicker-work shields and lances.
</section>

<section n="4.3.5" sentences="3">
 <s n="1635"/>The banks where these ranks were standing were three or four furlongs away from the river;
 <s n="1636"/>one road was in sight, leading upwards, apparently a regular highway.
 <s n="1637"/>The Hellenes tried to cross by this road.
</section>

<section n="4.3.6" sentences="3">
 <s n="1638"/>When they tried, the water came over the breast, and the river-bed was rough with large slippery stones.
 <s n="1639"/>They could not go through the water in arms, or else the river carried them off;
 <s n="1640"/>they could not carry the arms on their heads, or they were bare against arrows and other missiles, so they retreated and encamped by the riverside.
</section>

<section n="4.3.7" sentences="2">
 <s n="1641"/>Where they had spent the last night themselves on the mountain, they saw the Kurds assembled, armed, in large numbers.
 <s n="1642"/>Then the Hellenes were very despondent when they saw how difficult it was to cross the river, and when they saw troops ready to resist, when they saw the Kurds behind ready to fall on them while they crossed.
</section>

<section n="4.3.8" sentences="4">
 <s n="1643"/>So they stayed there in great despondency that day and night.
 <s n="1644"/>But Xenophon saw a dream.
 <s n="1645"/>He thought he was fastened in irons, and the fetters dropped off of themselves, so that he was free, and able to straddle as wide as he liked.
 <s n="1646"/>In the early morning he went and told Cheirisophos that he had hopes all would be well, and described his dream.
</section>

<section n="4.3.9" sentences="3">
 <s n="1647"/>Cheirisophos was glad, and as soon as the light of dawn came the captains made sacrifice, all being present;
 <s n="1648"/>and the omens were favorable even in the very first victim.
 <s n="1649"/>Leaving the sacrifice, the captains and officers sent round word for the army to breakfast.
</section>

<section n="4.3.10" sentences="2">
 <s n="1650"/>While Xenophon sat at breakfast, two young men ran up;
 <s n="1651"/>for all knew that anyone might come to him, at breakfast or supper, or if he slept, could awake him, and tell anything he had to propose for the war.
</section>

<section n="4.3.11" sentences="1">
 <s n="1652"/>They told him, then, that they had been gathering firewood, and they saw opposite, among some rocks which came down to the river, an old man with a woman and girls laying something like bags of clothes in a cave in the rock.
</section>

<section n="4.3.12" sentences="4">
 <s n="1653"/>As they watched, they thought it was a safe place to cross;
 <s n="1654"/>at least enemy horsemen could not come near that place.
 <s n="1655"/>So they stripped and went in naked holding their knives, expecting to swim for it;
 <s n="1656"/>but they simply marched on and crossed and never wetted their middle; then they took the clothes and came back.
</section>

<section n="4.3.13" sentences="3">
 <s n="1657"/>So Xenophon poured a libation at once, and told the young men to fill up, and to pray the gods who had shown the dream and the ford that they might accomplish what remained with good success.
 <s n="1658"/>After this, he took the young men straight to Cheirisophos,
 <s n="1659"/>and they told their story.
</section>

<section n="4.3.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="1660"/>Cheirisophos listened, and poured a libation himself.
 <s n="1661"/>Afterwards they sent round word to pack up; then called a meeting of the captains, and discussed how they could best cross and conquer those in front and not be hurt by those behind.
</section>

<section n="4.3.15" sentences="1">
 <s n="1662"/>They decided that Cheirisophos was to lead across with half the army, and the other half was to stay with Xenophon, and the baggage-train to cross in the middle.
</section>

<section n="4.3.16" sentences="3">
 <s n="1663"/>When all was arranged they marched;
 <s n="1664"/>the young men guided them, with the river on the left.
 <s n="1665"/>It was about half a mile to the ford.
</section>

<section n="4.3.17" sentences="3">
 <s n="1666"/>As they marched the troops of horse opposite kept pace with them.
 <s n="1667"/>When they reached the ford and the riverbanks, they grounded arms;
 <s n="1668"/>and first Cheirisophos himself put a wreath on his head and threw off his cloak, and took up his arms, giving command to the others to do the same; and he ordered the subalterns to lead their companions in column, part on his left and part on his right. Meanwhile, the soothsayers were sacrificing over the river.
</section>

<section n="4.3.18" sentences="1">
 <s n="1669"/>The enemy were shooting with bows and slings, but they were out of range.
</section>

<section n="4.3.19" sentences="3">
 <s n="1670"/>When the omens were favorable, all the men chanted the battle hymn and cheered,
 <s n="1671"/>and the women all raised the woman’s hosanna of triumph,
 <s n="1672"/>for there were many women in the camp.
</section>

<section n="4.3.20" sentences="2">
 <s n="1673"/>Cheirisophos then plunged in with his division;
 <s n="1674"/>but Xenophon took the handiest men of the rearguard and doubled back to the passage on the road which led away into the Armenian mountains, pretending that he wished to cross there and cut off the horsemen along the river.
</section>

<section n="4.3.21" sentences="2">
 <s n="1675"/>The enemy saw Cheirisophos and his forces easily crossing the water, and Xenophon doubling back.
 <s n="1676"/>Then, fearing they might be cut off, they galloped away towards the upper crossing, but as soon as they reached the road, they turned upwards towards the mountains.
</section>

<section n="4.3.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="1677"/>Lycios, who commanded the division of horsemen in the army of Cheirisophos, and Aischines, who commanded the light-armed troops, followed the cavalry when they saw them galloping away;
 <s n="1678"/>and the soldiers called out, “Don’t let them get away! Follow into the mountains!”
</section>

<section n="4.3.23" sentences="2">
 <s n="1679"/>However, Cheirisophos did not pursue the cavalry when his crossing was made, but climbed the high banks opposite the place to attack the enemy there above.
 <s n="1680"/>But these saw their own cavalry in flight, and saw the men-at-arms moving to attack them, and left the higher ground above the river.
</section>

<section n="4.3.24" sentences="2">
 <s n="1681"/>Now Xenophon saw that all went well across the river and he doubled back towards the crossing troops;
 <s n="1682"/>for the Kurds were visible coming down from the hills to attack the last men.
</section>

<section n="4.3.25" sentences="1">
 <s n="1683"/>Cheirisophos already held the upper parts, and Lycios with his little troop in pursuit caught the last of their baggage train and with it a load of fine dress and drinking cups.
</section>

<section n="4.3.26" sentences="2">
 <s n="1684"/>The last of the Hellenic baggage train and followers had just crossed when Xenophon returned and took a stand opposite the Kurds.
 <s n="1685"/>He ordered the captains to form up their companies in squads and to deploy to the left in line of squads, the captains and subalterns facing the Kurds with sergeants in the rear facing the river.
</section>

<section n="4.3.27" sentences="2">
 <s n="1686"/>When the Kurds saw the rearguard bare of the camp followers and looking just a handful of men, they advanced more quickly, singing songs of their own.
 <s n="1687"/>But Cheirisophos, being now in safety, sent back the light-armed troops and the bowmen and slingers to Xenophon, and told them to obey his orders.
</section>

<section n="4.3.28" sentences="3">
 <s n="1688"/>Xenophon saw them ready to cross; he sent a messenger with orders to stay near the river but not to go in.
 <s n="1689"/>When he began to cross himself, they should enter the river to meet him, on the left and right, as if to cross, with javelins on the thong and arrows on the string,
  <s n="1690"/> but they were not to go far into the river.

</section>

<section n="4.3.29" sentences="2">
 <s n="1691"/>To his own men the orders were: “As soon as a slingstone hits a shield and you hear the rattle, chant the battle hymn and attack the enemy.
 <s n="1692"/>When the enemy turn and the trumpet sounds the attack, right about face and the sergeants lead, all double and each cross opposite his post so as not to interfere with each other. The first man over is the best man!”
</section>

<section n="4.3.30" sentences="1">
 <s n="1693"/>The Kurds saw only a handful of men left, for many of those who had been ordered to wait were gone to look after their animals or baggage or women.
</section>

<section n="4.3.31" sentences="3">
 <s n="1694"/>The Hellenes chanted their battle hymn and charged at the double, but the enemy did not wait;
 <s n="1695"/>for they were well equipped for running up and running off in the mountains,
 <s n="1696"/>but not for a fight hand to hand.
</section>

<section n="4.3.32" sentences="2">
 <s n="1697"/>Then the trumpet sounded the charge; the enemy ran faster than ever,
 <s n="1698"/>and the Hellenes turned about and made for the river at full speed.
</section>

<section n="4.3.33" sentences="1">
 <s n="1699"/>Some of the enemy noticed this and ran back to the river, wounding a few with arrows, but most of them could still be seen in full flight even when the Hellenes were across.
</section>

<section n="4.3.34" sentences="2">
 <s n="1700"/>The others who came to meet them went too far in their ardor and had to cross again behind Xenophon.
 <s n="1701"/>Some of these also were wounded.
</section>



<section n="4.4.1" sentences="2">
 <s n="1702"/>When they had crossed, they formed up together about midday and marched through Armenia, all plain and smooth little hills, not less than five leagues;
 <s n="1703"/>for there were no villages near the river, because of their wars with the Kurds.
</section>

<section n="4.4.2" sentences="2">
 <s n="1704"/>But the village they reached was large and had a palace for the governor, and towers over most of the houses;
 <s n="1705"/>provisions in abundance.
</section>

<section n="4.4.3" sentences="4">
 <s n="1706"/>From there they marched two stages, 10 leagues, until they reached the source of the Tigris River.
 <s n="1707"/>From there they marched three stages, 15 leagues, to the River Teleboas;
 <s n="1708"/>a fine river, but not large,
 <s n="1709"/>and there were many villages about the river.
</section>

<section n="4.4.4" sentences="2">
 <s n="1710"/>This district was called Southern Armenia;
 <s n="1711"/>the lieutenant-governor was Tiribazos, the King’s Friend, and his was the right to mount the king on horseback when he was present.
</section>

<section n="4.4.5" sentences="3">
 <s n="1712"/>This man rode up with a troop of horse, and sent forward an interpreter to say he wished to parley with the commanders.
 <s n="1713"/>The captains decided to hear him,
 <s n="1714"/>and coming within hearing, they asked what he wished.
</section>

<section n="4.4.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="1715"/>He replied that he wished for a truce, on these terms: he would not damage the Hellenes, and they should not burn the houses, but take what provisions they needed.
 <s n="1716"/>The captains agreed, and a truce was made on those terms.
</section>

<section n="4.4.7" sentences="3">
 <s n="1717"/>Thence they marched three stages over the plain, 15 leagues, and Tiribazos followed with his force about a mile behind.
 <s n="1718"/>Then they reached the palace,
 <s n="1719"/>and many villages round, full of all sorts of provisions.
</section>

<section n="4.4.8" sentences="3">
 <s n="1720"/>While they were encamping, there came a heavy fall of snow;
 <s n="1721"/>and in the morning, it was decided to billet the companies with their captains among the villages,
 <s n="1722"/>for they saw no enemy, and it seemed to be safe with all that snow.
</section>

<section n="4.4.9" sentences="2">
 <s n="1723"/>There they had all the good things they wanted: sacrificial victims, corn, fragrant old wines, raisins, and all kinds of vegetables.
 <s n="1724"/>But some stragglers from the camp reported that they had seen large numbers of fires in the night.
</section>

<section n="4.4.10" sentences="3">
 <s n="1725"/>So billeting seemed no longer safe; they must assemble the army again,
 <s n="1726"/>and so they did.
 <s n="1727"/>The sky seemed likely to clear then, but while they slept, there came snow without end which covered the arms and the men lying there.
</section>

<section n="4.4.11" sentences="4">
 <s n="1728"/>The snow was a great hindrance to the animals, and nobody wanted to get up,
 <s n="1729"/>for it kept them snug and warm where they lay unless it slipped off.

</section>

<section n="4.4.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="1732"/>At last Xenophon condescended to stand up in his shirt and split wood,
 <s n="1733"/>and then one or another got up and pulled the log out of his hands and began to split; then others got up and lit fires and oiled themselves.
</section>

<section n="4.4.13" sentences="2">
 <s n="1734"/>There was plenty of oil there: hog’s lard, oil from sesame and bitter almonds, and turpentine, which they used instead of olive oil.
 <s n="1735"/>A balm also was made from the same.
</section>

<section n="4.4.14" sentences="3">
 <s n="1736"/>After this, they decided to billet again under shelter.
 <s n="1737"/>The men, with loud shouts of delight, rushed back to the shelter and provisions;
 <s n="1738"/>and then any who had burnt the houses they left just out of mischief were sorry for it when they had bad quarters.
</section>

<section n="4.4.15" sentences="2">
 <s n="1739"/>The next night, they sent Democrates of Tenos with a few men to the mountains where stragglers said they had seen the fires;
 <s n="1740"/>for this man had been found before to bring truthful reports, and when he said it was so, it was, and when he said it wasn’t, it was not.
</section>

<section n="4.4.16" sentences="1">
 <s n="1741"/>When he came back, he said he had seen no fires, but he brought back a prisoner armed with a Persian bow and quiver, and a bill such as the Amazons carry.
</section>

<section n="4.4.17" sentences="2">
 <s n="1742"/>Questioned, he said he was a Persian and that he was foraging for provisions from the army of Tiribazos.
 <s n="1743"/>They asked how large was the army and why it had been formed.
</section>

<section n="4.4.18" sentences="2">
 <s n="1744"/>He replied that Tiribazos had his own force and some mercenaries, Chalybians and Taochians;
 <s n="1745"/>said he was prepared to fall on the Hellenes while they crossed the mountains, in narrow places with only one road.
</section>

<section n="4.4.19" sentences="2">
 <s n="1746"/>The captains decided, on hearing this, to assemble the army.
 <s n="1747"/>They marched at once with the man as a guide, leaving guards under the command of Sophainetos, a Stymphalian.
</section>

<section n="4.4.20" sentences="1">
 <s n="1748"/>While they were crossing, the light-armed troops, going on before, caught sight of the enemy camp, and without waiting for the heavy troops, rushed on the camp with loud shouts.
</section>

<section n="4.4.21" sentences="2">
 <s n="1749"/>The natives, hearing the noise, did not stay; they ran for it.
 <s n="1750"/>But still, some were killed, and horses were taken, about twenty, and the tent of Tiribazos was taken, and in it couches with silver feet, and cups, and men who said they were bakers and butlers.
</section>

<section n="4.4.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="1751"/>When the captains of the heavy-armed troops heard this report, they decided to return to their camp as quickly as they could to prevent an assault on those left behind.
 <s n="1752"/>They sounded the recall and retired, and reached the camp the same day.
</section>

<section n="4.5.1" sentences="3">
 <s n="1753"/>Next day they decided to march as quickly as possible, before the enemy could collect and occupy the narrows.
 <s n="1754"/>They packed up and marched at once, through deep snow, having several guides;
 <s n="1755"/>and the same day they got over the height where Tiribazos meant to attack them, and encamped there.
</section>

<section n="4.5.2" sentences="1">
 <s n="1756"/>From that place they marched three desert stages, 15 leagues, and reached the River Euphrates, which they crossed waist-deep.
</section>

<section n="4.5.3" sentences="3">
 <s n="1757"/>The source was said to be not far away.
 <s n="1758"/>From there they marched through deep snow over a plain, three stages, 15 leagues.
 <s n="1759"/>The third was difficult, and a north wind blew in their faces, parching everything everywhere and freezing the men.
</section>

<section n="4.5.4" sentences="4">
 <s n="1760"/>There one of the seers advised them to sacrifice to the wind, and sacrifice they did.
 <s n="1761"/>The answer to their prayers was manifest at once to all, when the violence of the wind abated.
 <s n="1762"/>But the snow was a fathom deep, 
 <s n="1763"/>so that many animals and slaves were lost, and soldiers too, about thirty.
</section>

<section n="4.5.5" sentences="4">
 <s n="1764"/>They kept fire burning all night, and so got through; there was plenty of wood in that stage,
 <s n="1765"/>but those who came up late found none.
 <s n="1766"/>Those who had been there long and lit the fire would not let the late ones come near the fire
 <s n="1767"/>unless they gave them some of their wheat or anything else they had eatable.
</section>

<section n="4.5.6" sentences="3">
 <s n="1768"/>Here, then, they all shared together what they had.
 <s n="1769"/>Where the fire burned great holes were made in the snow right down to the soil,
 <s n="1770"/>and so it was possible to measure the depth of the snow.
</section>

<section n="4.5.7" sentences="2">
 <s n="1771"/>The whole of the next day they were marching through snow, and many men were faint with hunger.
 <s n="1772"/>Xenophon was in the rearguard, and he did not know what was the matter with them.
</section>

<section n="4.5.8" sentences="1">
 <s n="1773"/>Someone who knew about it told him it was hunger faintness, and they would get up if he gave them something to eat; so he went round the baggage animals, and if he found food anywhere he gave it round, or sent those who could run about to give some to the sufferers.
</section>

<section n="4.5.9" sentences="2">
 <s n="1774"/>As soon as they had had a bite, up they got and marched on.
 <s n="1775"/>They marched until, when darkness came, Cheirisophos reached a village and found women and girls fetching water from the fountain in front of their wall.
</section>

<section n="4.5.10" sentences="4">
 <s n="1776"/>These women asked who they were.
 <s n="1777"/>The interpreter answered in Persian that they came from the king to the governor.
 <s n="1778"/>They said he was not there, but about one league away.
 <s n="1779"/>Since it was late, they went together with the women to the village headman inside the stockade.
</section>

<section n="4.5.11" sentences="2">
 <s n="1780"/>Then Cheirisophos and all who could encamped there,
 <s n="1781"/>but the rest of the army, those who could not finish the march, spent the night foodless and fireless; some of the men perished there.
</section>

<section n="4.5.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="1782"/>Parties of the enemy were always following, and carried off disabled animals and fought over them together.
 <s n="1783"/>Men also were left behind who had been blinded by the snow or lost their toes by frostbite.
</section>

<section n="4.5.13" sentences="1">
 <s n="1784"/>It did some good to the eyes if the men marched holding something black before their eyes; for the feet, to keep them moving without rest all the time and to take off the shoes at night.
</section>

<section n="4.5.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="1785"/>But if any slept with shoes on, the straps worked into the feet and the shoes froze;
 <s n="1786"/>for the old shoes were gone, and they had to make them of raw leather from untanned hides newly flayed.
</section>

<section n="4.5.15" sentences="4">
 <s n="1787"/>Through such necessities some of the soldiers were left behind, and when they saw a black patch where the snow had disappeared, they imagined that it had thawed there,
 <s n="1788"/>and indeed it had thawed, because of a hot spring which was steaming in a dingle close by.
 <s n="1789"/>There these men turned aside and sat,
 <s n="1790"/>and said they would not go on.
</section>

<section n="4.5.16" sentences="3">
 <s n="1791"/>Xenophon, with some of his rearguard, noticed this, and begged them with prayers and entreaties not to stay behind,
 <s n="1792"/>telling them that packs of the enemy were on the chase.
 <s n="1793"/>At last, he grew angry, but they told him to cut their throats; march they could not.
</section>

<section n="4.5.17" sentences="2">
 <s n="1794"/>Then it seemed best to frighten the enemy following, if he could, and keep them off the weary men.
 <s n="1795"/>It was dark now, and the enemy were following in confusion and quarrelling over what they had.
</section>

<section n="4.5.18" sentences="3">
 <s n="1796"/>Then the rearguard, who were well and strong enough, jumped up and ran for the enemy,
 <s n="1797"/>and the weary men shouted as loud as they could and banged spears against their shields.
 <s n="1798"/>The enemy were frightened and smashed through the snow into the dingle, and not a sound was heard from them again.
</section>

<section n="4.5.19" sentences="3">
 <s n="1799"/>Xenophon and his party told the weary men that someone would come for them tomorrow and marched on.
 <s n="1800"/>They had not gone half a mile when they came on their soldiers on the road, lying upon the snow wrapped up and trying to sleep, without any guard at all, and he made them get up.
 <s n="1801"/>They said those in front would not go on.
</section>

<section n="4.5.20" sentences="2">
 <s n="1802"/>He passed by and sent on the strongest of his light troops to see what was in the way.
 <s n="1803"/>They reported that the whole army was sleeping like that.
</section>

<section n="4.5.21" sentences="2">
 <s n="1804"/>Then Xenophon settled down there for the night, without fire or food, after posting such guards as he could.
 <s n="1805"/>When day was near, Xenophon sent the youngest of his men back to the weary men, with orders to make them get up and force them to march.
</section>

<section n="4.5.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="1806"/>Meanwhile, Cheirisophos sent men from the village to see how the rear was doing.
 <s n="1807"/>Glad indeed they were to see them and handed over the weary men to be taken to the camp, while they marched themselves, and two or three miles farther on they reached the village where Cheirisophos had spent the night.
</section>

<section n="4.5.23" sentences="2">
 <s n="1808"/>When they met, they thought it was safe to billet the companies in the villages.
 <s n="1809"/>Cheirisophos remained where he was, and the others drew lots for the villages in sight, and each led his men to their place.
</section>

<section n="4.5.24" sentences="3">
 <s n="1810"/>Now Polycrates, an Athenian officer, asked for special leave, 
 <s n="1811"/> and taking the most active of his men, he ran to the village allotted to Xenophon, and found all the villagers inside, and their headman, and colts which were being reared for the king’s tribute, seventeen, and the headman’s daughter nine days wedded;
 <s n="1812"/>her husband was out hunting hares, and he was not caught in the village.
</section>

<section n="4.5.25" sentences="4">
 <s n="1813"/>The houses were underground, the mouth like a well, roomy underneath;
 <s n="1814"/>the people went down by ladders, but the animals had a way dug for them.
 <s n="1815"/>In the houses were sheep, goats, cattle, birds, and their young;
 <s n="1816"/>all the beasts were fed indoors on green stuff.
</section>

<section n="4.5.26" sentences="2">
 <s n="1817"/>There was also wheat and barley and vegetables, and barley wine in tubs;
 <s n="1818"/>there were barley grains floating on the wine at the rim, and straws lay there, large and small, without knots.
</section>

<section n="4.5.27" sentences="3">
 <s n="1819"/>If you were thirsty, you picked up one of these and sucked through it.
 <s n="1820"/>It was very strong wine if drunk neat,
 <s n="1821"/>and the taste was delicious when you were used to it.
</section>

<section n="4.5.28" sentences="1">
 <s n="1822"/>Xenophon made the headman of this village share his table, and told him to fear nothing; they would not rob him of his children, but they would fill the house with provisions before they went if he would do good service to the army and guide them to some other tribe.
</section>

<section n="4.5.29" sentences="2">
 <s n="1823"/>The man promised, and with all goodwill showed him where the wine was buried.
 <s n="1824"/>That night, billeted thus, all the soldiers rested in the midst of abundance, keeping the headman under guard and his family with him safe in sight.
</section>

<section n="4.5.30" sentences="2">
 <s n="1825"/>On the next day, Xenophon took the headman before Cheirisophos.
 <s n="1826"/>Whenever he passed a village, he looked in and found them everywhere feasting and making merry. The people never let them go without offering breakfast.
</section>

<section n="4.5.31" sentences="1">
 <s n="1827"/>Everywhere on the same table were piles of lamb, kid, pork, veal, fowl, with all sorts of cakes, both wheaten and barley.
</section>

<section n="4.5.32" sentences="3">
 <s n="1828"/>When one would show goodwill by drinking your health, he dragged you to the bowl, and you must duck your head and gulp it up like a bullock.
 <s n="1829"/>They let the headman take away whatever he liked.
 <s n="1830"/>But he would accept nothing, only if he saw any of his own kinsmen, he took him away with him.
</section>

<section n="4.5.33" sentences="2">
 <s n="1831"/>When they reached Cheirisophos, they found them there in their quarters, garlanded with hay and served by Armenian boys in their native costume;
 <s n="1832"/>they made signs to the boys as if they were deaf and dumb to show what they wanted.
</section>

<section n="4.5.34" sentences="5">
 <s n="1833"/>After Cheirisophos and Xenophon had greeted one another affectionately, they asked the headman together through the interpreter who spoke Persian, “What country is this?”
 <s n="1834"/>He said, “Armenia.”
 <s n="1835"/>They asked again for whom he was breeding the colts.
 <s n="1836"/>He said, “Tribute for the king.”
 <s n="1837"/>The neighbouring country, he said, was the Chalybeans, and pointed where the road was.
</section>

<section n="4.5.35" sentences="3">
 <s n="1838"/>Then Xenophon led him to his own servants, and gave him an oldish horse which he had caught to feed up and sacrifice, because he heard that the horse was sacred to the Sun: he was afraid it might die, for it had suffered from the march.
 <s n="1839"/>He took one of the colts himself,
 <s n="1840"/>and gave a colt each to the other captains and officers.
</section>

<section n="4.5.36" sentences="3">
 <s n="1841"/>The horses in this place were smaller than the Persian, but much more spirited.
 <s n="1842"/>Then the headman taught him to wrap bags round the feet of the baggage-horses when they are driven through snow;
 <s n="1843"/>without the bags, they sink down to the belly.
</section>

<section n="4.6.1" sentences="3">
 <s n="1844"/>When the eighth day came, he gave the headman as guide to Cheirisophos, but left all the headman’s household except his son, just growing up;
 <s n="1845"/>this boy he entrusted to Pleisthenes of Amphipolis, and he should be given back if the man was a good guide.
 <s n="1846"/>They filled his house with all the supplies they could find and marched away.
</section>

<section n="4.6.7" sentences="3">

<section n="4.6.9" sentences="2">
 <s n="1863"/>“My opinion,” said Cleanor, “is that when we have breakfasted, we should arm at once and attack.
