<section n="4.6.10" sentences="4">
 <s n="1865"/>Xenophon spoke:
 <s n="1866"/>“My opinion is this.
 <s n="1867"/>If a fight cannot be avoided, we ought to be ready to fight our strongest.
 <s n="1868"/>But if we wish to traverse the pass most easily, we should consider how to pass with fewest wounds and smallest loss of men."
</section>

<section n="4.6.11" sentences="2">
 <s n="1869"/>"What we see of the mountain is seven or eight miles across, and the men waiting for us are visible only on the road;
 <s n="1870"/>it is much better, then, to try to steal some part of the empty space and get round unseen, if we can, than to attack fortifications and men prepared to fight."
</section>

<section n="4.6.12" sentences="1">
 <s n="1871"/>"For it is much easier to climb a steep place without fighting than to go along the flat with enemies on each side; without fighting we can see better at night what is in front of our feet than we can by day while fighting; and the rough way before our feet will be easier to tread without fighting than the level path when our heads are being battered."
</section>

<section n="4.6.13" sentences="3">
 <s n="1872"/>"To steal a march does not seem impossible to me, since we can go by night so as not to be seen, and keep far enough away so as not to be heard;
 <s n="1873"/>and if we make a feint of attacking here the mountain will be more deserted,
 <s n="1874"/>since the enemy would remain all together where they are."
</section>

<section n="4.6.14" sentences="2">

<section n="5.1.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="2061"/>After this, they met and deliberated about the rest of their travels. 
 <s n="2062"/>First Leon, a Thurian, rose, and spoke as follows: 
 <s n="2063"/>“To speak for myself, sirs, I'm tired out by this time, with packing up and marching and doubling and carrying arms and falling in and keeping guard and fighting. I want a little rest now from these hardships. We have the sea, then let’s go by sea the rest of the way, lying flat like Odysseus, till we get to Hellas.” 
</section>
<section n="5.1.3" sentences="3">
 <s n="2064"/>There was great cheering at this, “Good! good!” 
 <s n="2065"/>and someone else said the same, and so said all of them. 
 <s n="2066"/>Then Cheirisophos rose, and said: 
</section>
<section n="5.1.4" sentences="5">
 <s n="2067"/>“I have a friend, sirs, Anaxibios, who is now Lord High Admiral at home, as it happens. 
 <s n="2068"/>If you will send me there, I think I shall bring you back ships of war and transports to carry you. 
 <s n="2069"/>If you want to go by sea, wait until I return; 
 <s n="2070"/> I won’t be long.” 
 <s n="2071"/>On hearing this, the men were delighted and voted that he should sail, the sooner the better. 
</section>
<section n="5.1.5" sentences="3">
 <s n="2072"/>Next Xenophon rose and spoke: 
 <s n="2073"/>“So Cheirisophos is despatched for ships, and we are to wait for him. 
 <s n="2074"/>But what is the proper thing to do while we wait? I will tell you. 
</section>
<section n="5.1.6" sentences="4">
 <s n="2075"/>First, we must get provisions from enemy country. 
 <s n="2076"/>Our market is not enough, and we have nothing to buy with, except some few. 
 <s n="2077"/>But this country is enemy country, 
 <s n="2078"/>so there is danger of losing many lives if you march for provisions carelessly and unguardedly.” 
</section>
<section n="5.1.7" sentences="1">
 <s n="2079"/>“I propose that you go in regular parties to get provisions, and so keep safe, and not go wandering about anyhow; and that we should arrange this for you.” 
</section>
<section n="5.1.8" sentences="4">
 <s n="2080"/>Agreed also. 
 <s n="2081"/>“Something more,” said he.” 
 <s n="2082"/>“Don’t forget that the enemy have plenty of time to make raids too, and they have a right to attack us because we hold what is theirs; they are planted up there above us. 
 <s n="2083"/>I propose, therefore, that guards be set round the camp; if we take it in turns to watch and to spy, the enemy would be less able to hunt us.” 
</section>
<section n="5.1.9" sentences="8">
 <s n="2084"/>Agreed also. 
 <s n="2085"/>“Something more,” said he.” 
 <s n="2086"/>“Don’t forget that the enemy have plenty of time to make raids too, and they have a right to attack us 
 <s n="2087"/> because we hold what is theirs;
 <s n="2088"/> they are planted up there above us. 
 <s n="2089"/>I propose, therefore, that guards be set round the camp;
 <s n="2090"/> if we take it in turns to watch and to spy, the enemy would be less able to hunt us. 
 <s n="2091"/> Then again, look here: ” 
</section>
<section n="5.1.10" sentences="4">
 <s n="2092"/>“if we knew for certain that Cheirisophos would bring us transports enough, there would be no need for what I’m going to say; 
 <s n="2093"/>but this is uncertain, in fact, so I propose that we try to collect vessels from hereabouts. 
 <s n="2094"/>Then if he does bring them, and we also have some here, there will be all the more to carry us; 
 <s n="2095"/>but if not, we will use what we have.” 
</section>
<section n="5.1.11" sentences="3">
 <s n="2096"/>“I often see vessels sailing past, let us ask the Trapezuntians for the loan of warships, and bring the vessels in and take off the rudders and keep them until we have enough to hold us. 
 <s n="2097"/>In that way perhaps we shall not lack the transport we need.” 
 <s n="2098"/>This was agreed to. 
</section>
<section n="5.1.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="2099"/>“Consider then,” he said, “whether it is fair that we find food by a general subscription for the crews we shall bring in, as long as they wait our pleasure here; 
 <s n="2100"/>and we should agree on the passage-money, that we may repay service with service.” 
</section>

<section n="5.1.13" sentences="2">
 <s n="2101"/>“And in case it turns out that we cannot succeed in collecting vessels enough, I propose that the cities along the sea-coast be instructed to put the roads in good repair, for I hear they are in a bad state. 
 <s n="2102"/>They will obey, because they fear us and will be glad to get rid of us.” 
</section>
<section n="5.1.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="2103"/>Then there were loud outcries, “No, not by road, we won't go by road!” 
 <s n="2104"/>When Xenophon saw their folly, he did not put this to the vote, but he privately persuaded the cities easily to repair the road, by telling them that they would get out of the way more quickly if the roads were in good repair. 
</section>
<section n="5.1.15" sentences="4">
 <s n="2105"/>They also got a fifty-oar galley from Trapezûs, and put in command Dexippos, a farmer from Laconia. 
 <s n="2106"/>But he neglected his duty of collecting vessels, and sailed clean away right out of the Euxine, ship and all. 
 <s n="2107"/>Later he had his deserts; for in Thrace he tried to meddle in the affairs of Seuthes, 
 <s n="2108"/>and he was killed by Nicandros the Laconian. 
</section>
<section n="5.1.16" sentences="2">
 <s n="2109"/>They got also a thirty-oar galley, and put an Athenian Polycrates in command; he brought in all the vessels he took to the camp. 
 <s n="2110"/>Any freights they took out of the vessels, and set guards to keep them safe, and kept the vessels for the transport. 
</section>

<section n="5.1.17" sentences="2">
 <s n="2111"/>While these things were being done, the Hellenes went out marauding, and some succeeded but some did not. 
 <s n="2112"/>Cleainetos led his company and another to a dangerous place, where he was killed and many with him. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.1" sentences="2">
 <s n="2113"/>By and by they could no longer find provisions within a day’s raid. 
 <s n="2114"/>Then Xenophon procured guides from Trapezûs and led half the army against the Drilai, leaving half to guard the camp; for the Colchians, having been ejected from their houses, were in large masses on the hills above. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="2115"/>The Trapezuntians would not guide them to places where it was easy to find provisions, because the natives were friendly to them; 
 <s n="2116"/>but they were ready enough to guide them against the Drilai, who had done them much damage. 
 <s n="2117"/>These were mountainous places and hard to approach, and the people were the most warlike of all on the Euxine. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.3" sentences="5">
 <s n="2118"/>When the Hellenes reached the highlands, the Drilai burnt all the villages which they thought might be taken, and retired; 
 <s n="2119"/>nothing could be found but a pig or a cow or some other animal which had escaped the fire. 
 <s n="2120"/>One place was their metropolis,
 <s n="2121"/> and all streamed into this. 
 <s n="2122"/>Round it was a very deep ravine, and the approaches to the place were difficult. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.4" sentences="3">
 <s n="2123"/>The light troops went ahead of the heavies half a mile or more, and crossed the ravine. 
 <s n="2124"/>They saw many sheep and other animals, and assaulted the stronghold, followed by large numbers of those who came to forage, spear in hand, 
 <s n="2125"/>so that those who crossed numbered more than two thousand men. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.5" sentences="2">
 <s n="2126"/>They could not take the place by assault, for there was a broad moat round it too, supported by a bank, with stakes on the banks, and wooden turrets in a row close together; 
 <s n="2127"/>so they tried to retire, but the enemy fell on them. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="2128"/>To run for it was impossible, for the path down from the place was only enough for single file. 
 <s n="2129"/>So they sent to Xenophon, who was leading the heavies. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.7" sentences="5">
 <s n="2130"/>The man came up, and said, “Here’s a place full of stuff, 
 <s n="2131"/>but we can’t take it, 
 <s n="2132"/>the place is too strong; 
 <s n="2133"/>and we can’t get away, 
 <s n="2134"/>for they have come out, they are fighting, and the road down is difficult.” 
</section>


<section n="5.2.8" sentences="1">
 <s n="2135"/>Xenophon on hearing this marched to the ravine, and ordered the heavy troops to ground arms. He crossed himself with the officers, and considered whether it was better to bring back those who had crossed, or to bring over the heavy troops also if he thought the place could be taken. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.9" sentences="2">
 <s n="2136"/>To bring the first men back, he believed, would cost many lives; 
 <s n="2137"/>they all thought the place could be taken, and Xenophon consented to try, trusting the omens: for the seers had declared that there would be a battle, but the issue good in the end. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.10" sentences="1">
 <s n="2138"/>So he sent the officers to bring the heavy troops over; and moved away the light troops himself, and forbade all long-distance shooting. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.11" sentences="2">
 <s n="2139"/>He told the commanders each to arrange his own companies as he thought they would fight best; 
 <s n="2140"/>for there, side by side, were officers who had been fighting in rivalry for the honours all these long months. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.12" sentences="3">
 <s n="2141"/>Accordingly they did so; and he ordered the targeteers to carry javelin on strap, and the bowmen to hold arrow on string, ready to let fly at the signal; 
 <s n="2142"/>and the light troops were to fill their pouches with stones. 
 <s n="2143"/>He appointed the proper men to see after all this; 
</section>
<section n="5.2.13" sentences="1">
 <s n="2144"/>And when all was ready, when the lieutenants and petty officers, and the men who thought themselves as good as anyone, had been arranged, they watched each other carefully—for the line was crescent-shaped to fit the place. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.14" sentences="1">
 <s n="2145"/>Then they chanted the battle-hymn, and the trumpet sounded, they cheered Eleleu! eleleu! and the heavy troops charged at the double, and missiles flew—spears, arrows, slingstones, showers of stones flung by hand, and some even carried firebrands. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.15" sentences="2">
 <s n="2146"/>The showers and volleys drove the enemy out of their barricades and towers; 
 <s n="2147"/>so Agasias the Stymphalian laid down his arms and climbed up in only his tunic, and one pulled up another, and others climbed, and they took the fortress, as they believed. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.16" sentences="3">
 <s n="2148"/>Then the targeteers and the light troops rushed in, and took what they could. 
 <s n="2149"/>Xenophon stood by the gate, and kept all the heavy troops outside as far as he could, 
 <s n="2150"/>for other enemies appeared on certain strong high places. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.17" sentences="3">
 <s n="2151"/>Not very long after there was a noise inside, and men came running out with what they had taken, now and then one wounded; 
 <s n="2152"/>there was a great crush at the gate. 
 <s n="2153"/>The fugitives questioned reported that there were heights in the place, and crowds of enemies who sallied out and attacked the men within. 
</section>


<section n="5.2.18" sentences="2">
 <s n="2154"/>Then he ordered Tolmides the herald to cry, “Anyone who wants spoil, inside!” 
 <s n="2155"/>Numbers went in; they managed to push in, and overpowered the sallying parties and drove the enemy back to their citadel.

<section n="5.2.19" sentences="2">
 <s n="2156"/>So outside the citadel all was despoiled; 
 <s n="2157"/>and the heavy-armed men stood easy, some round the breastworks, some by the road leading to the citadel.

<section n="5.2.20" sentences="3">
 <s n="2158"/>Xenophon and the captains considered whether it was possible to take the citadel, for that was certain safety, 
 <s n="2159"/>else it seemed very difficult to get away. 
 <s n="2160"/>They examined the place, and thought it impossible to take. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.21" sentences="1">
 <s n="2161"/>Then they prepared the retreat. Each party demolished the stockade nearest themselves; and they sent away the useless men, and those who had loads of stuff, and most of the heavy troops, and the captains left behind those whom they trusted most.

<section n="5.2.22" sentences="1">
 <s n="2162"/>But when the retreat began, crowds of the enemy sallied out armed with wicker shields, and greaves, and Paphlagonian helmets; parties took possession of the houses on both sides of the way that led to the citadel. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.23" sentences="3">
 <s n="2163"/>So that it was no longer safe even to pursue towards the gate that led to the citadel. 
 <s n="2164"/>For the enemy threw down great baulks of wood, so that it was dangerous both to stay and to go; 
 <s n="2165"/>and the following night was terrible. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.24" sentences="3">
 <s n="2166"/>While they fought thus in great straits, some providence gave them a way of escape; 
 <s n="2167"/>for suddenly a house blazed up on the right, kindled by someone or other; 
 <s n="2168"/>when this collapsed, the men cleared out of the houses on the right. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.25" sentences="1">
 <s n="2169"/>Xenophon heard of this bit of luck, and gave orders to set on fire the left-hand houses too, which were all made of wood; so they were soon burning. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.26" sentences="5">
 <s n="2170"/>And the men ran out of these also. 
 <s n="2171"/>Those at their front were now the only trouble, and these clearly meant to fall on the retreat and the descent. 
 <s n="2172"/>Then he gave orders to all out of range to collect wood and put it between themselves and the enemy. 
 <s n="2173"/>When there was enough, they set it alight, 
 <s n="2174"/>and they set fire also to the houses along the barricade itself, to give the enemy something to do there.

<section n="5.2.27" sentences="2">
 <s n="2175"/>So they escaped with difficulty from the place by making this fire between themselves and the enemy. 
 <s n="2176"/>The whole city was burnt, houses and turrets and barricades and all, everything except the citadel. 
</section>
<section n="5.2.28" sentences="2">
 <s n="2177"/>Next day the Hellenes retired with their provisions. 
 <s n="2178"/>But they still feared the road down to Trapezuntis, which was steep and narrow, so they laid a pretended ambush.

<section n="5.2.29" sentences="2">
 <s n="2179"/>A Mysian man (named Mysos himself) took ten Cretans and stayed behind in a bushy place, pretending to wait for the enemy unseen,

<section n="5.2.30" sentences="4">
 <s n="2181"/>The enemy could see this, and feared an ambuscade,

<section n="5.2.31" sentences="1">

<section n="5.2.32" sentences="3">

 <s n="2187"/>then they retreated themselves with the enemy on their heels shooting at them, 
 <s n="2188"/>and some of the Cretans were with them shooting back. So they all returned safe to camp. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.1" sentences="5">
 <s n="2189"/>Cheirisophos did not return, and they could not collect vessels enough, and they could no longer find provisions; so it was decided to go. 
 <s n="2190"/>They put on board the ships those who were sick, those over forty years of age, the boys and women, and all the baggage which was not absolutely necessary for themselves. 
 <s n="2191"/>All these they put in the charge of Philesios and Sophainetos, the oldest of the captains, 
 <s n="2192"/>and the others marched; 
 <s n="2193"/>the roads had been made up.

<section n="5.3.2" sentences="1">
 <s n="2194"/>The march brought them on the third day to Cerasiis, a Hellenic city on the seaboard, a colony of Sinope in the Colchian country. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.3" sentences="4">
 <s n="2195"/>There they stayed for ten days; 
 <s n="2196"/>there was a review under arms, and their numbers were counted, and proved to be eight thousand six hundred. 
 <s n="2197"/>These were all who survived; 
 <s n="2198"/>the rest had been lost, in fighting, or in the snow, and a few by disease.

<section n="5.3.4" sentences="3">
 <s n="2199"/>There they divided the money taken from captives. 
 <s n="2200"/>The tithe which they reserved for Apollo and Ephesian Artemis was divided again, and each captain took a share to guard for the gods; 
 <s n="2201"/>Neon of Asine took the share of Cheirisophos. 
</section>


<section n="5.3.5" sentences="2">
 <s n="2202"/>Xenophon from the share which fell to him afterwards dedicated the offering to Apollo which is in the Athenian Treasury at Delphi, and inscribed on it his own name and the name of Proxenos, who died with Clearchos,
 <s n="2203"/> for he was his friend. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="2204"/>The part which was due to Ephesian Artemis he left behind when he went with Agesilaos out of Asia on the expedition against Boeotia. 
 <s n="2205"/>He put it in charge of Megabyzos the sacristan of Artemis, because he was going where he might risk his life; and he instructed him to return it if he came back alive, but if not, to dedicate some offering which he thought would please the goddess. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.7" sentences="2">
 <s n="2206"/>After his banishment, when he had been settled by the Lacedaimonians at Scillos, Megabyzos visited Olympia to see the Games, and returned him the deposit. 
 <s n="2207"/>Xenophon bought with the money a place for the goddess which the god’s oracle indicated. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.8" sentences="4">
 <s n="2208"/>This place, as it happened, had a River Selinous flowing through it,
 <s n="2209"/> just as at Ephesus the Selinous River flows beside the temple of Artemis. 
 <s n="2210"/>There are fishes in both, and mussels; 
 <s n="2211"/>and in the estate at Scillos there is game, all the wild animals that are hunted. 

</section>
<section n="5.3.9" sentences="2">
 <s n="2212"/>He built also an altar and a temple from the sacred money, and ever after he tithed the kindly fruits of the earth and made sacrifice to the goddess, and all citizens and neighbours, men and women, shared in the feast. 
 <s n="2213"/>The goddess provided for this fair wheatmeal, bread, wine, dainties, and a portion of the victims sacrificed from the sacred pasture, and of the game too. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.10" sentences="2">
 <s n="2214"/>For there was hunting for that feast; the sons of Xenophon hunted, and the lads from the place, and anyone else might join in; 
 <s n="2215"/>game was got from the sacred place, and from Mount Pholoë too, pig and gazelle and deer. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.11" sentences="2">
 <s n="2216"/>The district lies on the road from Lacedaimon to Olympia, two or three miles from the temple of Zeus at Olympia. 
 <s n="2217"/>In the sacred estate there is meadowland, and hills covered with wood, suited for breeding pigs and goats and horses, so that even the sumpter animals of those coming to the feast have a feast of their own. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="2218"/>Round about the temple a grove of cultivated trees has been planted, all that bear fruit in their season. 
 <s n="2219"/>The temple is made to resemble that in Ephesus, as little to great, and the image made of cypress wood is like the golden one at Ephesus. 
</section>
<section n="5.3.13" sentences="4">
 <s n="2220"/>A pillar stands by the temple with these words engraved: “THIS PLACE IS SACRED TO ARTEMIS. 
 <s n="2221"/>HE THAT OWNS AND ENJOYS IT SHALL OFFER THE TITHE EACH YEAR: 
 <s n="2222"/>WITH THE RESIDUE HE SHALL KEEP UP THE TEMPLE. 
 <s n="2223"/>WHOSOEVER NEGLECTS THIS, ‘THE GODDESS WILL SEE TO HIM.”

<section n="5.4.1" sentences="1">
 <s n="2224"/>From Cerasûs they passed on, those on board ship by sea, the others by land. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="2225"/>When they reached the boundaries of the Mossynoicoi, or Wigwam-lodgers, they sent to this tribe Timesitheos the Trapezuntian, who was their public friend or patron at Trapezûs, to ask whether they should pass through the country as enemies or as friends. 
 <s n="2226"/>The answer was, that they should not pass at all; 
 <s n="2227"/>for they relied on their strongholds. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.3" sentences="3">
 <s n="2228"/>Then Timesitheos told the Hellenes that the tribe on the farther side was an enemy of these, 
 <s n="2229"/>so it was thought well to invite them to an alliance, if they wished. 
 <s n="2230"/>Timesitheos was therefore sent, and brought back their chiefs. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.4" sentences="1">
 <s n="2231"/>The Hellenic captains and the chiefs then met. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.5" sentences="4">
 <s n="2232"/>Xenophon spoke, with Timesitheos as interpreter:
 <s n="2233"/> “Gentlemen of the Mossynoicoi, we wish to return safely to Hellas; 
 <s n="2234"/>we must go by land, for we have no ships, 
 <s n="2235"/>and those people are in the way, who we hear are enemies of yours. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.6" sentences="1">
 <s n="2236"/>Then if it is your pleasure, you may have us for allies and punish them for any wrong they have done you, and make them obedient to you for the future. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.7" sentences="1">
 <s n="2237"/>If you let this chance go, consider where you could get such a power to help you again.” 
</section>
<section n="5.4.8" sentences="1">
 <s n="2238"/>The chief of the Mossynoicoi replied that they were in agreement and welcomed the alliance. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.9" sentences="1">
 <s n="2239"/>“Very well,” said Xenophon. “How will you wish to use us if we become allies? And how will you be able to help us on our way home?” 
</section>
<section n="5.4.10" sentences="1">
 <s n="2240"/>They said, “We are able to invade the country of our enemies and yours on the other side, and to send you vessels here, and men to support you and guide you through the country.” 
</section>
<section n="5.4.11" sentences="2">
 <s n="2241"/>On these terms they exchanged pledges and went. 
 <s n="2242"/>Next day they returned with three hundred dugouts and three men in each; two disembarked and joined the ranks, the third remained. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="2243"/>And these paddled off their boats, while the others formed up as I will describe. 
 <s n="2244"/>They stood in rows opposite each other, about a hundred in each; all carried wicker shields faced with hairy white oxhide, shaped like an ivy leaf, and in the right hand a lance of six cubits or so, with a blade in front and a round wooden knob behind. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.13" sentences="2">
 <s n="2245"/>They wore short shirts not quite reaching to the knees, about as thick as sackcloth; 
 <s n="2246"/>and on their heads caps of skin like the Paphlagonians, with a tuft in the middle very much the shape of the tiara. They had also battle-axes of iron. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.14" sentences="1">
 <s n="2247"/>Next, one led and the rest all followed singing in rhythm, and passing through the ranks of the Hellenes and their camp, they set out straight for the enemy to attack a stronghold which they thought most open to attack. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.15" sentences="3">
 <s n="2248"/>This lay in front of the city which they call their metropolis, where is the highest fortress of the Wigwam-lodgers. 
 <s n="2249"/>This was really what the war was about; 
 <s n="2250"/>for whoever held it were considered to be masters of all the Wigwam-lodgers; those who then held it (so people said) had no right to it, as it was common property which they had seized out of greediness. 
</section>



<section n="5.4.16" sentences="3">
 <s n="2251"/>Some of the Hellenes followed these for plunder, not on duty. 
 <s n="2252"/>As they approached the enemy did nothing at first; but when they were close to the stronghold they rushed out and routed them and killed a number of the natives and a few Hellenes. 
 <s n="2253"/>They pursued until they saw the Hellenes coming up in support. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.17" sentences="1">
 <s n="2254"/>Then they retreated, and cutting off the heads of the dead, shook them at the Hellenes and their own enemies, and off they went singing some tune or other. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.18" sentences="2">
 <s n="2255"/>The Hellenes were very indignant that these men had made the enemy bolder, and that those of themselves who had been with them ran away, although they were a good many: 
 <s n="2256"/> a thing which had never happened before in the whole expedition. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.19" sentences="3">
 <s n="2257"/>Xenophon assembled the Hellenes and said:
 <s n="2258"/> “Soldiers, do not be downhearted at what has happened. 
 <s n="2259"/>There is as much good as bad in it, let me tell you. 
  
</section>
<section n="5.4.20" sentences="3">
 <s n="2260"/>First, you knew that our guides are really enemies to the people who must be our enemies too.
 <s n="2261"/>Next, you know that those who were careless of discipline and union with us, and thought they could do the same with natives by their side as they can do with us by their side, have been punished for that: 
 <s n="2262"/>in future, then, they will be less likely to be slack in discipline again. 

</section>
<section n="5.4.21" sentences="1">
 <s n="2263"/>Now then, your part is now to prepare yourselves to show the friendly natives that you are better than they are, and to show the enemy that it is not the same thing to fight disciplined men as to fight a disorderly mob.” 
</section>
<section n="5.4.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="2264"/>That day, then, they stayed there. 
 <s n="2265"/>On the next day they sacrificed with good omens; and so after breakfasting, they formed the companies in column and posted the natives on the left in the same order and marched. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.23" sentences="4">
 <s n="2266"/>The bowmen were between the companies, a little behind the head of the heavy-armed men, because some of the enemy’s most active troops ran down and pelted them with stones. 
 <s n="2267"/>These were held up by the bowmen and the targeteers,  
 <s n="2268"/> and the others marched along steadily against the stronghold, where on the day before the natives and those with them were routed.
 <s n="2269"/>There the enemy were drawn up to meet them.
</section>
<section n="5.4.24" sentences="2">
 <s n="2270"/>The natives stood up to the targeteers and fought them, but when the heavy troops came near they turned; 
 <s n="2271"/>the targeteers chased them right up to the town, and the heavies followed in column. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.25" sentences="1">
 <s n="2272"/>As soon as they were up beside the houses of the metropolis all the enemy massed together and fought, casting their light lances, and holding other spears very heavy and thick, each almost too heavy for a man; and they tried to keep them off with these hand to hand. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.26" sentences="2">
 <s n="2273"/>But the Hellenes did not yield; they went pushing on, and then the natives turned to flight and all left the stronghold. 
 <s n="2274"/>But their king sat still in his mossyn, or wigwam lodge, which was built on the top of the height: there he stays and there they keep him at the public expense. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.27" sentences="2">
 <s n="2275"/>The Hellenes ransacked the places, and found in the houses stores of loaves in piles, last year’s loaves, as the Wigwam-lodgers said, and the fresh corn stored in the straw; 
 <s n="2276"/>  most of it was spilt.
</section>
<section n="5.4.28" sentences="1">
 <s n="2277"/>Besides, slices of dolphin were found in large jars, dried, and dolphin blubber in pottles, which the Wigwam-lodgers used as we use olive oil. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.29" sentences="3">
 <s n="2278"/>Plenty of nuts were in the attics, the broad ones with no division. 
 <s n="2279"/>Most of these and most of the corn they boiled and then baked into bread. 
 <s n="2280"/>Wine also was found, which when neat tasted very sharp from its dryness, but with water it was fragrant and pleasant. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.30" sentences="2">
 <s n="2281"/>Then the Hellenes took breakfast and marched on, leaving the stronghold to the Wigwam-lodgers who had fought with them. 
 <s n="2282"/>They passed many strongholds of those on the enemy side. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.31" sentences="4">
 <s n="2283"/>And these were most of them. 
 <s n="2284"/>The cities were eight or ten miles apart, more or less; 
 <s n="2285"/>when they shouted they could hear each other from city to city, so high were the hills, 
 <s n="2286"/>so deep the valleys. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.32" sentences="1">
 <s n="2287"/>When they were among friends in this march the people held shows for them of rich men’s children, fatted children fed on boiled chestnuts, tender and very white, and almost as broad as they were long, with backs and breasts variegated and tattooed all over in flower patterns. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.33" sentences="3">
 <s n="2288"/>They ran after the women in the camp and wanted to lie with them in broad daylight, 
 <s n="2289"/>which was their own custom. 
 <s n="2290"/>All the men and women were fair-skinned. 
</section>
<section n="5.4.34" sentences="2">
 <s n="2291"/>The army said that these were the most savage of all they had seen in their travels, and the farthest away from Hellenic customs. 
 <s n="2292"/>They would do in public what human beings would do in private, and when they were alone they did what people do in company, talk to themselves and laugh at themselves, stop and dance anywhere as if they were showing off. 
</section>
<section n="5.5.1" sentences="2">
 <s n="2293"/>Through this country, hostile and friendly, the Hellenes marched eight stages, and then came to the Chalybes or Ironmen. 
 <s n="2294"/>These are few, and subject to the Wigwam-lodgers, and most of their living comes from working iron. 
</section>
<section n="5.5.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="2295"/>Then they reached the Tibarenians. 
 <s n="2296"/>Their country was more level, and they had forts near the sea, not so strong. 
 <s n="2297"/>The captains wished to attack these places and get a bit in pocket, so they refused the gifts which were offered and told them to wait until they had deliberated. 
</section>
<section n="5.5.3" sentences="2">
 <s n="2298"/>They sacrificed, and the seers after many sacrifices declared one and all that the gods did not at all approve of war. 
 <s n="2299"/>Then they accepted the gifts of friendship, and marching as through friendly country for two days reached Cotyora, a Hellenic city, a colony of Sinope, which is in the Tibyrenian country. 
</section>
<section n="5.5.4" sentences="2">
 <s n="2300"/><note n="nottranslated"/> 
 <s n="2301"/><note n="nottranslated"/> 
</section>
<section n="5.5.5" sentences="2">

<s n="2302"/>They stayed forty-five days. 
<s n="2303"/>In this time they offered sacrifices to the gods, and made procession, each nation of the Hellenic race by itself, and held games and sports.
</section>
<section n="5.5.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="2304"/>They got their provisions partly from Paphlagonia,
 <s n="2305"/>partly from the villages of the district, for they would neither give them a market nor allow the sick to be brought within their walls.
</section>

<section n="5.5.7" sentences="3">
 <s n="2306"/>After a while an embassy came from Sinope; Cotyora was their city and paid them tribute,
 <s n="2307"/>so they were anxious about the city, and the country also, which they heard was being raided.
 <s n="2308"/>They came to the camp for a parley; their spokesman was Hecatonymos, reputed to be an accomplished orator, and he said:
</section>

<section n="5.5.8" sentences="1">
 <s n="2309"/>“We are sent to you, soldiers and gentlemen, by the city of Sinope, to congratulate you Hellenes as victors over barbarians; and to say we rejoice with you that, after so many great dangers, as we have heard, you are with us safe and sound.”
</section>

<section n="5.5.9" sentences="2">
 <s n="2310"/>We are Hellenes, and you are Hellenes,
 <s n="2311"/>and we expect from you no unkindness but some kindness.
</section>

<section n="5.5.10" sentences="3">
 <s n="2312"/>These men of Cotyora are colonists of ours; this country we have taken from barbarians and given to them,
 <s n="2313"/>and they in return pay us tribute like Cerasfis and Trapezûs,
 <s n="2314"/>so that if you do any harm to them the city of Sinope considers this as done to herself.
</section>

<section n="5.5.11" sentences="1">
 <s n="2315"/>Now we hear that some of you have violently entered the city and there made yourselves at home in the houses, and that you take what you want from the farms by force, not by consent.
</section>

<section n="5.5.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="2316"/>We do not think that right.
 <s n="2317"/>If you will do that, we must needs make friends with Corylas and the Paphlagonians and anyone else we can find.
</section>

<section n="5.5.13" sentences="3">
 <s n="2318"/>In answer to this, Xenophon rose and spoke for the army:
 <s n="2319"/>“We also, gentlemen of Sinope, are well content that we have come through with our lives and our arms all safe; 
 <s n="2320"/>for it is impossible at one time both to fight our enemies and to plunder their goods and chattels.
</section>

<section n="5.5.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="2321"/>And now we have reached the cities of our own race.
 <s n="2322"/>Well, at Trapezûs a market was given us, and we paid for our provisions, and for their good respect and the hospitable gifts they gave to the army we respected them in return.
</section>

<section n="5.5.15" sentences="2">
 <s n="2323"/>Ask them what manner of men they found us;
 <s n="2324"/>for some of them are here, whom that city sent with us in friendship as guides.
</section>

<section n="5.5.16" sentences="1">
 <s n="2325"/>Wherever we come and get no market, whether barbarian country or Hellenic, we take our provisions, not from brutality but from necessity.
</section>

<section n="5.5.17" sentences="1">
 <s n="2326"/>Kurds and Taochians and Chaldeans, although they are not subjects of the king, and although they are formidable, we treated as enemies because of the necessity of getting provisions, since they would give no market.
</section>

<section n="5.5.18" sentences="1">
 <s n="2327"/>The Macronians were not able to provide a market, but although they are barbarians, we considered them to be friends, and took from them nothing by force.
</section>

<section n="5.5.19" sentences="3">
 <s n="2328"/>But these men of Cotyora, who you say are yours—if we have taken anything from them, they are responsible themselves;
 <s n="2329"/>for they did not behave as friends to us, but they closed their gates.
 <s n="2330"/>They refused to let us in and sent no market out.
</section>

<section n="5.5.20" sentences="2">
 <s n="2331"/>You say we went in by force and made ourselves at home there; but we asked them to receive our sick and wounded under shelter,
 <s n="2332"/>and when they would not open their gates, we walked in where the place itself gave us leave.
</section>

<section n="5.5.21" sentences="1">
 <s n="2333"/>The rest of us, as you see, are encamped under the sky, and in military order, prepared to defend themselves if attacked, and to treat as friends those who treat us as friends.
</section>

<section n="5.5.22" sentences="3">
 <s n="2334"/>You have threatened to make Corylas and the Paphlagonians allies against us if it is your pleasure;
 <s n="2335"/>well, we will fight you both, if there is any necessity.
 <s n="2336"/>We have already fought others many times more numerous than you.
</section>

<section n="5.5.24" sentences="3">
 <s n="2337"/>It was clear after this that the other ambassadors were very angry with Hecatonymos for what he said;
 <s n="2338"/>and one of them rose and said: “We have come, not to declare war, but to prove we are friends.
 <s n="2339"/>If you will come to Sinope, we will receive you there with hospitable gifts; and now we will ask the inhabitants to give what they can, for we see that all you have said is true.”
</section>

<section n="5.5.25" sentences="1">
 <s n="2340"/>Then the people of Cotyora sent gifts of hospitality, and the captains entertained the ambassadors of Sinope; all talked together in the most friendly way; and particularly each inquired what the others wanted about the rest of their march.
</section>

<section n="5.6.1" sentences="7">
 <s n="2341"/>On this day the matter ended so;
 <s n="2342"/>and on the next the captains called a meeting of the soldiers.
 <s n="2343"/>They decided to call in the ambassadors and consult about their march onwards.
 <s n="2344"/>If they must go by land, they thought friends at Sinope might be useful, 
 <s n="2345"/>since they knew all about Paphlagonia;
 <s n="2346"/>they would need them all the more if they went by sea.
 <s n="2347"/>For only they seemed able to provide vessels enough for the army.
</section>

<section n="5.6.2" sentences="1">
 <s n="2348"/>Accordingly they invited the ambassadors to give their counsel, and they called on them, as fellow-countrymen, to begin their good entertainment by showing goodwill and giving good advice.
</section>
<section n="5.6.3" sentences="2">
 <s n="2349"/>Hecatonymos rose first, and gave an apology for what he had said about making friends with the Paphlagonian; he did not mean they were going to make war upon the Hellenes, but only that it was in their power to make friends with the barbarians, and they chose the Hellenes.
 <s n="2350"/>Since they asked his advice, he called thus upon Heaven:
</section>

<section n="5.6.4" sentences="4">
 <s n="2351"/>"If I give advice which seems to me best, may many blessings come to me;
 <s n="2352"/>if not, may the reverse befall.
 <s n="2353"/>For I believe that this sacred duty of advice, as it is called, is present with me now;
 <s n="2354"/>for if I am seen to have advised well, many will praise me, but if badly, many will curse me."
</section>

<section n="5.6.5" sentences="4">
 <s n="2355"/>"I know that there will be much more trouble for us if you go by sea, for we shall have to provide the vessels;
 <s n="2356"/>but if you journey by land, you will have to be the fighters.
 <s n="2357"/>Still, I must speak what I know;
 <s n="2358"/>for I know the country."
</section>

<section n="5.6.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="2359"/>"I know the Paphlagonian country and its power.
 <s n="2360"/>It has both splendid plains and most lofty mountains."
</section>

<section n="5.6.7" sentences="4">
 <s n="2361"/>"And in the first place I know exactly where it is necessary to make entry.
 <s n="2362"/>No other entry is possible, except one where the horns of the mountains are high above each side of the road; get hold of these, and a few men could keep the pass,
 <s n="2363"/>but if these are held, all the men in the world would be unable to pass.
 <s n="2364"/>I could show you the place, if you would send someone with me."
</section>

<section n="5.6.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="2365"/>"Secondly, I know that there are plains, and cavalry which the barbarians themselves believe to be stronger than all the king’s cavalry.
 <s n="2366"/>Just lately the king summoned them and they would not go; their governor is too proud."
</section>

<section n="5.6.9" sentences="5">
 <s n="2367"/>"And even if you can steal your way through, or seize the heights first, and on the plain if you conquer their horse, and their foot more than a hundred and twenty thousand, you will come to the rivers."
 <s n="2368"/>"First, the Thermodon, 300 feet wide, which I believe to be difficult to cross, especially in the face of so many enemies;
 <s n="2369"/>second, the Iris, also 300 feet;
 <s n="2370"/>third, the Halys, not less than a quarter of a mile, which I think you could not cross without boats—and who will give you boats?
 <s n="2371"/>So also the Parthenis is impassable,
</section>

<section n="5.6.10" sentences="5">
 <s n="2372"/>and you will come to that if you cross the Halys.
 <s n="2373"/>Therefore I think the land way is not only difficult for you but wholly impossible.
 <s n="2374"/>But if you go by water you can coast along to Sinope, and from Sinope to Heracleia;
 <s n="2375"/>from Heracleia neither land nor water is impracticable,
 <s n="2376"/>for there is plenty of shipping at Heracleia."
</section>

<section n="5.6.11" sentences="5">
 <s n="2377"/>When he had finished, some suspected he spoke in friendship for Corylas, 
  <s n="2378"/>for he was his national friend at Sinope.
 <s n="2379"/>Some, that he expected a handsome reward for this advice.
 <s n="2380"/>Some suspected that he feared they might damage the Sinopean country if they went by land.
 <s n="2381"/>But in any case the public vote was in favour of going by sea.
</section>

<section n="5.6.12" sentences="5">
 <s n="2382"/>After this Xenophon said,
 <s n="2383"/>“Gentlemen of Sinope, the men have chosen the journey by sea which you advise.
 <s n="2384"/>Then the matter stands thus:
 <s n="2385"/>if there shall be shipping enough, so that not one shall be left here, we will go by water;
 <s n="2386"/>but if some of us are to be left and some sail, we will not go on board."
</section>

<section n="5.6.13" sentences="3">
 <s n="2387"/>"For we understand that where we are masters, there we can get provisions and save our lives too;
 <s n="2388"/>but if we shall be caught anywhere weaker than our enemies, it is plain as daylight that we shall be so many slaves.”
 <s n="2389"/>On hearing this, the ambassadors asked them to send men to deal with the question.
</section>

<section n="5.6.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="2390"/>They chose Callimachos the Arcadian, and Ariston the Athenian, and Samolas the Achaian;
 <s n="2391"/>and they departed.
</section>

<section n="5.6.15" sentences="1">
 <s n="2392"/>Meanwhile Xenophon looked upon all these men-at-arms, and all those targeteers, and the bowmen and slingers and horsemen, too, and all fit from long practice—he saw all these on the Euxine, where so great a force could never have been collected without vast expense, and he thought it would be fine to found a city there, and to add territory and power to Hellas.
</section>


<section n="5.6.16" sentences="2">
 <s n="2393"/>It would be a grand place, he thought, when he calculated their numbers and all the people living about that sea.
 <s n="2394"/>But before he told anyone, he called up Silanos the Ambraciot, who had been seer to Cyros, and made sacrifice in consideration of this design.
</section>

<section n="5.6.17" sentences="1">
 <s n="2395"/>But Silanos was afraid this might be done, and the army might remain somewhere; so he spread the tale through the army that Xenophon wished the army to remain and to found a city, and get a great name and power for himself.
</section>

<section n="5.6.18" sentences="2">
 <s n="2396"/>Silanos wanted to get back to Hellas as soon as possible; for he had kept safe the 3,000 darics which Cyros gave him,
 <s n="2397"/>when he was right about the ten days at that sacrifice.<note>See p. 23.</note>
</section>

<section n="5.6.19" sentences="3">
 <s n="2398"/>Some of the soldiers were willing to obey when they heard the tale, but most were not.
 <s n="2399"/>Timasion the Dardanian and Thorax the Boeotian told some merchants from Sinope and Heracleia who were present that if they would not find pay for the men, so that they could get food on board ship, there was a risk that all this great force might remain on the Euxine.
 <s n="2400"/>“Xenophon wants it,” they said, “and he begs us to say suddenly to the men when the vessels are here.
</section>

<section n="5.6.20" sentences="2">
 <s n="2401"/>‘Look here, men, you see we are in a fix now to get provisions for the voyage home, and a few little presents for our people when we get home. 
 <s n="2402"/>If you like, just choose any place you fancy in the inhabited country round the sea here, where you would like to settle. Then go home if you like, stay if you like: there are your ships, choose wherever you like and just pounce on the place.’”
 
</section>

<section n="5.6.21" sentences="3">
 <s n="2403"/>The merchants reported this rumour in their cities;
 <s n="2404"/>Timasion the Dardanian sent two men, Eurymachos the Dardanian and Thorax the Boeotian, to say the same.
 <s n="2405"/>Then the people of those two cities sent someone with a present for Timasion, if he would arrange that the army should sail.
</section>

<section n="5.6.22" sentences="3">
 <s n="2406"/>Timasion was pleased to hear this, and when the soldiers were in assembly he spoke to them:
 <s n="2407"/>“Don’t set your hearts on staying here, men: Hellas and home!
 <s n="2408"/>There’s nothing better than that. But I hear some people are looking for lucky omens to stay here, without one word to you.”
</section>

<section n="5.6.23" sentences="3">
 <s n="2409"/>“I promise you as soon as you are on board—pay, from the first of the month, a cyzicene<note>a gold coin rather larger than the daric.</note> per man per month!
 <s n="2410"/>and I will bring you to the Troad, my own country, although I’m an exile, 
 <s n="2411"/>but they will be glad to see me and they will back you up.
</section>

<section n="5.6.24" sentences="2">
 <s n="2412"/>I will personally lead you and show you where you may find plenty of wealth,
 <s n="2413"/>for I know the Aeolis, Phrygia, the Troad, and all the satrapy of Pharnabazus, partly because I am from there, partly because I served with Clearchos and Dercylidas in it."
</section>

<section n="5.6.25" sentences="2">
 <s n="2414"/>Then Thorax the Boeotian got up next, who had a quarrel with Xenophon for the chief command; he said that once out of the Euxine there was the Chersonese<note>Gallipoli</note> ready for them,
 <s n="2415"/>a fine prosperous country if anyone wanted to settle there, or he might go home if he liked. When there was country plenty and to spare in Hellas, it was ridiculous to go poking about among the barbarians.
</section>


<section n="5.6.26" sentences="2">
 <s n="2416"/>“Until you get there,” says he, “I promise you the same pay as Timasion.”
 <s n="2417"/>This he said because he knew what the merchants had promised to Timasion if he cleared them out.
</section>

<section n="5.6.27" sentences="3">
 <s n="2418"/>Meanwhile Xenophon said nothing;
 <s n="2419"/>but two Achaians, Philesios and Lycon, said it was a shame that Xenophon privately advised people to stay, and looked for omens to persuade them to stay, and in public he would say nothing.
 <s n="2420"/>Thus Xenophon was obliged to get up and speak. He said:
</section>

<section n="5.6.28" sentences="2">
 <s n="2421"/>“Gentlemen, I make sacrifice, it is true, and seek omens as you see, to my best ability, for your sakes and for mine, to procure this:
 <s n="2422"/>I speak and think and do what shall be best for yourselves, and best for me too. And now I have been inquiring on this very point, whether it is better that I should propose it and try to bring it off, or whether I should not touch the matter at all.
</section>

<section n="5.6.29" sentences="4">
 <s n="2423"/>Silanos the seer declared that the omens were good, and that’s the chief thing.
 <s n="2424"/>He knew I could tell myself, since I have been present at many a sacrifice.
 <s n="2425"/>But he reported that the victim showed some plot and intrigue against me.
 <s n="2426"/>He discovered this really because he was plotting himself to spread slanders against me; for he sent word round that I meant to do this at once without your consent.
</section>

<section n="5.6.30" sentences="1">
 <s n="2427"/>But the truth is, if I saw you in difficulties, I should have inquired how it could be managed that you should take a city; when those who wished might sail at once, and the others might wait until they could get enough to put something in their friends’ pockets at home.
</section>

<section n="5.6.31" sentences="1">
 <s n="2428"/>But now that I see the merchants from Heracleia and Sinope are sending ships to carry you, and people are promising pay from the first of the month, it seems a good chance to come safe where we wish, and be paid for it at the same time. Therefore I give up that plan, and I ask any who have agreed with me to give it up too.
</section>

<section n="5.6.32" sentences="4">
 <s n="2429"/>This is my opinion:
 <s n="2430"/>While you are here, so many together, I think you will be respected and have your provisions;
 <s n="2431"/>for power gives plunder of those that are under!
 <s n="2432"/>But if you are scattered, and your strength is broken into little bits, you could not take and you could not come off scot-free.
</section>

<section n="5.6.33" sentences="2">
 <s n="2433"/>I agree, then, with you that you should sail for Hellas, but if anyone be found deserting before the whole army is safe, he shall be judged as a criminal.
 <s n="2434"/>Those who approve hold up your hands.”
</section>

<section n="5.6.34" sentences="3">
 <s n="2435"/>All held up their hands.
 <s n="2436"/>But Silanos rose, and tried to say it was fair that anyone should go if he liked.
 <s n="2437"/>But the men would not let him; they threatened that if they caught him showing his heels he should be punished.
</section>

<section n="5.6.35" sentences="1">
 <s n="2438"/>The ships were sent at once from Heracleia as soon as they knew that the army had voted to go, and that Xenophon himself put it to the vote; but they broke their promise to Timasion and Thorax that they would find the money.
</section>

<section n="5.6.36" sentences="2">
 <s n="2439"/>These men were confounded; they had promised the pay to the soldiers, and they feared the army.
 <s n="2440"/>Accordingly they gathered the other captains who had been in the secret—these were all except Neon, who was second-in-command to Cheirisophos, and Cheirisophos was absent still—and waited on Xenophon. They told him they were sorry, and they thought it best to sail to Phasis, as they had the ships, and take the Phasian country;
</section>

<section n="5.6.37" sentences="5">
 <s n="2441"/>the king there was descended from Aietes, as it happened.
 <s n="2442"/>Xenophon replied that he would not say one word to the army on this matter.
 <s n="2443"/>“Assemble them yourselves, if you like,” says he, “and tell them.”
 <s n="2444"/>Then Timasion suggested that they should not call a meeting but each try to persuade his own men.
 <s n="2445"/>So they departed and did this.
</section>

<section n="5.7.1" sentences="2">
 <s n="2446"/>Soon the soldiers heard of all this confusion.
 <s n="2447"/>Neon declared that Xenophon had talked over the other captains, and intended to take the men deceitfully back to Phasis.
</section>

<section n="5.7.2" sentences="2">
 <s n="2448"/>The soldiers were indignant; they gathered in clumps and circles, and there was danger that they might do as they did to the Colchian heralds and masters of the market—
 <s n="2449"/>stoned them all except those who jumped into the sea.
 
</section>

<section n="5.7.3" sentences="2">
 <s n="2450"/>But when Xenophon found out this, he decided to summon a general assembly at once, and not to let them gather in groups by themselves.
 <s n="2451"/>So he told the herald to cry meeting;
</section>


<section n="5.7.4" sentences="2">
 <s n="2452"/>and the men all assembled readily enough.
 <s n="2453"/>Then Xenophon did not denounce the captains for applying to him, but spoke as follows:
</section>

<section n="5.7.5" sentences="3">
 <s n="2454"/>“Men, I hear that someone is calumniating me, and saying that I mean to deceive you, and to sail for Phasis.
 <s n="2455"/>Hear me, then, in God’s name. If I am proved guilty, I must not leave this place until I am punished; but if these are proved guilty who slander me, treat them as they deserve.
 <s n="2456"/>But you know that I cannot be the one who deceives you, for we are all heading towards the same sun that rises and sets in the west."
</section>

<section n="5.7.6" sentences="3">
 <s n="2457"/>“You know, I suppose, where the sun rises and where he sets.
 <s n="2458"/>You know that if one is going to sail for Hellas he must steer westwards; if one wishes to sail for barbarian lands he must go back to the east.
 <s n="2459"/>Then who could deceive you in that? Who could persuade you that the sun sets where he rises, and rises where he sets?
</section>

<section n="5.7.7" sentences="2">
 <s n="2460"/>And further, you know that the north wind takes you out of the Euxine to Hellas, and the south wind takes you in to Phasis.
 <s n="2461"/>When the north wind blows, don’t they say, Good voyage for Hellas now! Can anyone deceive you in this, then, and make you embark when the south wind blows?
</section>

<section n="5.7.8" sentences="3">
 <s n="2462"/>Perhaps you think I will put you on board when it is calm.
 <s n="2463"/>Well, I shall be in one ship, and you in a hundred at least; then how can I compel you to sail with me if you don’t want to do it?
 <s n="2464"/>How could I deceive you and take you off?
</section>

<section n="5.7.9" sentences="5">
 <s n="2465"/>But I will put it that you are deceived and bewitched by me, and you do come to Phasis;
 <s n="2466"/>suppose we do disembark in the country—you will know that this is not Hellas, I suppose?
 <s n="2467"/>And I the deceiver shall be one: you the deceived nearly ten thousand armed men.
 <s n="2468"/>Could a man better make sure of his own punishment than by such a plan for himself and for you?
 <s n="2469"/>No, these are the words of men, who are both fools and jealous of me because you respect me.
</section>

<section n="5.7.10" sentences="6">
 <s n="2470"/>No, these are the words of men, who are both fools and jealous of me because you respect me.
 <s n="2471"/>However, they could not fairly find anything in me to make them jealous.
 <s n="2472"/>When do I hinder any of you from speaking, if you have anything good to say?
 <s n="2473"/>From fighting if he will for you and himself?
 <s n="2474"/>From watching and caring for your safety?
 <s n="2475"/>Come now, when you elect commanders, do I stand in the way of anyone?

<section n="5.7.11" sentences="2">

 <s n="2477"/>but you—if anyone believes that he could have been deceived in these things, or that he could deceive another, let him speak, and say how.

<section n="5.7.12" sentences="2">

 <s n="2479"/>If it shall come upon us, and if it shall be what it gives signs of being, it is high time to be careful for ourselves, or we may prove to be the basest and worst of men in the sight of heaven and earth, in the sight of friends and enemies.

<section n="5.7.13" sentences="3">
 <s n="2480"/>When the soldiers heard this they wondered what it could be, and told him to speak.

<section n="5.7.16" sentences="2">

<section n="5.7.17" sentences="3">

<section n="5.7.26" sentences="3">
 <s n="2508"/>Now what do you think about these?

<section n="5.7.27" sentences="2">
 <s n="2511"/>You as a whole will not have it in your power either to undertake war or to cease it at your own pleasure, but any single person will lead the army wherever he likes.
 <s n="2512"/>And if any ambassadors wait on you, asking for peace or anything else, those who wish will kill them, and keep you from hearing those who come to speak to you.

<section n="5.7.28" sentences="1">
 <s n="2513"/>Next, those whom you as a whole elect for commanders will be simply nowhere; but whatever single person elects himself commander and chooses to say, ‘Shoot ’em, shoot ’em,’ he will be able to kill the commander, and any private person among you, if he likes, untried, if he finds followers, as did happen just now.
</section>

<section n="5.7.29" sentences="3">
 <s n="2514"/>Just consider what these self-elected captains have done for you.
 <s n="2515"/>If Zelarchos the market master has done wrong, he has gone off unpunished;

<section n="5.7.30" sentences="4">

 <s n="2518"/>and those dead whom the very men who killed them offered for burial—for those dead they have arranged matters so, that even a man with a herald’s badge will not find it safe to remove them.

<section n="5.7.31" sentences="1">

<section n="5.7.32" sentences="2">
 <s n="2522"/>However, if any of you think that these are the doings of wild beasts, not of human beings, see how you can stop them.

<section n="5.7.33" sentences="4">

<section n="5.8.14" sentences="2">

<section n="5.8.15" sentences="2">

<section n="5.8.24" sentences="2">

<section n="5.8.25" sentences="1">

<section n="6.1.1" sentences="3">

<section n="6.1.5" sentences="3">

<section n="6.1.6" sentences="4">

 <s n="2620"/>but he had no harm.
 <s n="2621"/>After this, the Ainianians and Magnesians rose, and danced in full armour what they call the Harvest Song.

<section n="6.1.7" sentences="1">
 <s n="2622"/>The method of dancing is, that one lays down his arms, and sows, and drives the plow, turning again and again as in fear.
</section>

<section n="6.1.8" sentences="4">
 <s n="2623"/>A raider approaches; the other looks out, and picks up his arms, and goes to meet him, and fights in front of the yoke.
 <s n="2624"/>These also did their play in time with the pipes.
 <s n="2625"/>At last the raider ties up his man and drives the oxen away;

<section n="6.1.9" sentences="3">

<section n="6.1.18" sentences="3">
 <s n="2651"/>They thought that the best thing would be to choose one commander;
 <s n="2652"/>for one man could manage the army better than many, and use it by night or day;
 <s n="2653"/>if they had to be secret, things would be hidden better; and if they must be first, one is less likely to be too late, for there would be no need of discussion, but the opinion of the one would be carried out.

<section n="6.1.19" sentences="2">

<section n="6.1.20" sentences="1">
 <s n="2656"/>Xenophon was inclined for many reasons to accept. He thought that his honour would be greater among his friends, and his reputation would be greater in his native city, and perhaps it might benefit the army.

<section n="6.1.21" sentences="2">
 <s n="2657"/>These thoughts made him wish to be sole plenipotentiary commander.

<section n="6.1.22" sentences="3">
 <s n="2659"/>In his perplexity he thought it best to lay the matter before the gods above and let them decide.
 <s n="2660"/>He provided two victims, and made sacrifice to Zeus the King, the god to whom the oracle at Delphi had bidden him sacrifice.
 <s n="2661"/>He it was who had sent the dream, so he believed, which he saw when he began to take part in the business of the army.
</section>

<section n="6.1.23" sentences="4">
 <s n="2662"/>And at the time when he set out from Ephesus to be introduced to Cyros, he remembered an eagle which had screamed on the right, yet still sitting;
 <s n="2663"/>and the seer who was escorting him said that this was a great omen, and proper for a great man, full of glory and yet full of hardship,
 <s n="2664"/>for the other birds are most ready to attack the eagle when sitting;
 <s n="2665"/>however, not an omen of profit, for the eagle takes his provender on the wing rather than sitting.
</section>

<section n="6.1.24" sentences="2">
 <s n="2666"/>When he made this sacrifice, the god signified quite clearly that he was neither to ask for further command nor accept it if they should elect him.
 <s n="2667"/>This was how the matter was resolved.
</section>

<section n="6.1.25" sentences="3">
 <s n="2668"/>So things were when the army assembled, and all voted to elect one;
 <s n="2669"/>when this was decided, they proposed Xenophon.
 <s n="2670"/>Since it was clear that they meant to elect him, if it were put to the vote, he rose and spoke as follows:
</section>

<section n="6.1.26" sentences="3">
 <s n="2671"/>“Gentlemen, I am but human, and I cannot help being pleased to be honoured by you.
 <s n="2672"/>I thank you, and I pray the gods to grant that I may be the cause of some good for you.
 <s n="2673"/>But to prefer me as your commander when a man of Lacedaimon is present seems to be neither profitable to you nor to me: you would be less likely to win anything from them if you have need; and for me, I think such a thing is not very safe.
</section>

<section n="6.1.27" sentences="1">
 <s n="2674"/>For I see that they never ceased fighting against my own native city, until the whole city was compelled to admit that Lacedaimon was the leader:
</section>

<section n="6.1.28" sentences="2">
 <s n="2675"/>But as soon as they did admit that, they ceased fighting at once and besieged the city no longer.
 <s n="2676"/>Then, if seeing this I should annul this high claim of theirs, as far as in me lies, I have a notion I might be taught a lesson too quickly.
</section>

<section n="6.1.29" sentences="3">
 <s n="2677"/>As to your own notion, that there would be less faction with me commander than with many, be sure that if you elect another you will not find me leading a faction;
 <s n="2678"/>for I think that he who leads faction against his commander in war leads faction against his own life.
 <s n="2679"/>But if you elect me, I should not be surprised if you should find someone angry both with you and with me.”
</section>

<section n="6.1.30" sentences="5">
 <s n="2680"/>When he finished many more got up and said that he ought to be commander;
 <s n="2681"/>and Agasias the Stymphalian said it would be ridiculous if things really were as he described.
 <s n="2682"/>“Will Lacedaimon fly into a rage if a dinner party does not put a Lacedaimonian in the chair?
 <s n="2683"/>If things really are so,” says he, “we can’t be officers, because we are Arcadians!”
 <s n="2684"/>Loud applause followed; they thought that good.
</section>

<section n="6.1.31" sentences="3">
 <s n="2685"/>Xenophon saw that something more was necessary, and he rose and said, “Well, gentlemen, I must tell you the whole truth, on my oath by all the gods and goddesses in heaven.
 <s n="2686"/>I made sacrifice to inquire whether it was better for you to entrust this command to me, and for me to accept;
 <s n="2687"/>and the gods gave such manifest signs in the victims, that even an ignoramus could see I must hold aloof from sole command!”
</section>

<section n="6.1.32" sentences="6">
 <s n="2688"/>After this they elected Cheirisophos;
 <s n="2689"/>and Cheirisophos, elected, rose up and said, 
 <s n="2690"/>“At least I will assure you, gentlemen, that I would have made no faction myself if you had elected another;
 <s n="2691"/>however, as regards Xenophon, you were kind to him not to elect him,
 <s n="2692"/>for as it is, Dexippos has been running him down to Anaxibios, although I told him to shut up.
 <s n="2693"/>He said Xenophon would rather share command of Clearchos’s force with a Dardanian, Timasion, than with himself, a Laconian.
</section>

<section n="6.1.33" sentences="5">
 <s n="2694"/>However, since you have chosen me,” says he, “I also will try my best to do you some good.
 <s n="2695"/>Then you must all make ready to set sail to-morrow, if there is good weather.
 <s n="2696"/>We shall sail to Heracleia, so you must do your best to make anchorage there.
 <s n="2697"/>What next, we will consider when we get there.”
 <s n="2698"/>With these instructions, the meeting ended.
</section>

<section n="6.2.1" sentences="3">
 <s n="2699"/>From that place next day they set sail before a good wind, and for two days they coasted.
 <s n="2700"/>As they sailed along the coast they had seen Jason’s Beach, where the Argo is said to have moored, and the mouths of the rivers: first Thermodon, then Iris, then Halys, then Parthenios.
 <s n="2701"/>Lastly they reached Heracleia, a Hellenic city and a colony of Megara, lying in the country of the Mariandynians.
</section>

<section n="6.2.2" sentences="1">
 <s n="2702"/>So they anchored by the Acherusian Chersonese, where Heracles is said to have gone down for the Dog Cerberos; and there they still show the marks of his descent, a cleft more than four hundred yards deep.
</section>

<section n="6.2.3" sentences="2">
 <s n="2703"/>There the people sent gifts of hospitality from Heracleia: barley meal three thousand measures, wine two thousand jars, twenty oxen, and a hundred sheep.
 <s n="2704"/>There is a river running across the plain, named Lycos, width about two hundred feet.
</section>

<section n="6.2.4" sentences="6">
 <s n="2705"/>The soldiers met and debated whether to go on by land or sea out of the Euxine.
 <s n="2706"/>Lycon the Achaian rose and said:
 <s n="2707"/> “I am surprised, gentlemen, at our commanders; they do not try to get provisions for us.
 <s n="2708"/>These gifts are not three days’ food for the army.
 <s n="2709"/>Where to get food for our journey I don’t know,” says he.
 <s n="2710"/>“I propose, then, that we demand from Heracleia not less than three thousand cyzicenes.”
</section>

<section n="6.2.5" sentences="2">
 <s n="2711"/>Another proposed not less than ten thousand.
 <s n="2712"/>Ambassadors should be chosen on the spot, and the army should stay while the ambassadors went to the city to see what offers were made, and decide accordingly.
</section>

<section n="6.2.6" sentences="4">
 <s n="2713"/>Then they put forward as ambassadors, first Cheirisophos, because he was commander, 
 <s n="2714"/>and some added Xenophon.
 <s n="2715"/>They opposed strongly;
 <s n="2716"/>both agreed that they ought not to force from a Hellenic city, and a friend, what they did not offer willingly.
</section>

<section n="6.2.7" sentences="3">
 <s n="2717"/>Since these two were not so minded they sent Lycon the Achaian, a Parrhasian Callimachos, and Agasias the Stymphalian.
 <s n="2718"/>Those went and told the resolutions.
 <s n="2719"/>Lycon added threats, as was said, if they should refuse.

<section n="6.2.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="2720"/>The Heracleots replied that they would deliberate.
 <s n="2721"/>At once they brought in everything from the farms, made their market inside, shut the gates, and arms could be seen on the walls.
</section>

<section n="6.2.9" sentences="3">
 <s n="2722"/>Then those who had made this mess laid the blame on their captains, who had spoilt the business, as they said.
 <s n="2723"/>The Arcadians and the Achaians joined forces,
  <s n="2724"/> under the ringleaders Callimachos and Lycon.
</section>

<section n="6.2.10" sentences="2">
 <s n="2725"/>They said it was a shame that Peloponnesians should be commanded by an Athenian and a Lacedaimonian who had brought no force to the army.
 <s n="2726"/>They had the work themselves, and others had the profits, and that, too, although they had saved the army themselves; Arcadians and Achaians had done it, the rest of the army was nothing (and in truth Arcadians and Achaians were more than half the army).
</section>

<section n="6.2.11" sentences="1">
 <s n="2727"/>Then if they had common sense, they would join and elect their own captains for themselves, and go on by themselves, and try to make a good job of it.
</section>

<section n="6.2.12" sentences="4">
 <s n="2728"/>This was decided.
 <s n="2729"/>Any Arcadians or Achaians that were under Cheirisophos and Xenophon left them;
 <s n="2730"/> they joined together, and elected captains from themselves, ten in number, and voted that each was to do what should be decided by a majority.
 <s n="2731"/>Thus the sole command of Cheirisophos was ended then and there, a week after he was elected.
</section>

<section n="6.2.13" sentences="2">
 <s n="2732"/>However, Xenophon wished to proceed in company with them, which he thought safer than that everyone should go by himself.
 <s n="2733"/>But Neon persuaded him to go separately; for Cheirisophos had told him that Cleandros, the governor at Byzantium, said he was coming with ships-of-war to Calpe’s Haven.

<section n="6.2.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="2734"/>He wanted no-one else to share, only they and their own men would have a passage home in these ships, and that was why he gave such advice.
 <s n="2735"/>Cheirisophos just let him do what he liked; he was in despair at the whole business, and hated the army.

<section n="6.2.15" sentences="2">

 <s n="2737"/>The god signified by the omens that he should go with the army.

<section n="6.2.16" sentences="2">
 <s n="2738"/>Thus the army was divided into three: Arcadians and Achaians, more than four thousand, all men-at-arms; Cheirisophos with fourteen hundred men-at-arms and seven hundred targeteers, Clearchos’s Thracians; and Xenophon with seventeen hundred men-at-arms, and three hundred targeteers—he alone had cavalry, about forty horsemen.

<section n="6.2.17" sentences="2">

<section n="6.2.18" sentences="2">

<section n="6.3.1" sentences="2">
 <s n="2746"/>We will follow each party.
 <s n="2747"/>The Arcadians landed by night in Calpe’s Haven, and marched to the first villages, about eight miles from the sea.

<section n="6.3.2" sentences="2">
 <s n="2748"/>At dawn, each captain led his own company to a village.
 <s n="2749"/>Two went together if the village appeared to be large.
</section>

<section n="6.3.3" sentences="2">
 <s n="2750"/>They arranged a rendezvous on a hill.
 <s n="2751"/>By their sudden assault, they took many captives and surrounded large quantities of sheep.
</section>

<section n="6.3.4" sentences="3">
 <s n="2752"/>But the Thracians who escaped gathered together;
 <s n="2753"/> and very many did escape with their light shields clean out of the hands of the heavy troops.
 <s n="2754"/>When these had assembled, first they attacked the company of Smicres, one of the captains, as it was retiring to the rendezvous laden with booty.

<section n="6.3.5" sentences="3">

 <s n="2756"/>From the company of Hegesandros, another of the ten captains, they left only eight men,
 <s n="2757"/> and the captain himself was saved alive.
</section>

<section n="6.3.6" sentences="4">
 <s n="2758"/>The other companies rendezvoused, some with difficulty and some without.
 <s n="2759"/>The Thracians, after this great success, went round shouting for help and collected in force during the night.
 <s n="2760"/>At daybreak, they mustered round the hill where the Hellenes were encamped, horsemen and targeteers in large numbers, and more were always rolling in.
 <s n="2761"/>They attacked the heavy troops with complete safety.
</section>

<section n="6.3.7" sentences="4">
 <s n="2762"/>The Hellenes had not a bowman, not a javelineer, not a horseman.
 <s n="2763"/>The natives ran in and rode in and cast their javelins,
 <s n="2764"/> and when the others attacked, they easily escaped, 
 <s n="2765"/>and set on them from all sides.
</section>

<section n="6.3.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="2766"/>Most of the Hellenes were wounded, but none of the natives.

<section n="6.3.9" sentences="3">

<section n="6.3.10" sentences="3">
 <s n="2771"/>Cheirisophos marched safely as far as Calpe’s Haven.

<section n="6.3.11" sentences="3">

<section n="6.3.12" sentences="2">
 <s n="2777"/>“Soldiers,” he said, “the Arcadians, or what is left of them, are besieged on a hill, and many have been killed.
 <s n="2778"/>I think that if they are to be destroyed there will be no safety for us, with such hosts of enemies, so many and so bold."

<section n="6.3.13" sentences="1">
 <s n="2779"/>Then it is best for us to rescue these men, and have them fighting with us if they are still safe; or we shall be left alone and in danger.

<section n="6.3.16" sentences="5">

 <s n="2782"/>but the enemy are close by:
 <s n="2783"/>the shortest way is to Calpe’s Haven, where we suppose Cheirisophos to be, if he is safe.
 <s n="2784"/>But then, you may think, no ships are there to sail in, and if we stay there, not even one day’s provisions.

 <section n="6.3.23" sentences="2">
 <s n="2802"/>At first they wondered what had happened, but they learnt from those left behind that the Thracians had decamped after nightfall and were gone; and the Hellenes were gone, 
 <s n="2803"/>where they did not know.
 </section>
 <section n="6.3.24" sentences="3">

 <s n="2805"/>On the march they saw the tracks of the Arcadians and Achaians on the road to Calpe.
 <s n="2806"/>When they reached the place, the two parties saw each other with joy and embraced like brothers.

 <section n="6.3.25" sentences="4">

 <s n="2808"/>“When we saw no fires,” they said, “at first we thought you would attack the enemy that night.
 <s n="2809"/>And the enemy feared that, as it seemed to us, and went away for that reason;
 <s n="2810"/> for they did go about that time.”
 </section>
 <section n="6.3.26" sentences="3">

 <s n="2812"/>Then we decided not to be left behind,
 <s n="2813"/> and so we came here too.”

 <section n="6.4.1" sentences="3">
 <s n="2814"/>That day they spent under the open sky on the shore beside the harbour.
 <s n="2815"/>The place called Calpe’s Haven is in Asiatic Thrace.
 <s n="2816"/>This Thrace extends from the mouth of the Euxine, along the sea to Heracleia on the right.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="2817"/>In a ship-of-war it is a long day’s journey by oar to Heracleia from Byzantion.
 <s n="2818"/>In between there is no city either Hellenic or friendly, but only the Thracians of Bithynia.
 <s n="2819"/>Any Hellenes they take shipwrecked or otherwise they are said to treat with great cruelty.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.3" sentences="2">
 <s n="2820"/>Calpe’s Haven lies half-way on the voyage between Heracleia and Byzantion.
 <s n="2821"/>It is a promontory jutting out into the sea; the part by the sea being a sheer cliff, height where it is least no less than twenty fathoms, and facing the land a neck about four hundred feet wide. The space inside the neck is enough for ten thousand inhabitants.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.4" sentences="3">
 <s n="2822"/>The harbour under the cliff has a beach towards the west.
 <s n="2823"/>There is a spring of plentiful sweet water close beside the sea commanded by the promontory.
 <s n="2824"/>There is abundance of all sorts of wood, and particularly a great deal of fine wood for shipbuilding close to the sea.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.5" sentences="2">
 <s n="2825"/>The highland stretches into the country some two or three miles, good soil without stones.
 <s n="2826"/>The part along the seashore is longer still, set thick with much timber of all sorts.
 </section>

 <section n="6.4.6" sentences="2">
 <s n="2827"/>The rest of the country for a long way round is good, and has many villages full of people; 
 <s n="2828"/> the land bears barley and wheat and pulse of all sorts, millet and sesame, figs enough, plenty of grapes, good wine-grapes too, and everything else except olives.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.7" sentences="3">
 <s n="2829"/>So much for the country. 
 <s n="2830"/>They encamped on the beach beside the sea.
 <s n="2831"/>They would not camp on a place which might be turned into a city, and they thought indeed that they had really been brought there by some scheme of persons who wanted to found a city.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="2832"/>For most of the soldiers had not been driven by poverty to this expedition; but it was the fame of Cyrus which had brought them, some had followers with them, some had spent money themselves, and a few others had run away from home—they had left father and mother, or even children too, hoping to return with wealth for them, since they had heard how others had made their fortunes with Cyrus.
 <s n="2833"/>Men such as these wished to return safe to Hellas.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.9" sentences="7">
 <s n="2834"/>On the next day after this meeting Xenophon made sacrifice for an expedition,
 <s n="2835"/> since he must lead them out for provisions;
 <s n="2836"/> he intended also to bury the dead.
 <s n="2837"/>The omens were good; they set out, and the Arcadians went with them. Most of the dead they buried where they fell;
 <s n="2838"/> it was already five days ago, and the bodies could not be removed.
 <s n="2839"/>Others who lay on the roads, however, they did collect, and buried them with all possible ceremony.
 <s n="2840"/>For those they could not find they made a great cenotaph, and laid garlands upon it.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.10" sentences="4">
 <s n="2841"/>And then returned to camp.
 <s n="2842"/> That evening they supped and slept there.
 <s n="2843"/>On the next day there was a general meeting of the soldiers, 
 <s n="2844"/>summoned chiefly by Agasias the Stymphalian, captain, and Hieronymos the Elean, captain, and certain others, the seniors of the Arcadians.
 </section>
 <section n="6.4.11" sentences="3">
 <s n="2845"/>They passed a resolution, that for the future if anyone proposed to divide their army he should be punished with death.
 <s n="2846"/>And now that the army should resume the arrangement they had before, and the former captains be in command.
 <s n="2847"/>Cheirisophos was dead already, from some medicine he took in his fever. Neon of Asine took his place.
 </section>

 <section n="6.4.12" sentences="8">
 <s n="2848"/>After this Xenophon rose and spoke. 
 <s n="2849"/>“Soldiers,” he said, “it is clear that our journey must be made by land,
 <s n="2850"/>for we have no ships; 
 <s n="2851"/>and it is necessary to march at once,
 <s n="2852"/>for if we stay we have no food.
 <s n="2853"/>Then we,” says he, “will make sacrifice;
 <s n="2854"/>and you must prepare yourselves to fight now if ever you did,
 <s n="2855"/>for the enemy are full of confidence.”

<section n="6.4.13" sentences="3">

<section n="6.4.15" sentences="2">

 <s n="2862"/>and then sacrificed; great numbers were present.

<section n="6.4.16" sentences="3">
