<section n="6.4.17" sentences="4">
 <s n="2866"/>After this they had a meeting, and Xenophon said, 
 <s n="2867"/>“Gentlemen, for our departure, as you see, the omens are not yet favourable; 
 <s n="2868"/>but I see you need provisions,
 <s n="2869"/> and I think it necessary, then, to sacrifice about just that.”
</section>

<section n="6.4.18" sentences="3">
 <s n="2870"/>Someone rose and said, “There is good reason why the omens are bad.

 <s n="2872"/>Cleandros the governor of Byzantion intends to come with transports and ships-of-war!”

<section n="6.4.19" sentences="5">
 <s n="2873"/>Then they all decided to stay;
 <s n="2874"/> but the provisions had to be got, and there must be some expedition.

 <s n="2877"/>but he refused to lead them out without good omens.

<section n="6.4.20" sentences="3">

 <s n="2879"/>and almost the whole army was crowded about the place, because all were concerned, but no victims were to be had.
 <s n="2880"/>The captains did not lead them out, but summoned a meeting.

<section n="6.4.21" sentences="3">

<section n="6.4.22" sentences="4">

<section n="6.4.26" sentences="2">

<section n="6.4.27" sentences="4">

<section n="6.5.1" sentences="5">

<section n="6.5.2" sentences="2">

<section n="6.5.7" sentences="2">

 <s n="2934"/>Word was sent back calling officers and captains to the front.

<section n="6.5.13" sentences="2">
 <s n="2935"/>Xenophon wondered what the check could be, and soon hearing the summons he rode forward at full speed.

<section n="6.5.14" sentences="3">
 <s n="2937"/>Then Xenophon broke in firmly and said,
 <s n="2938"/> “You know, gentlemen, that I have never urged you to any danger if I had the choice.
 <s n="2939"/>You need no reputation for courage, as I can see; it’s life or death now!”

<section n="6.5.15" sentences="3">
 <s n="2940"/>“Without fighting we cannot get out of this;
 <s n="2941"/>if we do not attack, the enemy will follow whenever we retreat,
 <s n="2942"/>and fall on us.”
</section>

<section n="6.5.16" sentences="1">
 <s n="2943"/>“Think, then, whether it is better to go for the enemy with arms in front, or reverse arms and watch him following behind!”
</section>

<section n="6.5.17" sentences="3">
 <s n="2944"/>“You know, moreover, that to retreat before the enemy is not for a man of honour, but to attack makes even a coward bold.
 <s n="2945"/>I would rather myself attack with half this number than retreat with double the number!
 <s n="2946"/>As for these men—I know you expect yourselves that they will not stand before us, but if we retreat we all know they will dare to follow.
</section>

<section n="6.5.18" sentences="3">
 <s n="2947"/>And to cross, leaving a difficult ravine behind us, when we are going to fight, is surely an advantage worth taking!
 <s n="2948"/>For the enemy, I should like to give every opportunity to retreat;
 <s n="2949"/> for us, the ground itself ought to teach us that only victory can give safety.

<section n="6.5.19" sentences="3">
 <s n="2950"/>I’m really surprised if anyone thinks this ravine more terrible than the other places we have passed.

 <s n="2952"/>Or the mountains which we have passed, if all those light troops are following?

<section n="6.5.20" sentences="2">
 <s n="2953"/>And suppose we do get safe to the sea, what a huge ravine is the Euxine!

<section n="6.5.21" sentences="4">

<section n="6.5.25" sentences="5">

 <s n="2967"/>They posted the targeteers on each flank, and marched for the enemy.

 <s n="2970"/>The enemy stood their ground, thinking they had a good position.

<section n="6.5.26" sentences="3">

 <s n="3078"/>and if God grants it, I will lead you all home to Hellas.
 <s n="3079"/>This is a very different tale from what I heard from some people, that your whole army was apostate from the Lacedaimonians.”

<section n="6.6.38" sentences="3">

<section n="7.1.1" sentences="1">

<section n="7.1.26" sentences="2">
 <s n="3152"/>We shall be proved enemies to the Lacedaimonians and their allies.

<section n="7.1.27" sentences="2">

<section n="7.1.28" sentences="2">

<section n="7.1.29" sentences="2">

<section n="7.1.31" sentences="1">

<section n="7.2.17" sentences="1">

<section n="7.2.18" sentences="3">

<section n="7.2.29" sentences="1">

<section n="7.2.32" sentences="3">

<section n="7.2.33" sentences="2">

<section n="7.2.34" sentences="4">

<section n="7.2.35" sentences="1">

<section n="7.2.36" sentences="2">

<section n="7.2.37" sentences="1">

<section n="7.3.1" sentences="2">

<section n="7.3.2" sentences="4">

<section n="7.3.3" sentences="5">

 <s n="3286"/>That is what he says.

<section n="7.3.5" sentences="1">
 <s n="3289"/>My own opinion is that, since here we have no money to buy with—and without money they forbid us to take any—we should go up among the villages where might gives right to take, and get our provisions there.

<section n="7.3.6" sentences="3">
 <s n="3290"/>All in favour of this hold up a hand!
 <s n="3291"/>All held up hands.

 <s n="3294"/>Neon and others from Aristarchos tried to persuade them to turn back,

 <s n="3299"/>Then we will hear both you and the Laconian’s men, and choose what we think best.“We are marching to find food for this army.

 <s n="3301"/>So if you will lead us where plenty of provisions are to be found, we shall believe ourselves to be your guests.”

 <s n="3302"/>Seuthes answered,

<section n="7.3.10" sentences="6">
 <s n="3304"/>“Lead on, then,” said Xenophon.

 <s n="3306"/>“What I ask, men, is to take the field with me.

 <s n="3308"/>You shall have food and drink as now by taking it from the country.
 <s n="3309"/>But whatever is captured I claim for myself, that I may dispose of it and so find your pay.

 <s n="3312"/>Xenophon asked,

<section n="7.3.13" sentences="4">
 <s n="3316"/>Then it was open for all to speak.
 <s n="3317"/>Many did speak, very much the same, that this was an excellent offer of Seuthes.
 <s n="3318"/>For winter had come, and there was no voyage home if any wished; it was impossible to get through as friends if they had to pay for their keep.
 <s n="3319"/>To stay and be fed in a hostile country was safer with Seuthes than alone, in all this plenty.

<section n="7.3.14" sentences="5">

 <s n="3323"/>No-one opposed, he put the vote; and it was carried.

<section n="7.3.15" sentences="1">

<section n="7.4.4" sentences="1">

<section n="7.5.1" sentences="2">

<section n="7.5.2" sentences="2">
 <s n="3498"/>Heracleides was there already, with the price of the spoil.
 <s n="3499"/>Seuthes then drove out three pairs of mules (there were only three, all the rest were oxen), called Xenophon and told him to take them, and to distribute the others among the officers.
</section>

<section n="7.5.3" sentences="3">
 <s n="3500"/>Xenophon said, 
 <s n="3501"/>“I can wait for another time;
 <s n="3502"/>give these to those captains and officers who have served with me.”
</section>

<section n="7.5.4" sentences="4">
 <s n="3503"/>So these pairs of mules went one to Timasion, one to Cleanor, one to Phryniscos;
 <s n="3504"/>the pairs of oxen were distributed to the underofficers.
 <s n="3505"/>Then he gave the pay, but he could find pay only for twenty days, although the whole month was up.
 <s n="3506"/>Heracleides declared that was all he had got for the sale.
</section>

<section n="7.5.5" sentences="3">
 <s n="3507"/>Xenophon was angry, and said with an oath,
 <s n="3508"/> “I don’t think you look after the interests of Seuthes as you should;
 <s n="3509"/>if you had you would have brought the pay in full, even borrowing something if you couldn’t help it, even selling your own clothes!”
</section>

<section n="7.5.6" sentences="1">
 <s n="3510"/>Then Heracleides was angry, and feared he might lose the friendship of Seuthes; and he did all he could from that day to set Seuthes against Xenophon.
</section>

<section n="7.5.7" sentences="2">
 <s n="3511"/>Indeed, the soldiers blamed Xenophon because they had not their pay;
 <s n="3512"/>Seuthes was angry with him because he dunned him for the pay.
</section>

<section n="7.5.8" sentences="3">
 <s n="3513"/>So far he had been always saying that he would give him Bisanthe and Ganos and Newcastle, as soon as he got to the sea;
 <s n="3514"/>but from that time he never mentioned them.
 <s n="3515"/>For Heracleides kept dinning into his ears that it was not safe to give forts to a man who had an army.
</section>

<section n="7.5.9" sentences="2">
 <s n="3516"/>Then Xenophon began to think what he was to do about the campaign up country.
 <s n="3517"/>And Heracleides brought the other captains before Seuthes, and bade them say that they could lead the army quite as well as Xenophon; he promised them their pay within a few days, full pay for two months, and urged them to march.
</section>

<section n="7.5.10" sentences="3">
 <s n="3518"/>Then Timasion said, 
 <s n="3519"/>“I would not go without Xenophon, not if you paid me for five months.”
 <s n="3520"/>Phryniscos and Cleanor agreed.
</section>

<section n="7.5.11" sentences="3">
 <s n="3521"/>Then Seuthes scolded Heracleides for not having brought Xenophon too.
 <s n="3522"/>Presently they invited him by himself.
 <s n="3523"/>He understood the perfidy of Heracleides, and how he had been scheming against him with the captains; so when he came he brought the other captains and officers,
</section>

<section n="7.5.12" sentences="3">
 <s n="3524"/>and they all agreed.
 <s n="3525"/>So they set out together, and keeping the Euxine on the right, marched through the country of Millet-eating Thracians, as they are called, as far as Salmydessos.
 <s n="3526"/>There many of the ships that sail into the Euxine run aground and are wrecked, for there are many shallows running out into the sea.
</section>

<section n="7.5.13" sentences="2">
 <s n="3527"/>The Thracians in those parts put up boundary marks, and plunder all that are cast into their portions;
 <s n="3528"/>formerly, they say, many had been killed fighting with each other over the plunder, before they set up the marks.
</section>

<section n="7.5.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="3529"/>Here was a great find, beds and boxes and written books, and everything else which mariners carry in their wooden chests.
 <s n="3530"/>Having reduced all this district, they came back.
</section>

<section n="7.5.15" sentences="3">
 <s n="3531"/>By this time Seuthes had an army of his own larger than theirs;
 <s n="3532"/>for many more had come down from the Odrysians, and his new subjects all joined with him.
 <s n="3533"/>They made bivouac on the plain above Selymbria, three or four miles from the sea.
</section>

<section n="7.5.16" sentences="2">
 <s n="3534"/>No pay yet appeared; and the soldiers were very much put out with Xenophon, because he was no longer great with Seuthes,
 <s n="3535"/>but if ever he wanted to see him many difficulties were put in the way.
</section>

<section n="7.6.1" sentences="1">
 <s n="3536"/>At this time, when nearly two months had passed, a mission came from Thimbron, Charminos the Laconian and Polyneicos.

<section n="7.6.2" sentences="3">
 <s n="3537"/>As soon as these men arrived Heracleides heard they were visiting the army, and said to Seuthes,
 <s n="3538"/>“Here’s a great piece of luck! The Lacedaimonians want the army and you don’t;
 <s n="3539"/>then give them the army, and they won’t ask for any more pay—they will just clear out of the country.”
</section>

<section n="7.6.3" sentences="4">
 <s n="3540"/>Seuthes, on hearing this, told him to bring the men in.
 <s n="3541"/>They said they were visiting the army; and he said he would give it up, and wished to be their friend and ally,
 <s n="3542"/>and invited them to a banquet, which was magnificent.
 <s n="3543"/>But he did not invite Xenophon or any other of the captains.
</section>

<section n="7.6.4" sentences="5">
 <s n="3544"/>The Lacedaimonians asked, “What sort of a man is Xenophon?” “Not a bad sort,” he said, “but too fond of his men. 
 
 <s n="3545"/>So much the worse for him!”
 <s n="3546"/>“What,” said they, 
 <s n="3547"/>“does he harangue the men?”
 <s n="3548"/>Heracleides said, “I should think he does!”
</section>

<section n="7.6.5" sentences="2">
 <s n="3549"/>“Well, then,” they said, “perhaps he will object to our carting them off?”
 <s n="3550"/>“Just you collect them,” said Heracleides, “and promise the pay; much they will care for him! Off they will run with you.”
</section>

<section n="7.6.6" sentences="4">
 <s n="3551"/>“Then how could we get them together?” they wanted to know.
 <s n="3552"/>“To-morrow,” said Heracleides, “early in the morning we'll take you to them;
 <s n="3553"/>and I am sure,” says he, “as soon as they see you, they will run together glad enough.”
 <s n="3554"/>Thus ended that day.
</section>


<section n="7.6.7" sentences="3">
 <s n="3555"/>Next morning the Laconians came to the army, led by Seuthes and Heracleides, and the men collected.
 <s n="3556"/>Then two Laconians said, “The Lacedaimonians have resolved to make war on Tissaphernes, who has wronged you; then if you will go with us, you shall punish your enemy,
 <s n="3557"/>and every man shall receive a daric per month, every lieutenant two darics, and every captain four.”
</section>

<section n="7.6.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="3558"/>The men were delighted to hear it.
 <s n="3559"/>At once an Arcadian rose to accuse Xenophon. Seuthes was there also, wanting to hear what would be done, and stood with an interpreter within hearing;
</section>

<section n="7.6.9" sentences="5">
 <s n="3560"/>but he understood himself a good deal of Greek.
 <s n="3561"/>Then the Arcadian said:
 <s n="3562"/> “We should have been with you long ago, Lacedaimonians, if Xenophon had not persuaded us and brought us here, and what a place!
 <s n="3563"/>Through this dreadful winter we have been campaigning night and day without rest, and he enjoys our labours;
 <s n="3564"/>Seuthes has made him a rich man privately, and he keeps our pay from us.
</section>

<section n="7.6.10" sentences="3">
 <s n="3565"/>If I could only see this man punished and stoned for dragging us about, I should call it pay in full, and I wouldn’t worry about my labours.”
 <s n="3566"/>After this another got up and another, just the same,
 <s n="3567"/>and at last Xenophon spoke:
</section>

<section n="7.6.11" sentences="2">
 <s n="3568"/>“Then there is nothing a man must not expect in this world, as it seems, when I stand here now to be accused by you, and in something where I know in my conscience that I have used my utmost endeavours on your behalf.
 <s n="3569"/>I was already on my way home, when I was turned aside by hearing of you—not because I heard you were in luck, not at all; no, I heard you were at your wits’ end, and hoped to help you if I could.
</section>

<section n="7.6.12" sentences="3">
 <s n="3570"/>When I came to you, Seuthes, who is here present, sent messenger after messenger to me, and made great promises to me if I would persuade you to join him.
 <s n="3571"/>Yet I did not try to do that, as you know yourselves; I led you to the place where I thought you could cross most quickly to Asia.
 <s n="3572"/>I believed that to be best for you, and you wished it, as I knew.
</section>

<section n="7.6.13" sentences="1">
 <s n="3573"/>Then Aristarchos came with ships of war and stopped that. Next I did the natural thing, I suppose, and called you together that we might consider what we must do.
</section>

<section n="7.6.14" sentences="2">
 <s n="3574"/>Aristarchos ordered you to go into the Chersonese. Seuthes tried to persuade you to join his expedition: you heard both, and all said go with Seuthes, all voted for that.
 <s n="3575"/>What wrong did I do you, then, when I led you where you all decided to go?
</section>

<section n="7.6.15" sentences="2">
 <s n="3576"/>However, Seuthes deceived you about your pay: if I praise him for that, you would have a right to hate me and accuse me;
 <s n="3577"/>if I am now wholly estranged from him, having been formerly his especial friend, what right could you have to blame me for this estrangement when I chose you instead of Seuthes?
</section>

<section n="7.6.16" sentences="2">
 <s n="3578"/>Well, perhaps you might say that it is possible I got your money from Seuthes, and I am just pretending.
 <s n="3579"/>Then one thing is clear: if Seuthes paid me anything, he did not expect to have to pay it again to you, and so throw away what he gave to me.
</section>

<section n="7.6.17" sentences="2">
 <s n="3580"/>Very well, if you believe that happened, you have the power here and now to make the transaction worth less for both of us by demanding and getting the money from him.
 <s n="3581"/>It is clear that if I have had any of it from him, he will ask for it back, and quite right too, if I do not carry out the transaction for which I took the bribe.
</section>

<section n="7.6.18" sentences="3">
 <s n="3582"/>Far from it—I have nothing of yours.
 <s n="3583"/>I swear to you solemnly by gods and goddesses all, that I have not even what Seuthes promised me personally.
 <s n="3584"/>He is present himself, and hears me, and knows whether I am forsworn.
</section>

<section n="7.6.19" sentences="1">
 <s n="3585"/>What will surprise you more, I swear that I did not receive even what the other captains did, no indeed, not even as much as some of the under-officers.
</section>

<section n="7.6.20" sentences="3">
 <s n="3586"/>Why did I behave so?
 <s n="3587"/>I believed, gentlemen, that the better I helped him to bear the poverty he had then, the better friend he would be when he should become powerful.
 <s n="3588"/>But now I see him in his prosperity, and now I understand his mind.
</section>

<section n="7.6.21" sentences="3">
 <s n="3589"/>One might well ask whether I am not ashamed to be deceived like a fool.
 <s n="3590"/>Upon my word, I should be ashamed indeed if an enemy had deceived me;
 <s n="3591"/>but for a friend, I think it more disgraceful to deceive than to be deceived.
</section>

<section n="7.6.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="3592"/>If we can speak of precautions against a friend, I know we took every precaution to give him no just excuse not to pay what he promised;
 <s n="3593"/>for we have done him no wrong, we never were slack, never dawdled over what he asked us to do.
</section>

<section n="7.6.23" sentences="2">
 <s n="3594"/>But, you might say, security ought to have been taken, that he might not have been able to deceive if he wished.
 <s n="3595"/>Then listen to me while I tell what I should never have said in his presence, if you had not shown yourselves altogether stupid or else wholly ungrateful to me.
</section>

<section n="7.6.24" sentences="3">
 <s n="3596"/>Just remember what a state you were in when I brought you out to Seuthes.
 <s n="3597"/>You went to Perinthos, but you couldn’t get in— Aristarchos the Lacedaimonian shut the gates in your faces.
 <s n="3598"/>You were outside in the open air, it was midwinter, you had a market, but there was little to buy and little to buy with; you were forced to remain in Thrace—
</section>

<section n="7.6.25" sentences="2">
 <s n="3599"/>for ships of war were there to prevent a crossing;
 <s n="3600"/>but if you did remain, you would be in an enemy country; there were large forces of cavalry against you,
</section>

<section n="7.6.26" sentences="1">
 <s n="3601"/>and large forces of light troops, and what had we? A force of heavy-armed men, yes, and if we had taken it in a body to the villages perhaps we could have got some corn, nothing much; but to pursue and capture prisoners or sheep— we had nothing at all for that: I found no regular body at all of horse or targeteers with you.
</section>

<section n="7.6.27" sentences="1">
 <s n="3602"/>In such a necessity, if I had asked no pay at all from Seuthes and only got him for an ally, when he had the cavalry and light troops which you wanted, would you have thought I made a bad bargain for you?
</section>

<section n="7.6.28" sentences="1">
 <s n="3603"/>Only by sharing in those, of course, you found any amount of corn in the villages, because the Thracians were compelled to run away in a hurry, and you took more cattle and captives.
</section>

<section n="7.6.29" sentences="2">
 <s n="3604"/>Enemies—we never saw one of them any longer as soon as the horse was added to us;
 <s n="3605"/>before that, the enemy followed us boldly with horse and light infantry, and we could not break into small bodies and scatter anywhere to get more abundant provisions.
</section>

<section n="7.6.30" sentences="1">
 <s n="3606"/>And if the man who provided this safety for you did not really give you very large pay for this safety, that is really dreadful treatment! And that’s why you think I must never leave the place alive!
</section>

<section n="7.6.31" sentences="4">
 <s n="3607"/>And how are you off now?
 <s n="3608"/>You are leaving this place; you have spent the winter in the midst of abundance, 
 <s n="3609"/>and whatever you got from Seuthes is so much to the good, for what you spent the enemy paid.
 <s n="3610"/>Yes, and while this was your lot, you saw none of your comrades killed and you lost none alive.

</section>

<section n="7.6.32" sentences="2">
 <s n="3611"/>Besides, any fine feat you may have done against the barbarians in Asia, you have that safe and sound, haven’t you?
 <s n="3612"/>And now you have won a new glory by fighting the Thracians in Europe too and conquering them. I tell you that you ought to thank God for the very things you complain of, since they are blessings.
</section>

<section n="7.6.33" sentences="5">
 <s n="3613"/>So much for you;
 <s n="3614"/>now be so kind as to consider my side of the question.
 <s n="3615"/>When I set out for home that time I had your good wishes and best thanks, and through you I had a high reputation among all parts of our nation.
 <s n="3616"/>I was trusted by the Lacedaimonians, 
 <s n="3617"/>or they would not have sent me back to you.

<section n="7.6.34" sentences="1">
 <s n="3618"/>I go now, reprobated by you before the Lacedaimonians, and disliked by Seuthes on your account, whom I hoped to serve along with you and to find in him an honourable refuge for myself and my children, if I should have any.
</section>

<section n="7.6.35" sentences="1">
 <s n="3619"/>And you, on whose behalf I have made many enemies and those more powerful men than I am, you for whom I have never ceased working and still work now to do you what good I can, that is your feeling for me.
</section>

<section n="7.6.36" sentences="2">
 <s n="3620"/>Well, you have me. I did not avoid you, I did not run from you and let you catch me.
 <s n="3621"/>If you do to me what you say, you will have slain a man who has spent many a sleepless night for you, who has shared many toils and dangers with you, in my turn and out of turn, one who when the gods were gracious has set up very many trophies of the barbarians by your side, and strained against you with all my strength to save you from becoming enemies to any of your countrymen.
</section>

<section n="7.6.37" sentences="2">
 <s n="3622"/>Indeed even now it is possible for you without reproach to travel where you please both by land and sea.
 <s n="3623"/>But you, at the time when you have everything open to you, when you are sailing to the very place you have long desired, when great potentates want you and you can see your pay, when your leaders are the Lacedaimonians, by common consent the dominant power—this is exactly the time you think to be just right for putting me to death.
</section>

<section n="7.6.38" sentences="4">
 <s n="3624"/>But not in the time of our troubles: you have the best of memories, you never forget.
 <s n="3625"/>No, then you called me father, and vowed always to speak of me as your benefactor.
 <s n="3626"/>But these men who have come for you now are not hard-hearted themselves.
 <s n="3627"/>As I take it, they will not think the better of you when you behave so to me.” He spoke and ceased.

<section n="7.6.39" sentences="5">
 <s n="3628"/>Then Charminos the Lacedaimonian rose and spoke.
 <s n="3629"/>He said:
 <s n="3630"/>“No, be the twa gads! I for one think you have no right to complain of this man.
 <s n="3631"/>I’m a witness myself on his side.
 <s n="3632"/>What Seuthes said when I and Polynicos asked about Xenophon, what sort of a man he was, what he said was he had only one fault to find; he said he was too fond of the soldiers, that’s the reason why he didn’t get on with us Lacedaimonians or with himself.”

<section n="7.6.40" sentences="2">
 <s n="3633"/>On this an Arcadian got up, Eurylochos of Lusia, and said to the two envoys,
 <s n="3634"/>“My opinion is, that you begin being our captains here, and first get us the pay from Seuthes whether he will or no, but don’t take us away before.”

<section n="7.6.41" sentences="4">
 <s n="3635"/>Polycrates the Athenian said (prompted by Xenophon),
 <s n="3636"/>“Why, man,” says he, “I see Heracleides here too! That man was entrusted with the property which we won by hard work, and he sold it, and did not give the proceeds either to Seuthes or to us.

<section n="7.6.42" sentences="4">

<section n="7.6.43" sentences="2">

<section n="7.7.1" sentences="3">

<section n="7.7.2" sentences="3">

<section n="7.7.4" sentences="3">

<section n="7.7.5" sentences="1">
 <s n="3661"/>Before we became your friends,” he said, “we marched where we would through this country, plundering and burning at will.

<section n="7.7.6" sentences="1">
 <s n="3662"/>When you came to us on embassy, you lodged beside us fearing no enemy. Your people hardly came into the country at all, or if you did, you lodged as in an enemy country, with your horses bitted and bridled.

<section n="7.7.7" sentences="2">
 <s n="3663"/>Then you became our friends, and through us, you hold the land by God’s help;

<section n="7.7.8" sentences="1">

<section n="7.7.10" sentences="3">
 <s n="3667"/>“And then what’s the use of addressing me?” says he;

<section n="7.7.11" sentences="5">

<section n="7.7.12" sentences="2">

<section n="7.7.14" sentences="3">

<section n="7.7.18" sentences="2">

 <s n="3694"/>He said no to that; but he said the best thing would be for the two Laconians to approach Seuthes about the pay,
 <s n="3695"/>and he thought Seuthes would agree.
 <s n="3696"/>The next thing was to send Xenophon with him, and he promised to demand it.
 <s n="3697"/>He only asked them not to burn the villages.
</section>

<section n="7.7.20" sentences="3">
 <s n="3698"/>Then they sent Xenophon with the men who seemed best,
 <s n="3699"/>and he said to Seuthes: “I come to claim nothing, Seuthes;
 <s n="3700"/>but to show that you were not right to be angry with me, because I claimed so urgently from you what you had promised for the soldiers.
</section>

<section n="7.7.21" sentences="1">
 <s n="3701"/>In fact, I thought it no less useful for yourself than for them.
</section>

<section n="7.7.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="3702"/>First, I believed that after heaven it was they who had made you famous; they made you king of a great country and a great population,
 <s n="3703"/>so that nothing you do can be hidden, whether good or bad."
</section>

<section n="7.7.23" sentences="1">
 <s n="3704"/>"For such a man it seemed a great thing not to be thought so ungrateful as to dismiss his benefactors; and a great thing to have a good name with six thousand men; but greatest of all, not to make yourself distrusted when you make a promise."
</section>

<section n="7.7.24" sentences="4">
 <s n="3705"/>"Indeed, I see that when men are distrusted, their words are blown about empty and ineffective and despised;
 <s n="3706"/>but whenever men are known to keep their word, if they want anything, their words have no less power than the force of others."
 <s n="3707"/>"And if they desire to make people reasonable, I perceive that their threats bring them to reason no less than summary punishment from others.
 <s n="3708"/>And if such men make a promise, they achieve no less than others do by a gift on the spot."
</section>

<section n="7.7.25" sentences="3">
 <s n="3709"/>"Just remember yourself what you paid in advance when you took us as allies.
 <s n="3710"/>Nothing!—you know it.
 <s n="3711"/>But we believed what you said would be made true, when you encouraged all these men to join your army, and to win you a domain worth no less than thirty talents, the sum which these men claim, many times more indeed."
</section>

<section n="7.7.26" sentences="1">
 <s n="3712"/>"Then the belief and trust, which won your empire, is being sold only for the money we claim."
</section>

<section n="7.7.27" sentences="2">
 <s n="3713"/>"Remember again how great a prize you thought the conquest which you have made.
 <s n="3714"/>I am well assured that you would rather have achieved what you have achieved than to receive many times as much as our claim."
</section>

<section n="7.7.28" sentences="1">
 <s n="3715"/>"I believe myself that it would be a heavier blow, and more discreditable, not to keep now what you have than not to get it then; just as it is harder to become poor after being rich than never to have been rich at all, and more painful to become nobody after being king than never to have been king at all."
</section>

<section n="7.7.29" sentences="1">
 <s n="3716"/>"You know also that those who have become your subjects were not persuaded by friendship to be ruled by you, but yielded to necessity, and they would try to make themselves free again unless fear restrained them."
</section>

<section n="7.7.30" sentences="1">
 <s n="3717"/>"Then what do you think would make them fear you more and behave reasonably? If they should see the soldiers so disposed to you that they would stand by if you told them, and come back at once if you needed them, and if others heard from them such good stories about you that they would join you whenever you wished? Or if they should suspect that no one else would ever come to you because these doings made them distrust your word, and even your own men felt much inclined to this same feeling?”
</section>

<section n="7.7.31" sentences="2">
 <s n="3718"/>Really and truly, these people yielded to you from no lack of numbers, but only from lack of leaders.
 <s n="3719"/>Then there’s a risk now that they may find leaders in some of those who think you have wronged them, or even in the Lacedaemonians who are stronger still: suppose the soldiers promise more willing service to them if they exact what is due from you, and if the Lacedaemonians agree because they want the army!
</section>

<section n="7.7.32" sentences="2">
 <s n="3720"/>Besides, it is plain enough that the Thracians who are now under your rule would be readier to march against you than with you;
 <s n="3721"/>for under your rule slavery is their lot; if you were conquered it would be freedom.
</section>

<section n="7.7.33" sentences="1">
 <s n="3722"/>Again, if you think you must care for the country now as being your own, how do you think it more likely to be uninjured? Suppose these men receive what they claim; they would depart and leave peace behind them. But suppose they remain in the country as enemies, and you should try to bring in as many again and more to fight on your side, all wanting provisions!
</section>

<section n="7.7.34" sentences="1">
 <s n="3723"/>And which would cost you more money—if you pay what is due to these, or if you have to pay others, a force strong enough to conquer them, and leave the debt still due?
</section>

<section n="7.7.35" sentences="2">
 <s n="3724"/>Heracleides, indeed, as he used to explain to me, found the sum enormous.
 <s n="3725"/>Well, I declare it is much less now for you to get and to give than it was then to pay the tenth part before we came.
</section>

<section n="7.7.36" sentences="2">
 <s n="3726"/>For no fixed limit divides much and little, but only how much one can give and the other can take.
 <s n="3727"/>And now your yearly income will be more than your whole possessions then.
</section>

<section n="7.7.37" sentences="1">
 <s n="3728"/>All I have said, Seuthes, has been forethought for a friend; I wished you to be thought worthy of the goods which the gods have given you; and also that my name should not be ruined in the army.
</section>

<section n="7.7.38" sentences="2">
 <s n="3729"/>For I assure you that I could do nothing in the army now, whether I wished to injure an enemy or to help you again.
 <s n="3730"/>That is how they are disposed towards me.
</section>

<section n="7.7.39" sentences="1">
 <s n="3731"/>But I protest before the gods, who know, and before you, that I have never received anything from you on account of the soldiers, and I have never asked you for what is theirs, and indeed never asked you to give me what you promised.
</section>

<section n="7.7.40" sentences="2">
 <s n="3732"/>If you had offered it, on my oath I never would have taken it, unless the soldiers were to receive their own also.
 <s n="3733"/>It would have been a disgrace to make sure of mine, and to look on while they were wronged, especially as they held me in honor.
</section>

<section n="7.7.41" sentences="2">
 <s n="3734"/>Of course, Heracleides thinks everything is rubbish compared with money, wholly and absolutely;
 <s n="3735"/>but my opinion is, Seuthes, that a man can have no finer or more glorious possession, especially a ruler, than virtue and justice and generosity.
</section>

<section n="7.7.42" sentences="1">
 <s n="3736"/>For who has these is rich: friends are his wealth, those many he has and the many who wish to be; while he prospers, many are glad with him, and if he comes to grief, he lacks not those who will help.
</section>

<section n="7.7.43" sentences="2">
 <s n="3737"/>However, if you have not found out from my actions that I was heart and soul your friend, and if you cannot learn this from these words of mine, at least take notice of the words of all the soldiers:
 <s n="3738"/>you were there yourself, and you heard what they said, wishing to find fault with me.
</section>

<section n="7.7.44" sentences="2">
 <s n="3739"/>They accused me to the Lacedaemonians that I rated you higher than the Lacedaemonians, and their private charge was that I cared more for your interests than theirs;
 <s n="3740"/>they also said I had received gifts from you.
</section>

<section n="7.7.45" sentences="1">
 <s n="3741"/>What do you think: did they see any ill will in me towards you when they accuse me of receiving gifts from you? Had they not noticed my great readiness to serve you?
</section>

<section n="7.7.46" sentences="3">
 <s n="3742"/>I really think all the world believe that one ought to show goodwill to one who presents him with gifts.
 <s n="3743"/>And what of you? Before I had served you in any way you received me pleasantly with eyes and voice, you entertained me, and made promises for the future—which have not been fulfilled;
 <s n="3744"/>and when you had done what you wished, and now that you have become very great, and I did my best, now the soldiers dishonour me, and you can endure to look on!
</section>

<section n="7.7.47" sentences="2">
 <s n="3745"/>No, I believe you will decide to pay this money; time will be your teacher, and you will never allow yourself to see your benefactors accusing you.
 <s n="3746"/>Therefore I beg you, when you do pay, that you will take care to leave me as you found me with the soldiers.
</section>

<section n="7.7.48" sentences="3">
 <s n="3747"/>After hearing this Seuthes cursed the man who was the cause why this pay had not been given long ago;
 <s n="3748"/>all suspected this to be Heracleides.
 <s n="3749"/>“I myself,” says he, “never had a notion to cheat you, and pay I will!”
</section>

<section n="7.7.49" sentences="2">
 <s n="3750"/>Then Xenophon said again, “Since you mean to pay, now I beg you to pay through me!
 <s n="3751"/>Don’t allow me to stand differently in the army now from the time when we came to you.”
</section>

<section n="7.7.50" sentences="2">
 <s n="3752"/>He said, “No! you shall not be less in honour with the army for my sake;
 <s n="3753"/>and if you will stay with me, bringing only a thousand men-at-arms, I will give you those forts and all else that I promised!”
</section>

<section n="7.7.51" sentences="4">
 <s n="3754"/>Xenophon said, “To do that is quite impossible; just let us go.”
 <s n="3755"/>“Why, look,” said Seuthes, “you will be safer here with me.”
 <s n="3756"/>Xenophon responded, “Many thanks for your goodwill, but for me, to stay is impossible.
 <s n="3757"/>Yet when I shall be in better honour, believe that this will be a blessing for you also.”
</section>

<section n="7.7.52" sentences="4">
 <s n="3758"/>Then Seuthes said, “Money I have only a little here, and that I give you, a talent;
 <s n="3759"/>but oxen six hundred, and sheep about four thousand, and captives a hundred and fifty or so.
 <s n="3760"/>Take these, and take those who cheated you as hostages, and then you may go.”
 <s n="3761"/>Xenophon laughed and said, “Well, if this does not come up to the pay, whose talent shall I say this is?”
</section>

<section n="7.7.53" sentences="5">
 <s n="3762"/>“There’s danger for me, and I had better look out for stones; you heard the threats.”
 <s n="3763"/>For the time he stayed there.
 <s n="3764"/>Next day Seuthes gave over what he had promised, and sent drovers with them.
 <s n="3765"/>The soldiers had been saying that Xenophon intended to make a home with Seuthes and to get what he had been promised;
 <s n="3766"/>but as soon as they saw him, they ran up to him with joy.
</section>

<section n="7.7.54" sentences="4">
 <s n="3767"/>Xenophon saw Charminos and Polynicos, and said to them, “Because of you, all this has been kept safe for the army, and here I give it to you;
 <s n="3768"/>pray arrange to distribute it to the men.”
 <s n="3769"/>So they took it over, and appointed spoil-sellers and sold it,
 <s n="3770"/>and the men were very much discontented.
</section>

<section n="7.7.55" sentences="3">
 <s n="3771"/>Next day Seuthes gave over what he had promised, and sent drovers with them.
 <s n="3772"/>The soldiers had been saying that Xenophon intended to make a home with Seuthes and to get what he had been promised;
 <s n="3773"/>but as soon as they saw him, they ran up to him with joy.

<section n="7.7.56" sentences="4">

 <s n="3775"/>pray arrange to distribute it to the men.”
 <s n="3776"/>So they took it over, and appointed spoil-sellers and sold it,
 <s n="3777"/>and the men were very much discontented.
</section>

<section n="7.7.57" sentences="3">
 <s n="3778"/>Xenophon took no part; he was clearly preparing for his homeward journey,
 <s n="3779"/>for this was before the sentence of his banishment had been given in Athens.
 <s n="3780"/>But the authorities in the army came and begged him not to leave until he should lead them to Thibron and hand over the army to him.
</section>

<section n="7.8.1" sentences="2">
 <s n="3781"/>After that, they sailed across to Lampsacos, and there Xenophon was met by Eucleides, a Phleiasian seer, the son of Cleagoras, who painted the frescoes in the Lyceion.
 <s n="3782"/>This man congratulated Xenophon on being safe and sound and asked how much gold he had.
</section>

<section n="7.8.2" sentences="1">
 <s n="3783"/>Xenophon said on his oath that there would not be enough to pay his way home unless he could sell his horse and what he had about him.
</section>

<section n="7.8.3" sentences="5">
 <s n="3784"/>The man would not believe.
 <s n="3785"/>The Lampsacenes sent him gifts of friendship, and while he was sacrificing to Apollo, he asked Eucleides to be there;
 <s n="3786"/>then Eucleides at the sacrifice said he believed now that Xenophon had no money.
 <s n="3787"/>“But I’m sure,” says he, “that even if you are going to get some, an obstacle comes in the way; if no other, you are your own obstacle!”
 <s n="3788"/>Xenophon agreed.
</section>

<section n="7.8.4" sentences="4">
 <s n="3789"/>The man said, “Yes, the obstacle is Zeus Merciful;”
 <s n="3790"/>and he asked, “Have you sacrificed already, as I used to do at home for you, with whole burnt-offering?”
 <s n="3791"/>Xenophon replied that he had never sacrificed to that god since his travels began.
 <s n="3792"/>So the man advised him to do as he used to do; he said things would be better that way.
</section>

<section n="7.8.5" sentences="1">
 <s n="3793"/>Next day Xenophon went to Ophrynion and sacrificed a whole burnt-offering of pigs in the family fashion, and the omens were good.
</section>

<section n="7.8.6" sentences="1">
 <s n="3794"/>On this day arrived Bion and Nausicleides with pay for the army; they were entertained by Xenophon, and they brought back his horse, which he had sold in Lampsacos for fifty darics.
</section>

<section n="7.8.7" sentences="1">
 <s n="3795"/>After that, they marched through the Troad, and crossing Ida, they reached Antandros, then sailed by sea to the plain of Thebe.
</section>

<section n="7.8.8" sentences="2">
 <s n="3796"/>From that place, they made their way through Adramyttion and Certonos to the plain of the Caicos and so reached Pergamos in Mysia.
 <s n="3797"/>Then Xenophon was entertained by Hellas, the wife of Gongylos the Eretrian.
</section>

<section n="7.8.9" sentences="4">
 <s n="3798"/>She informed him that there was a Persian, Asidates, in that plain;
 <s n="3799"/>she said he could capture him with wife and children and cattle, numbers of cattle, if he attacked at night with three hundred men.
 <s n="3800"/><note n="not translate"/>
 <s n="3801"/>She sent to show them the way her own cousin and Daphnagoras, one whom she valued very highly.
</section>

<section n="7.8.10" sentences="2">
 <s n="3802"/>So Xenophon, keeping these two with him, sacrificed.
 <s n="3803"/>Basias the Elean seer was present and said the omens were most favourable and that the man could be taken.
</section>

<section n="7.8.11" sentences="3">
 <s n="3804"/>After supper, then, he set out, taking with him the officers who had been most friendly and faithful to him all through, in hopes to do them a good turn.
 <s n="3805"/>Others came thrusting themselves in to join, 
 <s n="3806"/>but the officers refused, “We don’t want them to have a share,” they said, as if the spoil was in their hands.</section>

<section n="7.8.12" sentences="1">
 <s n="3807"/>They arrived about midnight, but they neglected captives and cattle around the tower, and most of them escaped, for they wanted to capture the man himself and his belongings.
</section>

<section n="7.8.13" sentences="1">
 <s n="3808"/>They attacked the tower but failed to storm it, for it was very tall and large, had bulwarks, and strong men to defend it. So they began to dig through the wall.
</section>

<section n="7.8.14" sentences="4">
 <s n="3809"/>It was eight clay bricks in thickness,
 <s n="3810"/> but by daybreak the light showed through;
 <s n="3811"/>at the first glimpse of light, someone struck through with an iron rod large enough to spit a whole ox, and ran through the thigh of the nearest man.
 <s n="3812"/>After that, they shot arrows through the hole so that it was not safe to come anywhere near the place.
</section>

<section n="7.8.15" sentences="1">
 <s n="3813"/>Then with shouting and beacon-fires, they brought help—Itamenes and his force, and from Comania, Assyrian men-at-arms and Hyrcanian horsemen, about eighty, who were also in the king’s pay, and others, light-armed troops, about eight hundred, others from Parthenion, others from Apollonia and from the neighbouring villages, and horsemen also.
</section>

<section n="7.8.16" sentences="3">
 <s n="3814"/>Then it was high time to look for a way of retreat.
 <s n="3815"/>They took all the cattle and sheep and captives and made a square with these in the middle; they were not anxious about the booty so much as fearful that the retreat might become a scampering if they left the booty behind.
 <s n="3816"/>If the enemy should be bold and the soldiers discouraged—but now they retreated ready to fight for their winnings.
</section>

<section n="7.8.17" sentences="2">
 <s n="3817"/>When Gongylos saw how few the Hellenes were, and how many the enemy, he sallied out himself against his mother’s wish with his own force, eager to take a part.
 <s n="3818"/>Other supports were Procles from Halisarna and Teuthranias, who was descended from Damaratos.
</section>

<section n="7.8.18" sentences="1">
 <s n="3819"/>Xenophon’s party was very hard-pressed by this time with arrows and slingstones. They marched in a ring to keep their shields in front of the arrows; it was all they could do to cross the River Carcasos, and almost half of them were wounded.
</section>

<section n="7.8.19" sentences="2">
 <s n="3820"/>There Agasias, the Stymphalian lieutenant, was wounded; he had been fighting the whole time.
 <s n="3821"/>They came in safe at last with about two hundred captives and sheep enough for sacrifices.
</section>

<section n="7.8.20" sentences="1">
 <s n="3822"/>Next day, Xenophon sacrificed and led out the whole army by night. He intended to take the most roundabout way through Lydia, to put the people off their guard; he hoped they might not be alarmed by thinking he was near.
</section>

<section n="7.8.21" sentences="1">
 <s n="3823"/>Asidates heard that Xenophon had sacrificed for another attack on himself and was out with all the army. He quartered himself in some villages under the town of Parthenion.
</section>

<section n="7.8.22" sentences="2">
 <s n="3824"/>There, Xenophon and his force came upon him, and captured him and his wife and children and the horses and all he had;
 <s n="3825"/>so the first omens came true.
</section>

<section n="7.8.23" sentences="4">
 <s n="3826"/>Then they returned to Pergamos.
 <s n="3827"/>There Xenophon gave hearty thanks to God;
 <s n="3828"/>for all joined together, the Laconians, the other captains, the under-officers, and the soldiers, and gave him the choice of horses and oxen and everything.
 <s n="3829"/>So he was now well able to do a good turn to another.
</section>

<section n="7.8.24" sentences="1">
 <s n="3830"/>Then Thibron arrived and took over the army, which he mingled with the rest of the Hellenic forces, and marched to the war against Tissaphernes and Pharnabazos.
</section>

<section n="7.8.25" sentences="4">
 <s n="3831"/>These were the governors of the king’s provinces as far as we went:
 <s n="3832"/>Artemas in Lydia, Artacamas in Phrygia, Mithradates in Lycaonia and Cappadocia, Syennesis in Cilicia, Dernes in Phoenicia and Arabia, Belesys in Syria and Assyria, Roparas in Babylon, Arbaces in Media, and Tiribazus in Phasians and Hesperitians.
 <s n="3833"/>The Carduchians, Chalybes, Chaldaeans, Macronians, Colchians, Mossynoecians, Coeti, and Tibareni were independent.
 <s n="3834"/>In Paphlagonia, Corylas; in Bithynia, Pharnabazos; and in Thrace, on the European side, Seuthes.
</section>

<section n="7.8.26" sentences="2">
 <s n="3835"/>The distance of the whole journey, up country and down country, was 215 stages, 1,150 leagues, 34,255 stades, or about 3,360 geographic miles.
 <s n="3836"/>The time taken was one year and three months.
</section>
